THE RED PAGE.
Maeterlinck’s New Play.
have always been men who have seen
I in the Universe a temple of mystery where
‘ ® soul, like a flame, consumes away in
adoration, consoling itself with beauty and
worship. Mystics, symbolists—call them what
you will—men who know that our life is of “such
stuff as dreams are made on ’’—they mediate
between religion and art. The Flemish poet,
Maeterlinck is such a one, a notable figure stand-
ing aside from the lewd and commercial literature
of modern Europe. Novalis and Emerson and
Walter Pater seem by turns to be revived in him ;
St. i rancis of Assisi might have rhapsodied with
him over his garden of bees or sung with him a
hymn to the sun. On the other side, Gabriel
D’Annunzio, though without the ethical chastity
of Maeterlinck’s spirit, is own-brother to him in
reverence for the delight of life and the tragedy
of passion. The Belgian poet rather resembles,
perhaps, some Emerson become more artistic,and
more sensuous. He writes in French with lucid
beauty of style, but the Germanic seriousness
continually breaks through the lilt and lightness
of his romance form and claims him for a
Northerner. '
How clear these two contrasted notes come out
in his last writings, in the “ Tresor des Humbles,”
1896, for example—a terrible and beautiful book,
in which his treatment of Words, to seek an
analogy in painting, strives after that indefinable
spiritual atmosphere, in which is now recognised
the charm and power of Millet ! But in the back-
ground of all Maeterlinck s pictures the everlasting
Germanic vision of Death looms, with its dread
and its solace. It lends its sinister charm even to
the “ Drarnes pour Marionettes.” Everybody has
been reading his last book about the life of bees
(“La Yie des Abeilles”) and fallen to thinking
whether it might not be equally good and profit-
able to read also his writings about the lives of
men.
The plays Maeterlinck has written have been
found almost impossible for the stage. They were
intended to be so, in as far as they neglect the
traditions, parade and superficiality of the
modern stage. But now there is a growing
curiosity to understand and to approach them in
a more sympathetic spirit towards the artist.
Maeterlinck himself has been at pains to explain
the dramatic innovation he has attempted in an
essay on “The Modern Drama.” There exists,
he says, in all great works, by the side of the outer
story, a second plot, borne on an undercurrent of
dialogue. This second plot seems always to be
subordinate and never forces itself upon the
attention of those who have no eyes for it. The
object and intention of his own plays is to bring
this hidden dramatic action into the foreground.
It is the soul of life and of the drama which is his
theme. Thus he has hitherto scorned the rabble
conditions and garish show of the modern theatre,
and his pieces have mostly failed for lack of what
people call a story.
All this, however, seems to be altered in his last
play, “ Monna Vanna.” It has scored a great suc-
cess at the Nouveau Theatre. His young wife (who
was the beautiful actress, Georgette Leblanc),
played the title r6le. The play is inspired by Italy,
and the “ story ” in it is both interesting and pro-
minent. When the curtain rises it reveals the
town of Pisa (it is in the fifteenth century) be-
sieged by a Florentine army under the Condot-
tiere Prinzivalle. The garrison is in extremities,
the ammunition and food exhausted. Guido
Colonna, the Governor of the town, has sent his
grey-headed father, Marco, to the hostile camp, to
sue for terms, and the return of the old man is
eagerly awaited. ■ At last he comes, but dejected
and unnerved, unwilling to disclose the secret of
his negotiations. Finally, he reveals the Condot*
tiere’s demand. Prinzivalle, who has news of
plots against him in Florence, will consent to help
Pisa with food and ammunition under one condi-
tion—Vanna, the young wife of Colonna, shall
come to him that night in his tent. The husband,
furious, helpless, and hesitating, lays before the
signora Prinzivalle’s terms, on which the lives of
the citizens depend. He determines to leave the
decision in his wife’s hands. Monna Yanna
(played by the lovely and newly-wedded Georgette
Maeterlinck) consents to the sacrifice. “I will
go to-night to his tent,” she says.
The second scene shows the dim lamp-lit tent of
the expectant Condottiere. He is at the parting
ways of his fate, for to-day, as he knows, he has
beeii denounced at Florence and superseded in his
command. But what matter ? He has the consola-
tion of his love. The lady comes, alone as
he has .stipulated. In words of quiet rapture, he
tells her of his life-long love, reminds her of their
childhood together in the palace-garden of her
parents. She has forgotten. She had grown up
.and been wooed by the Lord Colonna, and he, in
those days of his humility, had not dared to
aspire to her hand. When she was married he
had ridden away to distant lands to be Fortune’s
knight. This is bis story. Monna Vanna listens
and confesses to have dreamed of such a love in
her girlhood—a dream unrealised in her, life.
They speak together about the beauty and
the felicity of true love, and she tells him
that from the very first sight of him that night, she
had known that the shameful contract of her
coming was a pretence. A page interrupts them,
with the news of the Florentine troops who are to
arrest Prinzivalle.'. He determines to become‘a
wanderer once, more, but Vanna offers him shelter
and protection in Pisa. As they set out through
the darkness,, the joy-bells, are already heard
ringing from the city and bonfires are blazing, for
the arrival of the promised relief supplied—Pisa is
saved. “ The bells are ringing as on my wedding-
day,” she says to him as she leads him into Pisa.
In the third scene, Colonna awaits the return of
the wife he has sacrificed and yet so passionately
loves. At the sight of Prinzivalle and his wife’s
triumphant face, he sees in her a new Judith.
Breathless, she tells him the ' story of
Prinzivalle’s love for her and the safety
of her honor. Colonna believes not a
word of her tale. Mad with jealousy and
hate, he commands his men to seize and
bind Prinzivalle, and put him to the torture.
In a flash, the character of her own love and that
of her husband is revealed. She knows, too, the
cause of the happiness she lias felt that night.
Her resolution is soon taken.. She confesses that
her story was a lie, and that she has enticed her
ravisher to Pisa but to revenge herself upon him.
She had lied, she maintains, to conceal her shame
an 1 that of her husband. Hers alone shall be the
revenge. She alone will keep the key to this
prisoner’s dungeon.
So ends the play. Effective, and full of pas-
sionate human interest as it is, it distinctly de-
parts from the older symbolical manner of the
author,, but the symbolism runs only the more
deeply beneith the story. It marks a turning
point in Maeterlinck’s artistic career.
BERLIN.
F.S.D.
Aboriginal Food and Frivolity.
IN tlie third and last of his etymological bulletins
in connection with the Australian aboriginal,
Dr. Roth, Northern Protector of Aborigines
in Queensland, tackles the huge subject of
“Pood —Its Search, Capture and Preparation”;
and out of the mass of material at his hand he has
written a most readable monograph. The Australian
black revels in horrific combinations of food-stuffs,
of which he partakes in enormous quantities with-
out any subsequent suffering. Moreover, he has
no specified time for eating. If there is anything
over from the night before (which is unlikely) it is
promptly consumed by the just-awakened carnp;
if not, they just wait patiently until something
edible turns up as the result of the day’s search.
The principal meal is the gorge just after sun-
down, when the hunters have returned with their
spoils, unless, as sometimes happens, the said
hunters have taken an unfair advantage and have
eaten where they killed. Vegetables, of which
Dr. Roth has listed some 240 as lit for food, are
prepared for cooking by washing, grinding,
pounding or grating; while meat, fresh for pre-
ference, is cut into convenient pieces with flint-
flakes or a lawyer-cane saw. It is then either
broiled on hot ashes, baked in an oven of hot
stones, boiled in a bark trough, or grilled on a
grid of sticks. If there is nothing more attractive
obtainable, clay from ant-hills is used to fill
the aching void, while kaolin, judging by the
careful preparation it undergoes, is looked upon
as a sort of bonne Louche. The blacks eat Zamia
fruit, which is dried and pounded into a kind of
flour, and an intricate set of preliminaries attends
the conversion into food of the mangrove radicles
—the cobbler’s pegs of our youthful days. The
larvae of certain ants’ eggs (your black is an
epicure as to the species) as well as the ants them-
selves are eaten raw, after a process of kneading
the ants and eggs into one lump, while a keen
appetite is always evinced for some of the Ooleop-
tera, the big fat white lice, grubs and caterpillars.
A fish diet is procured by treading, and muddying
the water or stupefying the fish with some vege-
table poison. The native angles for eels with
a particular sort of sucker-eel impaled on a lawyer-
cane or, in the case of small fry, he limes them
with an adhesive bait. Fish-hooks of bone
are fast being civilised out of existence, but the
“sucker fish” is still used, as are also various forms
of nets and traps from the hollow log to the more
complicated woven ones capable of holding a turtle
or a dugong. Dr. Roth explains all these various
methods at length, with many notes on peculiari-
ties in their use.
. In big game hunting a deal of trouble is taken.
Emus are either snared at their usual watering
places or driven into traps or pits, while all over
the North the spear is used after stalking the
quarry behind bushes. Smaller birds are speared,
meshed, or struck with sticks, and the aquatic
varieties the blacks swim or dive for with an
infinity of precaution. Kangaroos are hunted in
many ways. They may be tracked and caught
while resting, run down with dogs, netted, driven,
or speared, and the use of circling fires often
assists the men in gathering the marsupials into a
confined space. Dr. Roth found indubitable evi-
dences of cannibalism in the North of Queensland,
though the practice is becoming more and more
nearly obsolete. Killing to eat is not the rule,
but use is made of the fallen in battle,
or of those who have died from natural causes.
Sometimes cannibalism was indulged in for pure
devilment, and there have been occasions where
women have been eaten for no Other cause than
to provoke a quarrel with the unfortunate victim’s
relatives. One instance was recorded of an inter-
tribal fight where both sides “took time,” occu-
pied a pleasant interlude in amicably devouring
the corpses of those who had already succumbed,
and then resumed the contest with renewed vigor.
No special ceremony or cooking is practised in
cannibalism. Men alone indulge in it, and that
it is not the recognised thing it was once with the
Maoris, is shown by the fact that they always dis-
cuss the matter in whispers, as if there was some-
thing uncanny about it.
After the food there comes the frivolity,
provided that too much has not been eaten,
and Dr. Roth writes a special bulletin on
“Games, Sports and Amusements of the
Aboriginals.” The list, when one comes to look
into it, is quite a formidable one, ranging from
dolls to tournaments, and from hide and seek to
a social and dance. Dr. Roth classifies his sub-
heads under such high-sounding titles as : Imagi-
native Games, Realistic Games, Imitative Games,
Discriminative, Disputative, Propulsive, Exulta-
tive, and Introduced Games. Imaginative Games
are plain yarn-spinning, and each tribe has a stock
of fairy tales, told purely for amusement. In
some cases, men look on this sort of thing as
effeminate, but, in others they take especial pride
in the story-telling, and the narrator is frequently
interrupted if he dares to meddle with the corrobo-
rative detail, and embellish on his own account.
Under Realistic Games Dr. Roth classifies the keep-
ing of animals as pets. As a general tiling, the un-
fortunate captives have a very bad time of it, for
the blacks never think of feeding them, and unless
they are free to hunt on their own account the
young wallabies, kangaroo rats, snakes, etc., which
are caught, soon die. Dogs, of course, are an ex-
ception, and true domestication arid training only
takes place; with the dingo. The aboriginals
appear to be very fond of their dogs, share iheir
food with them, speak to them as intelligent
beings, and do not believein “ sooling” them on to
each other. Different forms of hide-and-seek are
popular with young and old, the latter being
usually satisfied with testing, their keenness of
vision by searching for some minute object thrown
in sand by one of the players. Wrestling con-
tests (Disputative Games) are . fairly common in
the Princess Charlotte Bay district, the contes-
tants endeavoring to throw one another off their
feet by lifting them round the loins—-time and
time about. Propulsive Games are another name
for ball, top and other games; “catchers,” bowls,
rounders and top-spinning are all represented
among the aboriginals, and the boomerang, in its
more primitive form the cross-stick, is universally
brought into requisition to while away an idle
hour.
• The most interesting part of the bulletin is,
however, that which deals with Imaginative and
with Exultative games. As might be expected,
the former are almost.solely the province of the
youngsters, and *the piccaninny is just as fond of
pretending to be a grown-up a'nd to do grown-up
things as any child of our own. They will play at
houses, or with dolls go to war or to hunting and
climb trees in the footsteps of their elders, and, as
is the case all over the world, there are certain
formula to be observed in their games, which are
as the laws of the Medes and Persians. Women
as well as children are exceptionally expert at
“ cat’s-cradle, ” and out of a length of twine they
will, singly or in couples, evolve a truly astonishing
number of designs, all having their own signifi-
cance and representing some object, natural or
artificial. More than, passing notice should
be taken of the regular “ prun ” or tournament
in the Cardwell and Tully River scrubs. This
institution is “essentially an entertainment
(though the opportunities may be taken of wiping
off old scores, and so settling disputes either real
or imaginary), and gives men a chance of showing
off their prowess and courage before the women.”
The “prun” is held at regular intervals all the
year round in a particular place, and all the
neighboring tribes take part in it. It com-
mences either by throwing a boomerang or
spear into the mob with whom a fight is
desired (“ the gauntlet’s down ”), or by a
slanging match, wherein all the most exasperating
insults are hurled about and the women take a
more important part. When the requisite pitch
of anger is reached by one or other of these
methods, the fun commences, and a regular
“willing go” is the result, lasting till sundown,
with a final bout next morning before dispers-
ing. The women are in great form all
through ; and as they, as well as the men, yell
at the top of their voices' during the fight, it
can be well imagined that the “prun” ground
is not a peaceful spot. Dr. Roth thinks that
the “ prun ’’ helps to settle old scores, and, at
the same time, promotas social intercourse and
amusement, and it is curious to note that Spencer
and Gillen, according to a published interview
with the latter, found a similar practice in vogue
among the Warramunga tribe of Tennant’s Creek
(Central Australia). There, at the conclusion of
a fire ceremony, all the men who had quarrelled
painted themselves with fireclay and then belted
each other with fire-pticke, after which they
were supposed to be good friends. The whole
affair was a carnival, and while it was in progress
the men chaffed each other incessantly and some-
times said the most insulting things to each other,
just as the Tully blacks do.
A word regarding the music of the blacks —their
songs, dancing, entertainments and musical instru-
ments. In the first named it is not surprising
that the coarser element predominates, and one
will find in a blacks’ camp as great a pride in the
composition of “ Limericks ” as ever exists in a
present day school. It is obscenity pure and
simple with the aboriginals, and the greater the
amount of filth the singer can voice the more he
is honored. That Dr. Roth, who is ordinarily
the most plain-spoken of mortals, finds it neces-
sary to use Latin in translating them is sufficient
proof of their beastliness. The same class of songs
is used, by women especially, in order to provoke
quarrels. The natives also sing dirges for the
departed, a different song being composed for
each occasion, and here may perhaps be seen
first faint glimmerings of the minstrel who sings
arma virumque. Another queer variant of
songs are the “waru-waru” (which means in
plain English, “acting the goat”), whiph are
sung at the prun on the deaths of certain persons
and in connection with newly married men. They
are excessively filthy in their nature and in the
details accompanying them (commencing with
chewing the leaves of the stinging-tree and' so
working up a frenzy). With the males dancing
consists in the main of a stamping move-
ment of the feet, and the females vary . this
movement in different ways. The dancing is
always individual. The corrobboree comprises.any
ordinary “social and concert” generally, but not
necessarily accompanied by a dance, and is an
entertainment given by.the tribe in general. The
corrobbor.ees are most often held at night, but the
women sometimes hold one on their own account
in the afternoon —“evening parties;” and “after-
noon teas,” in other words. The musical instru-
ments of the blacks are either wind or drums and
sounding sticks, implements not capable of -an
extended scale, but as the blacks’range of har-
mony is a small one this is no drawback. __
T.D.
Fighting Journaliasm.
THE death of the American Meat Trust con-
cludes one of the pluckiest and most effec-
tive journalistic campaigns on teeord.
Early in this year the Chicago meat-
packers formed a Trust to control the-buying and
selling prices of beef. Swift land Armour were
the leading spirits, and they raised capital and
developed an organisation to control the whole
of the buying in the West and the selling in
the East. They had the killing of the cattle
solely in their own hands, and by strenuous and
sinuous measures they got hold of 75 per cent, of
the sellers of the great cities ; New York particu-
larly was in the hands of the Trust, and the only
purpose of those hands was to squeeze. Not one
leading member, of the combine who was-, not
already a millionaire; but what of that ? They
wanted more, always more. And Having- contrtl
of the great staple -of life, reckoned that
the human kind of the States could be got within
their nippers. Retail butchers had to become
agents of the Trust, or put up their*shutters.
Everything was arranged with th.e trade, and
put to all people who might make them
selves troublesome before the first turn was given
to the screw; and that didn’t trouble anybody
much. The maize crop was to some extent a
failure in the United States last' year, and as
from 200,000,000 to 500,000,000 bushels are fed
to cattle every year, there was excuse for a rise.
But soon it became plain that the rise wasn’t
even. If the grower got one dollar a hundred
more, the retailer was charged two; and shortly
dollar after dollar was put on to the consumer
through the retailer, while the grower didn’t get
cents. The result was that meat buyers were
being squeezed to the tune of millions a month,
and when they turned to other articles of diet
the Trust arranged to follow them with a screw of
another sort.
For the People the New York Herald resolved
to fight. The campaign was begun with a special
edition of the paper and continued day by day
with facts gathered up from the length and breadth
of the continent ; with smashing leading articles,
with cartoons significant as the old black bull
head served up to a guest at a Highland dinner.
The Herald had the Trust as a body on the dis-
secting table, and bared the springs of all its
machinations in the face of the world. The
Herald appealed to all associations of workers, and
political or municipal organisations. The Herald
forced the Central Federated Labor Union with
its membership of 250,000 to rise and protest.
The Herald compelled Tammany'into a belated
championship of the People’s right, and when
the mind of the whole People was thoroughly
stirred, and its demand unmistakeably voiced,
declared plainly “ this matter is now up to Con-
gress, and let Congress see that the administra-
tion acts.”
The infamous wrong of the whole business had
been made so plain, that to have delayed action
would have been to court .overthrow, and then the
State Governor poked up the State Attorney-
General, and the President, the United States
Attorney-General, and the result, as the cable
curtly states, was an injunction granted by the
Supreme Court of the United States at Chicago
(the Court of last resort), which bursts up the beef
combination. The New York Herald has killed
that combination ; fighting journalism has killed
it. But it did not die without a struggle. A good
many of the journals who kept silent throughout
the fight are not the poorer therefor, and every
charge which the Herald made was answered by a
shower of pamphlets, crammed with lies, audacious
and convincing as money could buy. And yet
the fighting paper and the good cause won, even
in the United States ! The law was, and is, per-
fectly plain in the matter, though this doubtless is
news to many of our latter-day Cobdenites who
are for ever prating about the tyrannous usage the
people of these States will yet get from Protec-
tion-cradled Trusts. It has been laid down by the
Supreme Court in Washington that—•
The people have a right to the necessaries and con-
veniences of life at a price determined by the relations of
supply and demand, and the law forbids any combination
whereby the price is removed beyond the satisfactory
influence of legitimate competition.
By the statutes of the United States an offence
against that law is punished by fine or imprison-
ment. By the law of the State of New York,
Swift or Armour, if convicted within the State,
could be summarily sent to gaol. Is it the same
in England, or does Freetrade, amongst other
things, render Trusts innocuous there ? If it Were
so in N.S. W., how would that Homebush combine
amongst squatters, butchers, auctioneers and the
Sydney Meat Preserving Co. stand ? It is only a
paltry affair, but so far as the interests of the
Sydney consumer are concerned, it certainly does
remove the commodity of meat beyond the sphere
of legitimate competition.
Trusts, when they come to be hundred million
concerns, are awkward things to tackle, even when
the statutes are plain. Laws are apt to be silent
under their influences, and the fighting journal is
about the. only power which will set the law
effectively in motion. Unhappily there is no such
thing as a fighting journal among the Australian
daily papers.

Low, Languid, and Dispirited.
The Vagaries of a Torpid Liver.
A Useful Lesson for Sufferers.
The Case of Mir. J. DEGNAN.
(FOR THE “ REDFERN TIMES.”)
Among the most important missions of
inquiry placed on record was that recently
made by our reporter to the home of Mr.
John Degnan, Garden-street, North Botany.
This is due as much to the magnitude of Ins
discoveries as to the interest of the incident
he elicited on inquiring.
“ Will you favour me with a complete
outline of the vicissitudes you successfully
combated ? ” ,
“ Right willingly,” answered Mr. Deg-
nan, as he offered the pressman a seat. 3? ive
years went by from the time my illness
started till it was finally conquered, and if
I did not have a bad time it was a caution.
Bear in mind, it was all through the torpid
state of my liver, and, believe me, I got
into such a very wretched condition that I
did not care whether I lived or not. Before
I was really aware what disaster was be-
falling me I used to feel as though I had no
life in me, and was always low-spirited and
sleepy. There was only one thing that I
was tit for, and that was to lie down, but
when I did so 1 could not sleep, my mind
being actively engaged in going through a
multitude of unhappy thoughts which
seemed to be born of the melancholy condi-
tion I was in. What caused me considerable
uneasiness of mind was that my appetite
became poorer every day, and still more
anxiety was engendered by the knowledge
that what food I did eat was of no benefit
to me, my ability to digest any kind of
eatables having vanished. In this way it
transpired that my strength wasted away
till it was often compulsory for me to knock
off work, although my employment at that
time was not of a heavy description.”
“ How did you feel after your meals? ”
“Just as if I had swallowed so much
lead. Something like a heavy ball seemed
to form in my stomach, the pains arising
from which were simply horrible, and from
the stomach the heavy sensations travelled
up to my chest, thereby giving me extra-
ordinary difficulty in breathing. My head
ached to a degree which proved almost dis-
tracting, for I could not settle down to
read or do anything, and there was even a
swollen feeling in my eyes to annoy me.
Owing to a pricking sensation under my
shoulder-blades I used to think I had
caught a cold, but experience has taught
me that that was a deranged liver symptom,
while another one, although more torment-
ing in its persistency, was a nasty aching
pain across the loins, which I felt very dis-
tinctly whenf* moving about. The bitter
taste in my mouth was enough to turn any
man off' his food, and the dirty coating on
my tongue used to make me feel sick. At
times, when exhausted nature claimed a
few houis’ rest, I fell asleep, but there was
no comfort in doing so, as the ugly diearns
I had simply beggar description The flesh
gradually fell away from my frame till my
bones were quite visible, and my prospects
were anything- but rosy, especially as my
head had long since been troublesome.
W ithout any ostensible reason it used to
start beating violently against my ribs, the
sound it made being quite loud enough to
be heard several yards away. It seemed
more than likely that my heart was diseased,
particularly so as those fits of palpitation
were usually accompanied by feelings of
faintness. The worry of mind and pain of
body at last succeeded in wearing out my
patience, for it took but little to make me
irritable and excited, after which I sank
into the old gloomy groove of misery till
something else occurred to upset me. Ner-
vousness was not the least of my ailments,
as my nerves twitched involuntarily when-
ever anything happened to disturb me, and
in spite of the medicines I had taken, they
?till trembled at the least provocation.”
“ Where did you get your medicine from,
Mr. Degnan ?”
“ I refer to the medicines which my*
doctors prescribed for me, but which I
might as well have left untouched for all the
good they were. I was always paying
money away to doctors, and never got any
value back for it; but now if I should
have the ill-luck to fall into bad health
again I would go straight for Clements
Tonic, as I know it to be the best medicine
ever brought within the reach of suffering
humanity.”
“That being your opinion, why did you
not take some earlier than you did ? ”
“ Recause I had been suffering five years
before I was told about Clements Tonic,
and another reason was because I had de-
cided against taking any more medicine, as
I thought it was useless. But a relative
came upon the scene and told me how
Clements Tonic had cured his complaints,
which were like mine in nearly every detail;
so I promised to try it, and when I did so
it was the best day’s work I ever did, for
Clements Tonic piloted me to health. I
used to dread the very idea of taking any
food, but when I had been on Clements
Tonic a few days it was quite different, as I
was always the first to sit down to meals
and the last one to finish eating, and before
a week was over there was a great reduc-
tion in the severity of my pains in the
stomach. My food did not ferment and
turn to wind as in former years, but it
digested quite readily, and of course I got
the benefit of its The weary
feelings which had darkened my days were
wafted away by the invigorating qualities
of Clements Tonic, which likewise buckled
together my shattered nerves and made
them firm and true. The greatest surprise
I ever got in my life was when I discovered
what a splendid medicine Clements Tonic
was for promoting healthful sleep, and I
say ‘ healthful ’ advisedly, for there was no
languidness or depression on awaking, such
as is experienced after taking sleeping
draughts or other such means for producing
slumber. The anxious misgivings which I
had borne in silence when my heart was
given to palpitating were not long in being
dispelled, for Clements Tonic made that
organ beat with the regularity of a clock,
and I can make the same statement in
regard to my internal functions generally,
for I never suffered from constipation after
taking a few bottles of Clements Tonic,
which relieved my chest pains, and head-
aches very easily. It was pleasant to get
up and notice that my tongue was clean and
that the bitter taste had left my mouth,
but what was still more 1 o be appreciated
was the fact that every ache and pain had
deserted my body, which was regaining its
weight from day to day.”
“ Are you sure about the completeness of
your cure ? ”
“l am quite positive that Clements Tonic
made a new man of me by giving me better
health and more strength than I ever had
before. Not long ago I was helping to cut a
road through the mountains, about ten miles
from Nowra, and I found that I could do
the heaviest manual labour going without
being unduly fatigued, and, as Clements
Tonic gave me the health to do it, you can
publish my story in any way you want.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
1, John Df.gnan, of Garden-streer. North Botany,
in the State of New South Wales, Commonwealth
of Australia, do solemnly and sincerely declare
that I have carefully read the annexed document,
consisting of five folios, and consecutively numbered
from one to five, and lhat it contains and is a
true and faithful account of my illness and cure
by Clements Tonic; and also contains my full per-
mission to publish in any way my statements —
which I give voluntarily, without receiving any
payment; and I make this solemn declaration con-
scientiously believing the same to be true, and by
virtue of the provisions of the Oaths Act, 1900.
Subscribed and declared at Darlinghurst this twenty-
eighth day of February, one thousand nine hundred
and two, before me,
THOMAS MOORE. J.P.

Inherited Cough
“ Yery anxious about him —Father
and Brother died of Consumption ”
A cough that defies all efforts to suhdue it
gives rise to many an anxious hour. It need
not: the right thing will cure it, though all
the wrong things in the world will not.
Here is the illustration :
“ 13, Embankment Residences,
“ Cremorne Road, Chelsea,
“ London, England, Oct. 29th, 1900.
fl Dear Sirs, —I have much pleasure in
testifying to the excellent results obtained
from taking Scott’s Emulsion. My son,
Sidney Blackman, had a very trying and
stubborn cough, which would not respond
to treatment, and I was very anxious about
him, having lost both his father and brother
from that dread disease, consumption. My
son was recommended to take Scott’s Emul-
sion, and after taking it for a short time his
cough was decidedly better. The treatment
was continued with the result that my son’s
cough was completely cured, and his general
SIDNEY BLACKMAN.
(From a photograph.)
health was greatly improved, and I feel con-
vinced that Scott’s Emulsion has been the
means of building up his constitution. I
consider it a wonderful preparation, and so
much nicer and easier to take than the
ordinary cod-liver oil, which my boy never
would take plain, but experienced no
difficulty whatever with Scott’s Emulsion.
I feel that the benefits to be derived from
Scott’s Emulsion cannot be too widely
known, hence the reason of my sending
you this letter, trusting it may be the means
of benefiting many who may read it.—
Yours faithfully,
“ (Signed) M. 0. Loder.”
The story Mrs. Loder tells we have heard
o’er and o’er : how people wandering on the
b >rderland of consumption go on trying all
sorts of substitutes for and imitations of
the right thing and are no better, even
worse, because discouraged. Then suddenly
they are advised to try the right thing—
Scott’s Emulsion—and as suddenly they are
better, the cure has begun, the remedy they
need they have, and once again existence is
attractive, for the life within them seems to
stir again.
There must be many who will read this
paragraph who secretly have admitted to
themselves that they fear consumption.
They need not if they will attack the com-
ing trouble with Scott’s Emulsion. We
have hundreds of similar unsolicited testi-
monials in cases exactly similar to Master
Blackman’s.
And there is a solid reason why Scott’s
Emulsion makes short work with consump-
tive tendencies. The cod-liver oil it contains
in such palatable form enriches the blood
and stops the wasting, which is consumption.
Then the attacked tissue is strengthened
and healed by the hypophosphites of lime
and soda. These three elements, combined
as they are in our process, promptly and
definitely eradicate consumptive tendencies.
How long will you go on trying useless
things before you begin with the right thing,
which is—Scott’s Emulsion!
A man carrying a big fish is our trade
mark ; they must be on the wrapper of the
bottle if you want the right thing.
You can obtain a sample of Scott’s Emulsion
by sending fourpenceto Scott & Bowne, Ltd.,
483 Kent-street, Sydney, and mentioning this
paper. All chemists sell Scott’s Emulsion.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Fascination.
Grey Eyes, Grey Eyes, thou canst not know
Thy changeless, cruel charm !
Else, nun-like, veiled thou wouldst go
To spare the world a harm
Such as its fevered past hath known
From fateful eyes like thine —
The crash of monarchs overthrown,
Tall city, tower and shrine
Withered away, and sullen red
That stained the river tide
From eager hearts that nameless bled,
And unremembered died.
No one, Grey Eyes, will ever dare
Divine what meaning sleeps,
Like a crouched lion in his lair,
Within those shadowed deeps,
On thee, far rapt from prayer or boon,
An antique wonder lies,
Like a remote mysterious moon
Amid englamoured skies:
As to the moon the sea-tides yearn,
So in the hearts of men
To thee, full-flood, love’s tide shall turn,
Nor ever ebb again.
ZADIG
In a Fern Gully.
A grandeur there is that can awe,
A beauty serene, without passion;
A garden of roses is fair,
But roses are wedded to fashion.
We leave the old paths, and we roam
The deeps of the hills, fresh as morning,
That all the long ages have spent
Their unplundered wealth in adorning.
Here beauty unmeasured, unsung,
Time ripens, and man doth not sully;
A wild, wanton grace, that enchants,
Runs riot in this lonely gully.
This paradise made to refresh
A land that grows wan and sun-dreary—
This bower of a virgin who smiles
And hushes to sleep the world-weary.
Rich drapings of shimmering green,
Embroideries no art surpasses,
Of blossom, and berry, and leaf,
And ferns that outnumber the grasses.
The dead that lie here are not dead,
Soft mosses their starkness o’er-cover;
The sensuous air doth embrace,
And whisper, and sigh, like a lover.
We hear the low throb, and the moan
Of seas that beat back the world’s thunder,
Those seas we have crossed, and full much,
The years that have gone have trod under—
The loss hath seemed more than the gain,
And thorns have been gathered for roses ;
The wind through the gum-boughs may rave,
But not a sound awes when day closes.
The dying, the dead, shall we mourn ?
We front not the Dark, but the Dawning,
And kiss the fresh lips of new loves
That give life a richer adorning—
Time opens the gates that were shut,
And unrolls the scroll as he races
And Beauty more beautiful grows,
And flowers bloom in earth’s desert places.
The music of water and wind
Is symphony, passionate, tender ;
The birds in the musk bushes sing
Of love that to love would surrender,
And love steeps the odorous air—
The full love that Nature confesses —
The love of a mother who tends,
And spoils a spoilt child by caresses.
ANDREW SARD.
To Lydia.
As tho’ you heard me I will sing;
As tho’ you e’en were here ;
My thoughts shall float on Fancy’s wing
Till dull earth disappear;
And then perchance may sink to rest.
Where you yourself may be ;
Then, if they nestle in your breast,
You must remember me.
I’ll sing, but not of love; for that
Is far too deep a theme.
All song love well might cavill at,
Love, too sweet e’en for dream !
I’ll only tell you that my days
spent with shapes of you ;
All clothed in many diverse ways—
In robes of many a hue.
I’ll only tell you that my nights
Have you for their lode-star;
That life with all its flashing lights
Is centred where you are.
I’ll only tell you—no, I’ll pray
That love may aid me well,
And tell you that which, night or day,
Myself can never tell.

NANSEN’S RECORD BEATEN.
The' Baldwin-Zeigler North Pole expedition has re-

asBldeg. 33min.

175min.]
■!6deg. 175 min., it may be explained, is equal to
one pound, 36 shillings and 23 pence, or to eighteen
bushels past 12 in the morning, which is the same
as one mile, three quarts and two ounces of road
measurement. And the Sunday Times actually
doesn’t know that 86deg. is nearer to the Pole
than Sldeg.

SATURDAY, AUGUST 23, 1902.
The Victorian Railway Troubles.
Whatever may be the end of the disturb-
ance that has been raised in the Victorian
public service by the Irvine-Shiels Govern-
ment’s famous sweating scheme for the partial
restoration of the finances (for it isn’t certain
even now that the end has been reached), the
episode itself is discreditable all round. The
coarse, ignorant brutalities of Minister Bent,.
no doubt,, aggravated the case. A man with
more of the finer instincts that are supposed
to, differentiate humanity from the lower
aaiimals might have cut down the wages of
the railway servants with less trouble, but
Bent’s constant tendency to add insult to'
injury, his pot-house methods, and his fixed
idea that to be a “strong man” it is necessary
to have the manners of a chucker-out, were
bound to create friction. Minister Bent
is one of these unfortunately-constituted
members of the bear tribe who could hardly
raise the wages of the men—to say nothing of
lowering them—without causing irritation.
Butapart fromthe mistake involved hißents:
political existence—which mistake was in
itself about equivalent to appointing a prize-
fighter to be ambassador at Paris or St.
Petersburg—the whole business was a piti-
able example of slipshod administration,,
incapacity, lack of courage, lack of foresight,,
lack of everything, in fact, that goes to make
a statesman.
« * * *
The proposed reduction in the wages of
the Victorian railway employees, which led.
to all the trouble, was not based on the
assertion that they were overpaid. The only
reason assigned was that an inept Govern-
ment, or a series of inept Governments, had
wandered into a bog of difficulties, and wanted
to find the easiest way out. It had to find,
money somewhere, and if it was a
Government created wholly by the Victorian
civil service, then it would be a fair thing j
to lay a specially heavy tax on the civil ser-
vice to make good the failings of its special
Government. Or if the old age pension
expenditure and war expenditure, which are
partly the causes of the deficit, were solely
for the benefit of the civil service, then that
body should be specially taxed to pay them.
Or if the abolished inter-State duties, the
loss of which is also largely responsible
for the shortage, were taken principally off
the shoulders of the civil service, then
that body should make up the loss. As
none of these things are so, the retrench-
ment proposals are a cowardly device in the
way of class taxation —an attempt to put an
extra share of the load of the Government’s
incapacity on a section of the community
whose sole qualification for bearing it is its
comparative helplessness. There is no
earthly reason for charging the railway
engine-driver an extra 9d. or Is. in the £
income-tax, without exemptions , to make up
the shortages of a Government for whose
existence he is no more responsible than
anybody else, than for similarly charging
Frederick Sargood, or Austin Zeal, or
Simon Fraser, or Baronet Clarke —save
that the Unspeakable Bent can terrorise
the engine-driver by threats of the “sack,”
while he can’t very well sack Sargood.
The Unspeakable, in fact, prefers to hit
something smaller than himself.
* • • •
Treasurer Shiels, as one reason for his
wage-reduction proposals, averred that
there are 4000 superfluous persons in
the Victorian public service. If that is
so, it suggests either gross incapacity
or shameful corruption on the part, among
others, of one William Shiels, who was
lately joint-Premier for a year (1899-1900),
and didn’t reveal this fact or take steps to
remedy it, but rather, by new appointments,
made the evil more aggravated. Also, as
Shiels has even now come forward with no
scheme for the gradual abolition of the
alleged superfluous 4000, he seems as incap-
able as ever. In any case, the Victorian
public servants didn’t themselves appoint
the superfluous 4000, and to cut down their
wages to make up the price of other people’s
political corruption is absurd, to say nothing
of being dishonest. If the Victorian rail-
way employees are over-paid, that again
reflects on the courage or honesty of one
William Shiels, who hasn’t the courage to
tell them so even now, and who recently held
the joint Premiership for 12 months without
doing anything to prevent these railway ser-
vants fromlootingthe pubfic. But no one says
the Victorian public service isoverpaid. Even
Bent doesn’t assert that. He merely says
that the proposed reduction only amounts to
3s. a week taken off the wages of a first-class
engine-driver, and a trifle like that is no
justification for a strike or other disturb-
ance. By Bent’s own showing this is
exactly the same as if the income-tax, in the
case of the very best-paid engine-driver, was
increased by about 4s. 6d. in the £ (from 4d.
to 4s. 10s.)—the tax being only chargeable in
Victoria on that part of the income which
exceeds £2OO a year. Probably, if the
engine-driver’s wages were accurately ascer-
tainable at the moment, the increase
amounts to more than that. It is a pity
Bent and co. do not try an increase of even
Is. in the £ on the income-tax of the
Kyabram reformers, and see how that
trifle—which is 75 per cent, less than
the trifle now under consideration—
would be received. Retrenchment (when
it doesn’t take the form of discharging
superfluous employees, but of cutting down
the wages of indispensable workers) and taxa-
tion are exactly the same thing. One is giving
less to a man ; the other is taking more from
liim. One is no more honorable than another,
if they are both applied allround. But to re-
trench one class or to tax one class, not
because it is the one best able to stand it, or
the one of least value to the community, but
because it is the ■ only class that Bent can
yell at with impunity and threaten with the
loss of its means of livelihood if it resists, is
a cowardly sacrifice of principle to expedi-
ency. If Bent, who proposes to raise the
income-tax rate on the best-paid engine-
driver by 4s. 6d. in the £, had threatened
the Kyabram reformers with an extra
Is. in the £ they would have used far worse
language than the engine-drivers. And Bent
himself would probably have crawled in
apology and explanation.
* * •
The most visible immediate causes of the
present Victorian financial trouble are these
eight :
(1) The granting of old age pensions (which was a good
thihg) without making any arrangement for raising the
money (which was not a good thing). Quite a number of
the present retrenchment crowd gained much popularity
by advocating the payment of old age pensions, without
tackling the unpopular job of finding the money to pay
them with. The public service isn’t responsible for this,
nor are old age pension! for its special benefit.
(2) The S’African war bill, and the flat refusal of the
wealthy class which howled most loudly about its desire
to “ make sacrifices for the Empire” to sacrifice a copper
in payment of the bill.
(3) The overloading of the civil service through corrupt
politicians (who now cry bitterly for retrenchment) foist-
ing their incapable relatives and constituents on it
wnfen there was no real work for them to do. They did
this from the meanest motives of self-interest, and
Treasurer Shiels says that the practice has loaded the
service with 4000 superfluous employees. The wages or
these 4000 are in themselves said to be equal to the whole
deficit. The politicians who brought this about now
expect great credit.for partly making good the shortage
by cutting down the wages, among others, of capable
public servants who got into the service on their merits.
But thev don’t offer to find their hangers on employment
elsewhere, nor do they offer any guarantee that they
won’t push 4000 more incapable and superfluous friends
inti the service. , ~
(4) The railway shortage. The railways don t pay
because grasping landowners loaded them with an im-
mensity of needless debt by the wholesale way they
swindled the Government over the land resumptions.
Also because there is too much railway in proportion to
population, the land-owning class having log-rolled alt
manner of hopeless lines for land boom purposes. Like-
wise because the farming and pastoral class (the Kyapram
reform class) insists on having its produce carried below
cost at the expense of the general taxpayer.
(5) The abolition of a large number of taxes by tne re-
moval of all inter-State duties, and the refusal of a weak
and incapable Government to levy new taxation to nil
Tefthe shameful dishonesty of Kyabram “ re-
formers” and other grasping and selfish characters,
who repudiated the obligations they had solemnly
entered into when their irrigation works were con-
structed, and shifted the burden on to the general
revenue. ,
(7) The greed and mendicant habits of the Local
Government bodies (largely representative of the land-
owning class), which, in the country especially, refuse to
levy nearly enough rates to pay for their local works, and
are perpetually cadging on the Treasury for aids and
subsidies.
(8) The fact that the great bulk of Victoria pays
no land-tax, and its unpatriotic landlords would sooner
see the State insolvent than pay. Only estates which are
over 610 acres in extent and also over £2500 in value pay
tax in Viotoria, and then merely on the value over and
above £2500. All Melbourne is exempt, it not being divided
into 640-acre blocks.
• * * »
These are the most immediate reasons of
the Victorian deficit. Behind them is the
huge burden of public debt. Also the in-
capacity of the great Bent and Shiels tribe
of legislators, whose rule is to take no thought
for the morrow, and to pay off none of the
debt in good years, but rather to accumulate
new loans, so that there will be an intoler-
able burden of interest to pay in bad years.
Behind everything, there is the greed and
cowardice of the propertied class, whose
burden of taxation, local and national, is
about one-fourth that carried by the cor-
responding class in England, and whose
pitiable wails for still further reduction of
its burdens, keep the Treasury always on
the verge of insolvency, and always accumu-
lating fresh liabilities and fresh deficits.
There is no unavoidable misfortune about
the matter only corruption, greed,
cowardice, incapacity, hard lying, miserable
pandering to the propertied class and the
Kyabram looters, and Shiels and the Un-
speakable Bent on top of all.
« • ~ • *
Probably enough, even the retrench-
ment proceeding would have passed off
quietly if Bent’s methods had not been
so suggestive of the slum or the prize-
fight, and if the reductions had been accom-
panied by an increase of taxation. But
when almost the most lightly-taxed crowd of
landlords in the world didn’t offer to carry a
penny of the burden, and instead shunted
the cost of its private irrigation works on
to the State—when Shiels informed the
public servants that the wage of the working
section of them had to be cut down because
he and his kind had put 4000 of their super-
fluous friends and hangers-on and political
supporters into the service as useless para-
sites—and when Bent jeered at the superior
railway officials for objecting to pay 4s.
lOd. in the £ income-tax when the Kya-
bram patriots lament about paying 4d. in the
£ —and when they heard a country which
is more lightly taxed, even with its Old
Age Pensions, than the United Kingdom is
without them, complaining that its burdens
were so heavy that it could only pay its way
by sweating its employees —when the
service heard all these things its attitude
naturally became unfriendly. The yellings
of the bull-voiced Bent may bear down
opposition, but they won’t make the Irvine-
Shiels-Bent Government any the less a
pitiable combination of cowardice, shuffle,
and incapacity.
Capital Punishment.
The Bill to amend the law in regard to Capital
Punishment was, as usual, talked out in N.S.W,
Assembly last week. The Parliamentary system
rarely allows a private member to carry through a
Bill of any importance ; measures which are not
adopted by a Government as their own have but
small chance of getting successfully through the
many stages which must be passed ere a Bill be-
comes an Act, even though there is a fairly strong
public and legislative opinion in their favor. But
The Bulletin hopes to see very shortly a Bill for
the abolition of Capital Punishment brought for-
ward as a Cabinet measure in every Australian
State. The whole tendency of modern sociology
is trending steadily against the community paying
back a murder with a murder, against a system
which is as inherently illogical as that of
the Corsican vendetta. This, needless to say,
arises from no sympathy with assassins as a
class; no desire to make things easy for persons
committing wilful murder, but from a profound
conviction—a conviction unassailable in argument
—that capital punishment is useless as a deterrent,
barbarous as a reprisal, and utterly mischievous,
in that it tends to allow criminals to escape due
punishment, and to permit maniacs, who should
in the community-interest be shut up for ever, to
have freedom and opportunity for further out-
lages- .
There is also the argument from reason ; it is
as clear a priori as a posteriori. Hanging is
admittedly a barbarous act as an act. No j udge, no
sheriff, no “respectable person” will hang a man
himself. The actual work is left to some degraded
criminal, who can thus earn a concession from
prison discipline, or to a freeman who is willing
to become a pariah among his fellows, and whose
nature can be accurately judged by that very
willingness. The. hangman to any ordinarily
constituted person, is a loathsome being; very,
very few would con|ent to eat. with him, talk
with him, or allow him the rights .of human
intercourse. The community, in this attitude
towards the instrument of its decrees, admits the
barbarity of those decrees. If it is not a savage
and brutal thing to hang a man, why hasn t the
hangman the right of every other civil servant
the right of the sheriff and of the Judge, to go to
Government House and have the honest hand
which does such valuable work for the community
grasped by the Governor and fondled by the
society lady? The community knows it is a
savage thing to pay back the killer with a killing,
to throttle, as in some cases, the mad victirn of a
cruel destiny ; and, whilst it permits hanging, its
sentiment, more or less conscious, revolts against
hanging and refuses to help actively m a hanging.
Tfierein lies the grave danger to the community
in the system of Capital Punishment. A jury
with, say, a murderess, clearly and unmlstake-
ably a murderess, before it for judgment, hesitates
to say “ guilty ” ; the spectacle of the woman m
the hands of the hangman comes to its eyes.
Better a lie than that, and the woman goes out of
court, perhaps to murder another. Or, a cold-
blooded murder has been committed, and the
criminal, some dangerous lunatic with a mama for
killing, or some wretch whose ungovernable fits ot
fury are a peril to all with whom he has to asso-
ciate, is fleeing from justice. By right every
member of the community should be eager to
help the law to lay its hands on the criminal; and
it is usually sought to stimulate this eagerness by
the offer of a money reward. But in practice,
who gives really practical aid to the officers of the
law ? How many, on the other hand, do all that
they can to shield the offender ? In the nnnd of
every man who has the option of helping the law or
the criminal, there is the thought of tne choking,
the horror of the blood-money. To help to cage the
criminal, he is more than willing; to help to
hang him, no. The scaffold scene would haunt
his nights afterwards. So the police rarely have
murderers betrayed into their hands, except by
women, whose smaller sensibility permits them
often to do things at which men would revolt.
• • * •
The purpose of the law should be to deter the
criminal as far as possible, and to shut up for ever
those whose instincts make them a source of
danger to the community. It is of vital im-
portance that the law should be sure ; there is no
need for it to be savage. To shut up nine mur-
derers out of ten i 3 better than to hang five and
leave five with a chance to repeat their crimes.
The system of capital punishment tends, in the
first instance, to brutalise the community, and to
make life less sacred ; in the second instance, to
allow homicides sometimes to escape trial, some-
times to escape conviction. The plea for its
abolition is not put forward on behalf of assassins,
but on behalf of those who are liable to be
assassinated.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
M'Millan’s Testimony in Favor of
Protection.
William M‘Millan, the one-time Freetrade
Treasurer of New South Wales, who heavily pro-
tected newspapers, beer, wine, tobacco, cigars,
sugar, all kinds of woodwork, fruit, bacon, bis-
cuits, butter, candles, cement, cheese, confec-
tionery, hams, tongues, all kinds of galvanised
iron, jams, jellies, salt, and a number of other
things, is a man of many parts. The same brief
and rather excitable William is deputy-leader of
the Freetrade party in the House of Representa-
tives, where, despite his N.S.W. career as a
Treasurer who protected the State’s principal
industries and denounced Protection at the same
time, he poses as a man whose devotion to Free-
trade has always been solid and unquestionable.
And as he stands upon an envelope in order
to see over the top of twopence he is full of
pitying scorn for Protectionists who not only
preach but practise their creed, and wants to
know why they can’t see the simple truths of his
own faith —the truths which he so badly failed to
see himself. Also M'Millan represents here the
rich absentee firm of M‘Arthur and Co.—which
had to be Freetrade in the old days, because it
lived by bringing the products of cheap English
labor into Australia, and the advent of a decently-
paid Australian industry would naturally inter-
fere with its business. But times have changed.
Australia has a tariff which aims at fostering Aus-
tralian industries—not the outside industries of
rich absentees ; and, in practice if not in theory,
M'Millan moves with the times. This is an
extract from a circular issued by his firm early in
the present year:
In future it is our intention to manufacture locally all
lines that can be made up here to advantage, thus saving
the heavv import duties on apparel.
The Clothing Factory.—Owing to the very large in-
crease in this branch of our business we have been com-
pelled to add two additional storeys to our factory, and
throughout it is now equipped on entirely modern lines
and able to cope with all demands.
Our Shirt Factory has been established for some three
months now, and during that time has been engaged in
making up goods for the new season ; these shirts will
escape the 274 per cent, duty on imported lines. In this
factory we have already over 100 hands employed.
Straw Factory.—ln this branch we have also had to
duplicate the machinery to enable us to keep up output
with the demand, and are now turning out 500 dozens per
week ; clients may, therefore, thoroughly rely upon all
orders placed with us being duly executed in the future.
That is the painful spectacle of M'Millan ad-
mitting that Protection increases local industry
and supplies the Australian people with work and
good wages (it can’t be supposed that M'Millan
pays sweater’s wages). And after preaching for
years that the 274 per cent, is not only added to
the price of the goods, but that it adds 40 or 60
per cent, to the price of the goods, the above
shows M'Millan assuring the public that, by
buying his protected manufactures, they will
ESCAPE THE 274 per cent. duty. Then here is an
extract from another circular (dated 20th Feb.,
1902) also issued by M'Millan’s firm :
THREE GREAT LINES OF FLANNELS AT LESS
THAN FREETRADE PRICES.
28/29-inch. 60yd. lengths, at 9d. per yard.
30-inch full, 48yd. ~ lid. „
36-inch full, 48yd. ~ 13d. ~
In Assorted Colors as Samples enclosed.
Please COMPARE these with any imported or
locally purchased goods you may have obtained before
the Duties were imposed.
You will recognise these GOODS are of EXCEP-
TIONAL VALUE, and it will prove to your advan-
tage to fill requirements for the coming season
IMMEDIATELY.
Our supplies from the Mills are coming forward
freely, and we are prepared to execute your orders at
once', or if placed with us now can guarantee delivery
for either March Ist or April Ist, as you may desire.
That presents the solemn spectacle of M'Millan
assuring the public that nis goods are cheaper
under Protection than they were previously under
Freetrade. Finally this is from M'Millan s
newest circular (9th August) :
The demand for Locally Manufactured Goods has in-
creased to such an extent that we have also had to
extend and add further equipment to our Clothing.
Shirts and Straw Hat Factories ; we have also under way
a Factory for our Mantle and Underclothing Depart-
ments. . . , . . ~
These goods we can no longer import owing to the
Duties now levied on same, but we are in a position to
make all this class of goods locally, and compete success-
fully with any goods entering this Market.
Not a word, be it noted, about any increase of
prices owing to Protection, but a cordial assur-
ance that the new tariff supplies much-needed
employment to builders, carpenters,-bricklayers,
tailors, tailoresses, shirt - makers, hat-makers,
mantle-makers, seamstresses—all no doubt at good
wages, forif M'Millan’s firm paid sweating wages,
the fact would doubtless have been honestly men-
tioned in the circular. And by furnishing em-
ployment for these people in Sydney, the tariff,
on M'Millan’s plain evidence, helps.to build up
Sydney, and make it the great city it should be.
And at the same time these new industries, which
M'Millan has started by reason of Protection,


are not loading the country with debt for the
purpose of making roads and railways to “open
up ” the rabbit-infested backblocks; they don’t
ask huge loans for irrigation works, or clamour to
have fodder carried at one-tenth of what it costs
to carry it—as the alleged “ natural industries ”
which M ‘Millan bids the people rely on are re-
duced to doing. Instead they give employment
to people who help to pay the interest on the
great loans contracted to boost up the country’s
alleged “ natural industries.” Likewise they sup-
port M'Millan, and he is an “amusing cuss”
whose loss would be much felt, and who couldn’t
make axle-grease for himself if he “went on the
land ” and depended on “natural industries.” In
one way and another M'Millan is a very good
advertisement for Protection, and this paper
begins to regard him as a brother —a small
brother, that is. It may yet be able to regard
him as a full-grown brother—who knows ? The
first step in the direction of complete brotherhood
is a very heavy absentee-tax on M'Arthur and
Co., the absentee principals of the M‘Millan
concern, which may compel them to sell out the
above flourishing protected business to Australian
owners.
The Cornstalk Savings Bank.
Arthur Griffith, M.L.A., writes on the above
subject:—
As you are, of course, aware, the Saving- Bank of N.S. W.
is worked under an Act passed 40 years ago, when condi-
tions of life and settlement were very different from what
they are to-day, and I feel sure my fellow-trustees are
fully seized of the need of an amending Act to bring our
institution up to date. In my opinion, the chief defects
of our Act are :
1. The provision which prevents the officers of the bank
in any place outside of Sydney from receiving deposits
from the public and confining such duties to local
honorary trustees. One result of this restriction is that
in a great centre, such as Newcastle, with a large indus-
trial population, and between 3000 and 4000 depositors,
the time for the receipt of deposits is limited to three
hours per week, and these outside of ordinary business
hours. It has been found quite impossible to get an
honorary trustee to attend during business hours. We
have in this branch two highly-paid officers, who are
compulsorily idle during almost the whole of their time.
In the new Act I would propose the abolition of country
trustees, and the placing of the entire control of the local
officer in our Board, assisted, of course, by a competent
inspector.
2. The provision in our Act which permits money to be
lent only on land held in fee simple, and the immemorial
tradition that the bank may advance only on land on
which are erected brick or stone buildings, prevents the
bank from lending on the best security in the State—
good agricultural, horticultural, and pastoral land—and
from assisting the very class which would make the best
possible use of cheap money in small lots. A direct result
of this limitation on the operations of the bank has been
to force it to place vast sums (according to our last annual
report aggregating £1,168,000) on fixed deposit in the
Sydney banks at 3 and 3£ per cent., as compared with
the beggarly sum of less than £lO,OOO advanced by us to
country producers.
Personally I regard this as a shocking state of things, as
any financial crisis seriously affecting the Sydney banks
might land us in a loss of hundreds of thousands of
pounds without any tangible security to show for it. A
very large amount of our money was shut up in the re-
constructed banks during the last crisis, and some of it is
still irrecoverable. As a matter of fact, the amount
deposited by us forms an enormous percentage of the
fixed deposits of some of the Sydney banks. According
to the last published N.S.W. banking returns, the total
amount of money on fixed deposit with one Sydney bank
was £107,000 odd, of which we had deposited over £61,000
or some 57 per cent. Another holds £90,000 odd of fixed
deposits from the N.S.W. public, and £75,000 from us,
while seven other banks hold our deposits in nearly as
high a proportion. Now, if our depositors wished to have
their money placed in these privately-owned banks, they
could put it there direct without passing it over our
counters, and I feel sure the Savings Bank of N.S.W. was
never intended as an institution for the collection of
deposits for other banks.
Supposing we were to withdraw all this vast sum of
money from the Banks (except, say, £lOO,OOO to meet any
unexpected run or crisis) and invest it in mortgages on
good productive lands in the country we would not only
have tangible security for our money but would be able
to reduce the interest to our present as well as our future
clients from 5 and 6 to 4£ per cent, and still earn over
£lO,OOO per annum more interest than we received last
year.
By way of a healthy contrast with our present methods
I will call your attention to what the Victorian Savings
Bank is doing.
Under their Credit Foncier system, they have advanced
£1,200,000 to some 3000 farmers at 6 percent. (41 being
interest and 11 sinking-fund), on which terms principal
and interest will be paid off in 31 years.
. As a result of five years’ operation of this system, only
six of the 3000 farms have been foreclosed upon, and not
one penny of either principal or interest has been lost by
the bank, which has paid 0) per cent, interest and carried
over £20,000 to its accumulated profits-fund.
~ln view of the unmixed benefits of this system I am
going to ask our trustees to agree to the establishment of
this Credit Foncier system in the new Bill which I am
about to introduce. There are one or two other matters
which I think will be necessary to deal with before we
have our institution up-to-date. When we abandon the
system of stowing our money in the Sydney banks and
lend it to the customers of those banks (oil very much
better terms than they can) it will be necessary to avoid
the invidious position caused by having on our trust
directors of rival banks. I intend! therefore, to include
in my Bill Section 7 of the Victorian Act, which enacts
that “ no trustee shall be a director or member of the
managing body of any banking institution.”
I should also like to suggest, by way of enhancing
public confidence, that we should adopt the Victorian idea
of submitting our accounts annually to the Auditor-
General.
A furGher indication of our backwardness in meeting
requirements of the public as compared with our progres-
sive Victorian rival is the fact that whereas we have in
New South Wales only 13 branch savings banks, two in
the metropolitan area, and 11 in the country, the Vic-
torian Savings Bank has 48 properly-constituted branches,
one in each of the large Melbourne suburbs, and one in
each important country town. Further than this, eaoh
of the 48 branches of the Victorian Savings Bank is a
properly-constituted bank, where the public can pay in
and draw out money all day and every day, while our
branches, with one solitary exception, are mere agencies,
open to receive deposits only three hours per week.
A Missed Iniquity.
Many years ago Thomas M‘lt wraith devised a
fearful and wonderful scheme whereby Queens-
land was to induce certain British specu-
lators to build railways in its territory by giving
them a present of land to about twice the whole
value of the lines, and was then to buy the lines
from them for about times the price at which
it could have built them in the first place, so that
it would ha ve lost the land and yet have been a great
deal deeper in debt than it is now. The same
frantic project, or one very like it, was revived in
later years, but each time the scheme dropped
through. Now Queensland has fallen on evil
days, and certain Bat men and Bat influences
begin to remark in the papers that the trouble
all arises through not building those railways on
the lunatic-asylum terms proposed. Having
ruined the. State by his own short-sighted pro-
fligacy, it is so like the Bat person to point out
that the disaster is all owing to the misconduct of
the Democratic party in not allowing him to be
so profligate as he ought to have been. The
causes of Queensland’s disasters are obvious
enough to any intelligent person, and these are a
few of the largest of them :
The idea that the one essential was to get population
without making any provision to find work for it when it
came. Instead of trying to make Queensland a desirable
place to which immigrants would come naturally.
Queensland spent nearly £3,000,000 of loan money on
buying people. It bought no less than 112,714 assisted
white immigrants between 1881 and 1899, and so many of
them left the neglected and ill-governed country as soon
as they looked at it that Queensland only gained 111,970
immigrants in all that time, including niggers, Ohinamen
and persons who paid their own passages.
The building of far more railways than the population
could profitably use—more, proportionately to popula-
tion, than are possessed by any other country on earth,
save Westralia.
The discouragement of agriculture by selling or giving
away the best wheat land in big estates to sheep farmers.
Queensland spent immense sums in importing small
farmers, and had practically no small farms to offer them
when they arrived.
The refusal of the grand old inflated pioneers to pay
direct taxation, and the adding of deficits (about
£2,500,000 in all) and the cost of unproductive works to
the public debt till it is the biggest debt per inhabitant
on earth. The wholly unproductive debt is now close
on £3O per inhabitant, which is just about equal to the
total debt of the most embarrassed European country.
And the £46 per head of “productive" debt only pro-
duces enough to pay about half the interest, so half of it
may also be called unproductive debt. This makes a
total of £53 for every inhabitant, the interest in which
has to be paid out of taxation.
The discouragement of every kind of profitable industry
by the employment of a ridiculously large proportion of
the people on unproductive public works.
• childlike faith in the policy of encouraging the
influx of foreign capital, which has led to most of such
enterprises as Queensland does possess being owned
abroad, so that the profits go to foreign lands.
. T he wasting of immense sums on such silly political
jobs as the attempt to make artificial harbors at Rock-
hampton and Townsville, while good natural harbors
at Bowen and Gladstone, right alongside, were neglected.
. le frantic haste to make a whole nation at any cost
in 50 years, when there were 5000 years ahead that would
have done just as well.
There is no need to look for the cause of Queens-
land’s depression in the iniquities it has left un-
done. The iniquities it has done are sufficient to
account for anything. The drought comes into
the question only indirectly. Queensland bought
population with loan moneys, and rushed them
out into the dry West. It stocked the dry West
with sheep and cattle largely bought with money
borrowed from English and other outside institu-
tions. It made railways with borrowed money,
so that the people it had obtained with borrowed
money might get the produce that was required
to pay interest on borrowed money down to the
port that was made with borrowed money (if the
truck that was repaired with borrowed money
didn’t break down by the way), and put it on the
foreign-owrted ship. And the Western drought
struck this foolish business below the belt, and
knocked the foundation from under the house that
Jack borrowed. But the drought wasn’t the
original trouble, nor anything like it.

The Opinion of Alderman Boggs.
Within the last few weeks The Bulletin has
read at least a dozen statements to the effect that
this or that set of suburban aldermen has con-
sidered the Greater Sydney idea and disapproves
of it strongly. Also N.S. W. politicians in general
have considered the question of abolishing a large
number of themselves and disapprove of it
strongly. Burther, the last person who was sen-
tenced to be hanged lias considered the idea in all
its bearings and disapproves of it very strongly
indeed. All these things are of one piece and are
evidences of the same habit of mind. No person
of any comprehension supposed for a moment that
the typical alderman of the small halfpenny
suburb would approve of a scheme which begins
by setting forth that he is a failure, an incubus
and a back number, and that the system which
gives rise to him is a fraud ; and which ends by
proposing his abolition. At the same time no
person of any intelligence expected that he
would tell the true reason of his opposition
to the Greater Sydney idea. No man is likely to
acknowledge that he seeks to sacrifice the interests
of the community to his own mean little im-
portance, any more than he is likely to advertise
Damp House for Sale at much above its real
Value.” Therefore, as a matter of course, he
opposes the idea of making the N.S. W. metropolis
a decently-governed city, and struggles to preserve
the old chaos, stagnation and lack of enterprise
from (ostensibly) the noblest motives. And when
he does favor some kind of Greater Sydney he
proposes to preserve the existing 40 odd councils
and merely create a Bederal Municipal Council in
addition—not ostensibly to save himself from ex-
tinction and to avoid sinking from Alderman
Boggs to plain Boggs, but again from the noblest
motives. Why anyone should ask an alderman’s
opinion on the matter, or think it worth while
publishing it when it comes to hand unasked, is a
problem.

Th.e Illegitimates.
In Brisbane, according to recent statistics, out of
every 100 births 13 are illegitimate, but out of
every 100 children who die 24 are illegitimate.
The illegitimate child has the same chance to be
born healthy and to grow up healthy as other
children have, if it is given the same opportunities.
In fact it has a greater chance, because the ille-
gitnnate is, in a very large proportion of cases, the
child of a young mother, and her child has more
vitality as a rule than the child of a middle-aged
woman. In N.S.W. over 35 percent, of the births,
where the mother’s age is between 15 and 20, are
illegitimate, and so are 11| per cent, of the births
where the mother is aged between 20 and 25. The
only possible explanation of the vast excess of
deaths among illegitimate infants is plain murder,
or privation and neglect which may be intended
to end in death, or which, again, may not
be so intended but be merely the result
of poverty. The neglect, when it happens
at a baby-farm, is generally intended to be slow
murder—just slow enough to prevent the law call-
ing it such. The discovery which has shocked
Brisbane is not a new one ; it is old and almost
world-wide. In N.S.W. seven out of every 100
children are illegitimate, but out of every 100
children who die before reaching the age of 12
months 25 are illegitimate. This is even a worse
record than that of Brisbane. And though the
unlawful infant has a worse show, all the world
over, than the lawful one, he seems to have the
worst show of all in Australia. Here it may be
roughly calculated that the illegitimate child has
three chances of dying before the age of 12
months where the legitimate child has only
one ; in most European countries the odds
against the love-child, as compared with its
“ respectable ” brother or sister, are only
about 2to 1. But everywhere the odds are
enormously against the offspring of unmarried
parents, and no one can find a remedy. Plain,
obvious murder may be suppressed, but that
lack of intense maternal care and attention
which often turns the scale between life and death
can’t be. Even the largest and most conscientious
policeman can’t make a mother do her utmost to
keep her infant alive if she doesn’t want it to
remain alive ; and ten large policemen can’t make
7s. fid. a week a sure guarantee that the infant
will get everything it needs to give it a fair show
in this world, even supposing that there is no
lack of maternal attention. The fact that the
illegitimate is usually the child of young parents
tends, to start it out with a healthy frame and a good
constitution. It also tends to furnish it with a
father who is earning £1 a week as a beginner at
some ill-paid occupation, which is a counter-bal-
ance. Some illegitimates have the luck to have a
wealthy K.C. in large practioe for a father, but
they are few.
