A Sinking Fund.
A few years ago that reasonably honest man,
Premier Turner, of Victoria, started the first
beginnings of a sinking fund for the repayment of
the Vic. public debt. The proceeds of the sales
of the mallee lands were to constitute the fund,
and it has now got as far as £7s,ooo—which isn’t
much with which to repay £50,000,000 of debt,
though it is something to go on with. And already
the dismal and incapable Shiels proposes to seize
part of the money for current needs, to make his
deficit look less, and to help to avoid facing the
difficulty, for Shiels, in all his political dealings,
is a cocktail at heart and never faces anything.
The rest of the sinking fund will doubtless go in
like fashion very soon, and then the mallee lands
money will begin to go into revenue, and that will
be the last of Turner’s reform. Turner also re-
solved that when there was a surplus at the end of
any financial year, it was not to be carried
forward to ease next year’s taxation, but applied
in reduction of the accumulated deficit. Just as
soon as Turner went out of office that wise proviso
was suspended, and another sinking fund went
bung. In like manner, the Lyne Government in
N.S.W. decided manfully to pay a considerable
extra sum annually in reduction of the deficit—
and then suspended the new arrangement inde-
finitely before the first payment fell due, and kept
it suspended. Maoriland also has a sinking fund—
the weirdest of all. Every year the Govern-
ment scrupulously and honestly makes the stipu-
lated payment into the fund, and then borrows a
corresponding amount and puts it into revenue.
The sinking fund also increases by the accumula-
tion of interest, and the Government borrows
an amount equal to the year’s interest accu-
mulation, and puts that into revenue like-
wise. Obviously, the sinking fund never gets any
“ forrarder ”—in fact there is none, except in
name. The other States that have sinking-funds
(except Westralia) have annexed them, except in
name, by similar processes. The payment to the
funds made a deficit in the revenue, and more
money was borrowed to make good the deficit.
Obviously, Australasia can’t be trusted with its
own sinking-fund any more than a monkey can
be trusted to deal honestly with a bag of nuts. In
the ordinary course of events, the fund is started
with a great flourish of trumpets one year ; and
suspended next year; and in the third year
the first year’s payment is seized. The fact
that these seven States haven’t enough stability of
purpose to stick to their good resolution for three
years running is childish and pitiable, and sug-
gestive of Kipling’s description of the chaotic
monkey republic, which had no fixed laws
or purpose, but merely whirled its tail pro-
miscuously _in the tree-tops but the fact
is too obvious to be shirked. The Bulle-
tin would seriously suggest that an organised
effort be made to take up a subscription among
the rich men of Australia to create a real sinking-
fund, and that the money be deposited with the
Bank of England. There it would have to accu-
mulate, the interest being re-invested every year
in the purchase of securities (not necessarily Aus-
tralian securities, for otherwise these Governments
might get at the sinking-fund by refusing to pay
interest on the bonds held by the fund) till the
total amounted to enough to wipe off the whole
debts of the six Commonwealth States. It would
be a pity to hand the business over to an outside
institution and let it receive the per cent, com-
mission on the income—which would be a large
item in course of years—but the money couldn’t
be entrusted to an Australian institution; if it
was, an Act for the confiscation of the whole lot
would be passed before ten years had gone by. If
the hat was passed round, would Australia pan
out for a total subscription of a million to start a
real sinking fund ? Or would that hat be seized
by Shiels or Waddell as it went by and sold for
2s. fid., and the half-crown be put into revenue ?

The Enfranchised Female.
The women Qf New South Wales have now full
franchise rights, both for the Federal and State
Parliaments. One-woman-one-vote is as much a
r «ahty as one-man-one-vote. A body of voters
suddenly enfranchised could not wish for a better
opportunity than the N.S.W. women now have
to start a political career in a notable way.
Women are, without a doubt, by nature or by
training, of a more economical nature than men
(speaking ot women in general, not of the few
society ” women). The housewife in Australia
Has, as a rule, precious little to housekeep on, and
what she gets she has to do with. There are no
means by which she “can raise the wind,’’outside
of the sum. from £1 to £4, which her hus-
band brings her home at the week-end. Tne
amount may be miserably inadequate to provide
even decent comfort; but she has to make it do.
There are no surplus goods in the household on
which she can borrow money without courting
absolute disaster by the sale of “ her few sticks.”
She has precious little credit, and practically no
hope of a “windfall.” Her life, therefore, has to
be one of steady grinding economy. Now, as her
domestic conditions, so the national conditions of
N.S.W. at the present time. The indebtedness
of the State is such that it can’t afford any frills,
and, figuratively speaking, should eat molasses
with its bread because butter is too dear, and give
EP aii expensive display and face the situation.
What woman has to do in the household
N.S.W. ought to do in the State. And women
paving the franchise can see that it is done.
The block woman vote, if cast for a regime of
household economy in public affairs, would utterly
overwhelm the Owe’Sullivan’s gorgeous extrava-
gance and probably bring that pliant politician to
the front with a platform of cut-to-the-bone
economy. Woman in private life can’t be a bor-
rower. The average woman won’t lend money to
another woman, and the woman who borrows
money from a man has generally to pay for it
otherwise than financially. Let her in public life
be as little of a, borrower as she is in private life,
and N.S.W. in a generation or two will rise
up and call political woman blessed. The
Bulletin has not at heart much hope that
woman will rise to the occasion. It is
more than probable that her eye will be captured
by the cheap Brummagem glare of the borrowing
policy and that she will be an unreasoning
follower of the system of having a glorious time
to-night and darn the headache to-morrow. But
the opportunity is there, if woman is equal to it —
as great an opportunity as a newly-enfranchised
class has ever had in history.

Sydney Telegraph has laid in a new and
infuriated correspondent (one “Britisher”),
who apparently wants German ships shut
out of British and Australian ports, and
who goes qn in a terrible fashion about what
Britain has done for Germany, and the debt of
gratitude Germany owes to Britain, and how
“Germany has feasted and prospered under the
systematic manner in which she has worked
England’s Freetrade policy,” and so on. He is a
desperately excited person, is “ Britisher,” and
he is a fine representative of a strange but very
popular delusion. The British Empire lives to a
considerable extent on Germany—that country,
despite the “made in Germany” joke, buys far
more from the British empire than it sells to the
British empire. It is Britain’s best customer ;
yet the average person, who never reads trade
statistics, has a wild idea that the German is pick-
ingl British pockets all the time. Germany didn’t
impose any protective duties against British goods
till sundry of the British States set the example
by penalising German goods—a thing they
were perfectly right in doing. There is
more British shipping in German ports than
tnere is German shipping in British ports.
“ Britisher’s ” bitterest grievance is that
the German Government is said to be a
shareholder in the North German Lloyd’s. Even
if that is so, it is difficult to see that there is any
more harm in it than in the British Government
being a shareholder in the Suez Canal and making
£830,000 a year out of its shares (over 20 per cent,
on the £4,000,000 it paid for them), a profit partly
made at the expense of German shipping. There
are many countries which are making a good
thing out of Britain’s Freetrade policy (the United
States does more in that way than any other three
countries put together), but Germany, almost the
only really profitable customer Britain has, arouses
more enmity than all of them. It is a kind of
trade insanity.
♦
The proposal, affirmed in S.A. Assembly by 21
votes to 16, that the Government should buy the
Fulton pipe works, now for sale, and make pipes
for the public, would be at all events a sensible kind
of relief work, even if it proved to be nothing better
than relief work. There would at least be no debt
attached to it. The Government would get pipes
for the money it paid, even if it didn’t get so many
pipes as it should do. Compared with the usual
process of floating a loan, and making a road that
is of no real use to anybody, and then paying
some hundreds every year to keep the road in
repair, the pipe-making idea has a whole ocean of
common sense behind it. Even if it was so badly
managed that the State paid £lOOO for the pipes
it could import for £BOO there would still be £BOO a
year less going out of the country to pay for im-
ported pipes, whereas the “ public works ” device
leaves just as much money as ever going out to
pay for imports, and generally something extra
going out to pay interest. It is better for Australia
to pay its people £lOOO for the pipes than to pay a
foreigner i/800iind haveits own people unemployed.
It is better, if the unemployed must be relieved,
to set them making anything that will save the
country paying for imports, than to set them forest-
thinning and doing like jobs which leave the
country with just as much imported goods to pay
for as before, and which add to the public debt at
the same time. If the unemployed difficulty can’t
be got over otherwise, let us have State cotton
factories, linen factories, distilleries—anything
where the wages will give some tangible return
and where the present generation will pay the bill
if there is one to pay—anything except the big
loans and scrub-cutting jobs, and the relief work
which gives no return, or practically none. Even
if the Treasury lost £1,000,000 in running these
new industries it would probably mean £20,000,000
less paid away to the foreigner for imported goods,
and the country would be much better off. A
million spent on the usual kind of relief works
generally means: (1) that the £20,000,000 has
gone away to pay for imports just as usual; (2)
that it is necessary to spend another million to
prevent the scrub growing again. Also, the
worst-managed State factory may always be made
profitable by better management, but the scrub-
cutting job is just an expanse of dust, and all the
good management on earth won’t make it any-
thing more.
The history of the Factories and Shops Act
Continuation Bill in Vic. Leg. Council fully
justifies Liberals in the Assembly putting their
whole souls into the Reform Bill now before that
Chamber. The Factories Continuation Bill was
passed through all its stages in the Assembly in a
few minutes. It provided merely for the con-
tinuation of the present law until such time as the
Royal Commission, appointed to inquire into
industrial legislation, had reported to Parliament.
While the Bill was on its way to the Council that
rat-necked body dodged out of its wav by
adjourning for a fortnight. When it met again it
carried a resolution postponing the second reading
of the measure for a month. The object of that
move stuck out plainer than a Leg. Coun-
cillors waistband. The Factories and Shops
Act will expire at the close of the pre-
sent session. If anything happens, such as
a dissolution, to close the session suddenly,
the Act will be wiped off the statute-book. Fat-
man does not like an Act that compels him to
The Limited Monarchy.
(A Study in Constitutional Evolution.)
When Log the First was gone— a useless thing,
Then cried the Frogs : “ Again we seek a King—
A King, 0 mighty Zeus, with tooth and maw,
A King to lay the backs of rebels raw,
A King to guard us and a chief to guide
And fill the hearts of Frogland high with pride.”
The Lord of great Olympus smiled again,
And sent a Watersnake to rule and reign.
The slimy Ruler, fierce of fang and jaw,
Soon filled the poor Batrachian tribe with awe,
For ev’ry morn he gulped a fatted score,
And every evening called for twenty more.
His son succeeded when the father died,
And bitter anguish filled the Frogland wide.
At length great Zeus, to ease their bitter pain,
Drew up their King, then cast him down again,
But sent him back (to where he’d ravened red)
With ne’er a tooth to arm his greedy head ;
Whereat the frogs, elated, burst the skies
With song and croak and wild exultant cries,
Yet seemed to love His Kingship'more and more,
And paid him greater reverence than before.
Said Zeus, astounded : “What, ye fools, ye hail
As King a toothless wretch of no avail;
A helpless reptile wriggling through the flood,
A limp and harmless Lord of marsh and mud ! ”
“ We joy, 0 Lord,” the sapient frogs replied,
“ To see our King in splendor cleave the tide;
We joy to think, although he cannot bite,
He almost looks at times as if he might;
We joy to know, in loyal bosoms glad,
He has no teeth, but then his father had / ”

♦
Being driven like a dry leaf before the
Wind of Circumstance by an unexpectedly loud
demand for more “Village Priest,” Robert Brough
abandoned his intention to reproduce the
“Tyranny of Tears ” at Sydney Royal, and that
item is off the bill. The “Priest” was a good
Eerformance, though Brough’s abandonment of
is fine old part of Jean Torquenie for that of the
Abbe was a regettable circumstance. Lovell did
Jean in fairly good style, considering that that
light young man only howls along the gloomy
paths of tragedy once in a while, but Brough was
much missed. On the {other hand Robert B.’s
first appearance as the Abbe was a large event.
Neither Titheradge nor Carne ever did that
humble pastor better, if, indeed, they did him
quite as well, yet both looked better fitted
physically to doze in the bosom of the Church
than Brough does. The miracle, whereby a
casual draught blows over a leaf of the Scripture
happened as usual; it is a well-oiled miracle, and
always happens up to time. One thing that will be
regretted, now that “The Village Priest ”is gone,
is the garden. It was a nice, cool, green garden,
with ancient, mossy walls around it, and in a
country where the haggard post-and-wire fence
straddles the dry leagues of land such a garden is
a blessed thing to look upon.
* * * •
Unless there is some more unexpected change
in the arrangements, “Mrs. Tanqueray” will be
on at the Royal this (Thursday) evening. It
will be the last appearance of that eminent mem-
ber of the oldest profession on earth—that
profession which doubtless began with the Fall,
when sin, ugliness, pickles, tobacco and
procrastination, to say nothing of other ills,
came upon this world. “When We Were
Twenty-one” is due on Friday and Saturday,
with “ Sowing the Wind ” to follow. Just now,
when most other branches of agriculture are under
a temporary cloud, it is pleasing to note that this
branch is still prospering, and The Bulletin
appeals to the Sydney community to roll up at the
Royal and give one of our great natural resources
some encouragement. The seed may be sown almost
anywhere, and if it is watered with loans, beer,
girl and profanity, and a veil of hypocrisy is drawn
over it when the sun is hot, the crop is something
extraordinary. “Niobe,” “Lady Ursula” and
“The Liars” on Wednesday, Thursday and
Friday of next week are to close the Brough
season —the last Brough season of all.
* * *
“ San Toy,” continuing its bright career at
Sydney Palace to crowded houses, gives no reason
to modify the first favorable impression of its
entertaining power. The comedy element is
chiefly relied upon for success, and it would be
hard to get a better comedy team than Li, Yen
How, the Emperor, and maid Dudley. Mr.
Frank Pierce, as Yen How, sings his patter songs
so well, and talks with such a humorous air of
melancholy, as to quite rival Mr. Graham’s efforts
as Li, the bland heathen with “ kleptoluniac ”
habits. On a second hearing, too, the music of
“San Toy ” shows as of finer quality. There is
no ambitious attempt at orchestration, nor even
any startling degree of novelty, but the songs are
always melodious, and their setting quaint and
pretty. Miss Rose Musgrove has more confidence
than on the opening night, and sings more clearly
and strongly ; and Miss Digges’ chirpy voice
chirps better, and her dainty, bird-like hopping
hops better with longer acquaintance.
** * *
“The Lady Slavey ” continues to wind up
her useful career every evening at Sydney
Criterion by wedding her millionaire in a remark-
able hurry, also in a fashionable costume which
she seems to have had put together in the space of
ten minutes or so, or else she kept it by her in
case a millionaire should happen along. The elec-
tric lamp which bogus-millionaire Sheridan sports
in his shirt-front by way of a diamond shines bright-
ly, as do the various musical gems of this fairly
bright production. Whether Sheridan’s play will
do the harassed modern “ missus ” a good turn by
making girls take to domestic service in hope of
marrying a millionaire, or whether it will make
the few girls already in the business more inflated
and unbearable by reason of the improved pros-
pect opening before them, is not clear. A special
matinee, which is to be held at the Cri. on Satur-
day, may throw further light or further darkness
on this problem.
** * #
At Sydney Lyceum “ With Flying Colors ”
pursues the uneven tenor of its way, the supposed
suicide being as unconvincing as ever, and the
scenery still atoning for much. As a drama that
roves from London railways to wayside cottages,
and that is equally at home at a naval ball and at
Dartmoor prison it is hard to beat, though the
colors don’t fly to any extent worth mentioning,
and the name of the drama bears little relevancy
to the drama itself. Not that that is at all un-
usual. As a general thing the name of any drama
would do equally well, or just as badly, for any 27
other dramas, and if all dramas went by the same
name it would save a great deal of trouble.
*# * *
Smith’s Empire Company, which hangs
out at Sydney Protestant Hall, is a combination
which modestly eschews programmes as a for-
mality of which it is not worthy. All the same,
it gives a very large show with a great many
items in it, and a good deal of audience is gener-
ally present, some of which spends the time in
genial slumber. The night The Bulletin dropped
in a large baby and some small juggling tricks
were amongst the items, but the greatest applause
was won by a girl who left off in the middle of her
item to have hysterics behind the scenes, and then
came on once more. For there is more joy in Syd-
ney over one singer that breaks down and gets up
again than over 99 righteous persons who reach
their top note and need no repentance. Though
there are no programmes somebody mentions the
name of each performer in an indistinct voice, so
that everything is satisfactory.
** # *
A Mount Kembla disaster benefit concert
on a large scale is to be held at Sydney Town
Hall on Saturday. Social and official dignitaries,
of such weight that the globe tips up when they
step, are lending their joint and several counten-
ance. Misses Amy Castles, Maud Dalrymple,
and Eva O’Connor, also Phil Newbury, Neal
M‘Cay, R. Needham, and City Organist Arthur
Mason are among the names on the programme,
likewise Hazon, Benno Scherek, and the Sydney
Amateur Orchestral Society in general.
** * *
A few grown-ups and a lot of children
presented an original three-act comedy at a
matinee at Sydney Palace on Saturday. The
piece has the alliterative title of the “ Marrying of
Ma,” and it is by one J. C. Lee, a local youth.
Mr. Lee has made a good start as a playwright.
He has got off a few decent epigrams and several
very comic situations. He ought to achieve some-
thing distinctly good on the same lines as Broad-
hurst, but of better literary merit. The youngsters
who interpreted the piece evidently had excellent
drilling, and were the best amateur juvenile
mummers this writer has ever seen. They reflect a
great and shining credit on the Players’ Club, to
which they belong.

Barron Morley makes his farewell to-nighfc
(Thursday), in the Centenary Hall, York-street.
All good Australians know that pianist Morley is
far ahead of Paderewski, and that he can out-con-
certo any dead pianist, even the great Rubenstein
himself. He leaves for America by the Ventura,
on Monday, to give the Yankees a much-wanted
treat. We repay evil with good, and loan them
our star pianist in return for the unasked-for loan
of the awful Geil and Torrey. Tenor Newbury
is associated in to-night’s concert with the Fertile
Morley.
** * *
America, the land of the five-minutes*
dinner, has set itself to condense the drama into
ten-minutes playing time, and a specimen of the
compressed show was offered in Sydney by the
Drews at the Tivoli last Saturday. “ When Two
Hearts are Won” is Shakespeare’s “Taming of
the Shrew,” as revised, improved, corrected, and
brought up-to-date by an American author. In a
very dainty and novel setting (the bridal apart-
ments of the Hotel Astoria, by Clint), Mr. and
Mrs. Drew developed their comedy within 10 or
15 minutes to a perfect hurricane of laughter.
There is no very high art about play or
players; simply broad, slap-dash comedy
and character-drawing of the poster type;
but it is genuinely funny, and altogether
novel. Mr. and Mrs. Shamley have been married
just six hours, and Mr. Shamley sees that his
wife’s main devotion is to her dog and that she
has a cayenne temper. He starts to fight fire
with fire, and effects a complete cure by wrecking
the flat, almost murdering the dog, and threaten-
ing to “biff” the lady—all in a few minutes.
Cinquevalli now does some quite new juggling
tricks, and Tom Woottwell sings a fresh budget
of songs. Miss Florrie Gallimore re-appears after
a somewhat lengthy absence through illness, and
shows as a clever dancer and a bright singer; but
she should give up “yodelling”; she hasn’t got
the voice for it.
** # *
“ Robin Hood,” which came new to Mel-
bourne, at Her Majesty’s, on Saturday, is the
work of a capital musician, who had the misfor-
tune to take a deplorable Henry B. Smith for
librettist. Composer De Kovan, notwithstanding
his baptismal name of Reginald, falls only a little
short of excellence in his music, whereas Henry
B. Smith rarely exceeds puerility in his “book.”
The result is rather irritating to people who
would have welcomed “Robin Hood” in
a serious, romantic opera form, even though
the plot had been as seriously ridi-
culous as “The Bohemian Girl.” On the other
hand it is not likely to delight the multitude that
wants a light opera to be quite as conversational
as, say, “Dorothy.” A flimsy pantomime narra-
tive set to sparkling music is treated by a musical
comedy co., the performance being too good for
Henry B. Smith, and not good enough for De
Kovan. One leaves the show with the feeling
that there was nothing harmonious in it, except
the melodies. Regarded as food and drink for
the soul, it had the effect of salt pork on a rare
porcelain plate, and choice champagne in a
pewter-pot. It was acted above its merits, and
sung beneath its deserts.
The legendary Robin, as he appears in the flesh
at Her Majesty’s, is a piump,
who fools round the ancient town of Nottingham
waiting to be a rightful earl when he r
age of 21. Carrie Moore, whom heßas never
is under instructions to marry his title,

George Lauri and Hugh Ward transpire
Lauri being the Bailie of Cornev-no, the Sheriff
of NottingLm-and Ward a droll sort of d isciple
suffering from general imbecility and co
nose. For financial reasons Lauri declares his
palsied follower to be the rightful earl whois
going to wed Carrie’s dowry, whereupon Robm
swears to trump La un’s trick (in a 12th century
way of speaking).. With a view to secreting
the four aces in his buckskin boot, angry .
Hood loins the firm of Thorley, Ban took, Bathurst

where Carrie Moore comes along in another cos-

The staging and the chorus are the best
features of this tuneful production, yet the chorus
singing and the orchestral work as a rule might
be more expressive. Not one of the principals. is
a conspicuous success. Miss Young, whose voice
has gone off of late, got most applause on Satur-
day for the well-known ballad. “O, promise me,
and Miss Carrie Moore’s prettiest notes rang out
sweetly in “If all the stars were mine, the
song she used to sing in a pantomime. But
Carrie Moore strains after effect. Her style
is louder than her voice. She opens her
mouth and shuts her eyes too much. .Also
she rolls those eyes too much. Lauri and Ward
would be irreproachable if they were brighter
vocalists, not that Lauri sings badly for a low
comedian. Miss Ghiloni is out of place as Dame
Durden. Miss Chetwynd doesn’t sing the part of
Annabel effectively, or look in the least like a
girl of any period but the present. Thorley, as
Littlejohn, isnotan impressive outlawed baritone,
and the fine song Stannis Leslie sings in a rich
voice might as well be Greek as English.
New tenor, George Lyding, starts with the dis-
advantage of an American accent, which, though
quite in keeping with an allusion to trump cards,
seems utterly foreign to Robin Hood. He fills the
stage very well, and his voice is of “ manly tenor
quality.

“ Sweet Nell ” is proceeding steadily at
Melb. Princess’s. Two nights last week Albert
Gran was kept out of the cast by a sore throat,
and in his absence the lurid villainy of the Lord
Chief Justice fell to Harry Hill’s care. Hill, a
better character actor nowadays than many other
people who get better opportunities, made Jeffries
a comparatively quiet and probable - looking
monster. Gran boils over with wrath all the time,
Hill’s hatred and malice were less demonstrative,
but what the show gained in naturalness it lost in
atmospheric effect through the change. Gran
amounts practically to an intermittent thunder-
storm, and the play, being gilt-edged melodrama
in a poetical binding, can carry off a good deal of
electrical disturbance. The original Jef resumed
his gross injustice on Saturday. Meantime
Nellie Stewart’s throat had gone wrong, so. the
picturesque king in his high-backed chair didn’t
get favored with her song about the pleasures of
love.
** # *
At Melb. Royal there will be only two more
nights of “The Worst Woman in London.” Mel-
bourne has shown a sufficiently warm interest in
the Worst Woman and her vengeful lunatic, but
the Anderson season has other dramas awaiting
their turn within a limited number of weeks.
Therefore the elderly husband will go to bed in
his boots for the last time on Friday night. ‘ ‘ The
Ladder of Life ” comes in action again on Satur-
day. Although “The Ladder” is otherwise
known as “Gordons to the Front,” its fiery
patriotism is tempered with considerable humor of
a sort.

At the Opera House things are the same
as they were a week ago, except that Fagan has
departed, and Harris, the lamp-post inspector, is
telling his sad old tale once again. Pictures of
Sand ow in all his muscular nakedness adorn the
Opera House vestibule; also the appalling majah-
general who poses at the entrance is observed to
have a more padded chest development than
usual; therefore Melbourne assumes that the
Strong Man will arrive shortly.

The Bijou, where “Cyrano de Bergerac ”
perished of cold neglect, is now awaiting the
return of the “Mars” company; and the
Athenseum Hall will probably be vacated by
M'Adoo’s Jubilee Singers after Saturday.
** * *
At Adelaide Royal Charles Holloway has
made a personal appearance in ‘ ‘ The Power and
the Glory ” —which, taken to pieces, turns out to
mean only the power of wealth and the glory of
“ motherhood.” It was finely mounted and
well acted, Holloway, in particular, being every-
where at all times, after the ordinary manner of
a British tar. At present “The French Spy” is
being produced, with scenic effects varying from
Paris to Kronstadt and back. At the Tivoli,
Sandow carries the programme on his back, as
well as men and pianos and other baubles. He
made a preliminary appearance before a stageful
of doctors and lawyers, and obtained a certificate
that he was sound in limb and wind. His feats
of strength, besides being marvellous, are excel-
lently stage-managed, and his show is the talk of
the city.

The Standard Life Association, Limited
(Sydney), one of the new crop of proprietary life assur-
ance institutions which have sprung up in recent years,
publishes its third annual report (for year ended 30th
June, 1902): ~ ,
Total Added
Total Claims. Ex- Out- to Deficit.
Income. penses. goings. Funds.
1900 . £9,193 £2lO £12,998 £13,214 £4021
1901 24 593 453 23,833 24,280 £307 3714
' Assur-
ance
Fund.
1902.. 31,481 1081 25,838 26,919 4562 848
Includin' 1 - the paid-up capital of £12,500, therefore, the
actual funds are £13,348. As is usual with new com-
panies in the industrial business, part of the expenses are
carried to a suspense account, which makes the assurance
fund look like £6348, but this species of suspense account,
not being a real asset, is disregarded in the above state-
ment. The income for the year just closed consists of
interest, &c., £248 ; ordinary life premiums, £6926 ; and
industrial premiums, £24,307, which is a very large
income for an office in its third year. The A.M.P. was in
its 12th year before it had such an income ; in its third
year the A.M.P.’s receipts were only £1667. But the
A.M.P. in its third year spent barely 25 per cent, of its
revenue on working expenses, while the Standard Life
Association shows up as follows:
Expenses Compared with Total Income.
1900.. £l4l 710 per cent. 1902 .. £B2 1 6 per cent.
1901.. 96 18 2 „
These rates, large as they are, are nothing very
unusual in the pursuit of the thriftless and extravagant
industrial life business. But though the balance-sheet,
for the last two years, has shown the ordinary and
industrial premiums separately, it doesn’t show the
expenses of the two departments separately, nor
is there any separate assurance fund shown for the ordi-
nary life business. If the ordinary life business is being
done at a cost of 30 per cent, then the industrial business
is still costing 97 per cent.—which would mean that about
19s. sd. out of every £1 paid by the co.’s clients went last
year in expenses, and only 7d. was left to accumulate
towards payment of their policies. And as yet the share-
holders have received no dividend—if they had things
would have been worse. Of course there can be no doubt
that there will be a material reduction in the expense
rate as time goes on, but with the great competition that
exists in industrial business there is no prospect of it ever
being conducted cheaply enough to make it worth the
while of any sensible person to take out an industrial
policy. The industrial business lives on the fools and the
ignorant.
The best feature about the Standard Life
Association is that its investments (£10,502 in all) are
solely in Government debentures and Sydney municipal
securities. Presumably, no matter what the great Aus-
tralian loan policy leads to, the interest on locally-owned
State securities will be paid, and, so long as it sticks to
this system, the Standard Life Association will never be
going round Sydney trying to sell a big building for
£16,000 less than the amount of.the mortgage. But the
Association should, at the earliest possible date, begin to
keep a totally separate account of its ordinary life busi-
ness. That is a thing to be taken seriously, even if the
industrial allair isn’t. Further, the multiplication of
proprietary offices nowadays is a powerful reason why
every possible haste should be made to pass a sensible life
assurance law providing for rigid State audit and State
valuation of securities, making an actuarial valuation
compulsory at certain fixed intervals, and fixing the basis
of that valuation on a standard which will make solvency
a matter of certainty.

The Australian Brewery and Wine and

This last transaction makes £155,876 (£48,000 capital and

1891, which is a large amount for a concern whose whole
capital and reserves in 1891 were only £108,214. And
even now the co. appears to be deeply mortgaged. Its
balance-sheet shows no cash or liquid assets (apart from
debts due by customers and the ordinary stock-in-trade)
in hand, while among its liabilities appears “ Sundry
creditors and due to Bank,” £74,835.

The Melbourne Tramway and Omnibus Co.
finished a highly profitable year on 30th June, 1902. This
co. lives, to some extent, on the sympathy of the public as
a monopoly that isn’t doing too well. Its reserves are made
to look much smaller than they really are through £230,000
of them being transferred to capital. Its dividends are
made ,to ‘look much smaller that they are by being des-
cribed as 12£ per cent, bn £480,000 capital when they are
really 24 per cent, on £230,000, which was the capital
actually subscribed by the shareholders. In the follow-
ing table an effort is made to show things exactly as they
are, by calculating the dividend on the £250,000 actually
subscribed by the shareholders, and including in the re-
serves that £230,000 which was disguised by being trans-
ferred to capital account. All purely paper items are
eliminated from the reserves. Owing to a certain lack of
detail in some of the earlier accounts the profits shown
for 1889 are a close approximation only ; from that date
onward they are absolutely accurate:

These things don’t necessarily prove that the Melbourne
Tramway and Omnibus Co. can afford to reduce fares or
grant any of the other concessions asked for by the
public, but they go a very long way in that direction.
The co. is one of the richest monopolisers of a valuable
public concession in this hemisphere, and even in the bad
times, when its annual report was full of dirge-like
remarks about the falling off, it was doing better than
most concerns did in the heyday of the boom.

Wheat was quoted in Sydney a few days ago at
4s. 9d. a bushel and flour at £lO a ton, and Sydney
Telegraph stated in an excited tone that “some people
are making fortunes out of fodder and grain just now.”
Pity the Telegraph didn’t notice the same fact under
Freetrade in 1896 and 1897. In 1896 wheat averaged
4s. 3d. a bushel in Sydney, and flour averaged £lol3s. Id.
a ton ; in other words, the grower received 6d. a bushel
less for his wheat than the Protectionist figure the
Telegraph was lamenting about, and the consumer paid
13s. Id. a ton more for his flour, and the blessed importer
or middleman grabbed the rest. Again, in 1897, under
Freetrade, wheat averaged 4s. sfd. per bushel, and flour
£l2 ss. 3d. per ton—the producer received 3Jd. a bushel
less, and the consumer paid £2 ss. 3d. per tou more than
the figure the D.T. was so much grieved about.

"Very extensive alterations and improvements
have just been completed at the engineering works of
Mephen Ferguson, Melbourne. The principal item of
interest is a new Electric Welding plant, which is now in
use welding the steel-plates required in the manufacture
of the patent spiral riveted pipes which are in such large
demand for water-supply, mining and other purposes.
The new method of welding is giving very satisfactory
results, and is well worth seeing.*

The antidote to cyanide poisoning, as supplied
by Ernest de Beer, 439 Bourke-street, Melbourne, has
been approved by the Victorian Mines Department as
being the only real antidote known. All who use
cyanide should keep one or two of these on hand. The
price is 10s. each.*

* • • •
Many doubtful claims have been advanced on
behalf of duplex pumps. Some makers state that their
duplex pumps are “ double the capacity of single pumps
of same diameter and stroke.” An A. S. Cameron single-
acting pump can be run safely and comfortably at a speed
that would knock a duplex pump to pieces. But even at
a low speed, duplex pumps seldom attain even their
theoretical capacities, and the reason for this can be
readily explained. Apply to the Agents, Messrs. W. and
J. Lempriere, 506 Little Collins Street, Melbourne, for
further particulars. The A. S. Cameron pumps are noted
for their simplicity, durability, and absence of outside
valve gear. *
* * • *
The Austral Otis Engineering Company, Ltd.,
of South Melbourne, have under construction at their
works a complete waterworks pumping plant for the
Greenbushes Tin Mining Co., West Australia. The plant,
which consists of a set of Triple Expansion Condensing
Duplex pumping engines with Multitubular boilers, is a
very fine and powerful one, capable of supplying one
million gallons per day. The pumps have to deliver
through a line of pipes 6£ miles in length, and raise the
water 800 feet in height. To do this heavy work the
pumps have been specially designed with four rams or
plungers, directly connected to the triple-expansion
engines. The Austral Otis Company make a speciality of
waterworks pumping plants, and were the builders of the
fine new sewerage pumping engines, lately installed by
the Melbourne and Metropolitan Board of Works, and
which weigh upwards of 240 tons.*

For the first time since the State took chief control of
its affairs the profits of the Bank of New Zealand show
a diminishing tendency. All the same they are very
large profits indeed. The 5 per cent, dividend is the first
that the private shareholders (whose paid-up capital
stands at £427,320) have received for a good number of
years.
The Assets Realisation Board, which is the
institution into which all the worst assets of the Bank of
New Zealand were put when the new regime commenced,
shows a much worse falling off than does the bank itself.
This concern began with what may fairly be called a
visible deficit of £54,507, inasmuch as it took over its
properties at £54,507 over and above the latest valuation.
If this is included (though in some previous notices in
this column the item was omitted) the progress has been
as follows:

on Property Loss. from Deficit

1902.. 62,280 18,365 80,651 151,135 88,647
The Assets Realisation Board took over practically all the
stations and other landed assets of the old Bank of New
Zealand ; and also became responsible for a liability of
£2,680,285 in 3£ per cent, debentures, of which it has, so
far, only redeemed £330,285, leaving £2,350,000 still out-
standing (of these £1,600,000 worth are held by the bank).
The process of winding up is, therefore, decidedly
slow. The year just closed was the very worst
the Assets Realisation Board has had, so far as
concerns the earnings of its properties. It had £82,827
to pay in interest, and only earned £20,541. It has only
succeeded, out of its own resources, in paying off £30,000
of its debentures, the other £300,000 having been paid on
its behalf by the Bank of New Zealand.
The real situation as regards the private share-
holders is best arrived at by taking the net earnings of
the Bank of N.Z. and its Assets Realisation Board as one.
The interest paid on sums advanced by, or raised on the
guarantee of, the State is first deducted. The balance
shows what is available to write off old losses, either those
of the Bank or the Assets Realisation Board, and for
dividends:—
Combined Report, Bank op N.Z. and Assets
Realisation Board.

The net amount provided since the Bank of New Zealand
came under State control in reduction of the losses of the
old private management has thus been £436,466. In
addition £75,000 has been provided out of earnings
to pay for the purchase of the Colonial Bank’s
business, and one 5 per cent, dividend has been
paid in seven years. The net earnings of the joint
institution last year (£147,216) were at the rate of
£34 9s. per cent, on the private shareholders’ capital,
which is the record for Australasia. At the same
time if the Bank of New Zealand were situated as other
banks are, and all its capital ranked equally for dividends,
the earnings would be only at the rate of £9 7s. 3d. per
cent, which is a considerable difference. The business is
steadily increasing; deposits are now over £10,000,000 ;
and the reserves of cash and liquid securities are very
solid, amounting to over £5,500,000, without reckoning
£1,600,000 of the debentures of the Assets Realisation
Board. The only drawback is the serious falling off in
the earnings of the joint institution. If there is any per-
manent slump in the Assets Board’s earnings it may tend
to postpone the final clearing up of the debris of the old
institution, and till that is finished the Bank’s share-
holders can’t expect to get the dividends they might
otherwise look for. And the realisation process is slow
enough, without any further delay. Also, if the earnings
of the Board continue at anything like last year’s low
rate the value of the assets must be much less, and the
deficit to be made good by the Bank much more, than was
formerly supposed.

The profits for the year just closed were equal to a shade
over 10J per cent., but there are good and sufficient
reasons for keeping the dividend down to 3J. The whole
paid-up capital of £145,953 wasn’t sufficient to pay for
the original purchase of the property. Most of the
reserve has since been invested in additions to the build-
ings and plant; and the huge business (stock on hand
alone is valued at £110,212) is mostly run on borrowed
money. The record of the mortgages and bank over-

It is a case of running an immense and growing business
on a capital which, large as it is, is quite insufficient. If
the present system of small dividends is continued the
accumulated reserves may, in time, enable these heavy
liabilities to be paid off, but it is essential to keep the
dividends low till that is done.
■» * * #
The South Australian Treasurer reckons on
making ends meet next year by levying a 2| per cent, tax
on money going through the totalisator; by increasing
the bank note tax to 3 per cent. ; making it obligatory to
put a Id. stamp on receipts for sums over £2 ; raising the
land-tax to fd. in the £; and lowering the income-tax
exemption to £l2O, with a slight increase in the tax on
large incomes. By these devices it is expected that next
year will show a surplus of £lll2—the old deficit being-
shunted comfortably into the great mass of the public
debt. Thus no attempt is even proposed to pay off any
part of last year’s deficit, or to check the process of using
loan money for purposes of ordinary expenditure.
An Australian Treasurer isn’t supposed to keep the
finances solvent ; all that is expected of him
is. to put last year’s deficit out of sight and
make a plausible paper calculation to the effect that
things this year won’t be so bad as they might be. The
S.A. Treasurer bases his calculation on a 6-bushel per
acre harvest —a thing that S.A. hasn’t had since 1894,
though it has had harvests since then that were over five
bushels—and also harvests that were under two bushels.
There have, been exactly two S.A. harvests in the last
11 years which realised six bushels or a little more to the
acre.

How the pleasant old delusions stick ! Wes-
tern Mail (Perth, W. A.) tells its readers:—“Of course
the primary industry of this State is undoubtedly mining.
No one doubts that. But just as certainly the second
place of importance belongs to agriculture.” This is a
flattering dream, but it won’t bear inspection. The
things on which “Westralia really lives, and the annual
income it derives from them, according to latest available
statistics, are these :
Mining ..£6,346,000
Manufactures .. .. „ .. 1,515,000
Borrowing 1,495,292
Pastoral, dairying, &c. 1,120,000
Cutting down forests and selling them 734,000
Agriculture 600,000
£11,710,292
The mining business isn’t permanent, for the Golden
Mile won’t last for ever. The forests are not permanent;
that business consists in selling the country’s capital. The
loan business isn’t permanent. And as for agriculture, it
takes practically the whole value of the agricultural pro-
duce of Westralia—not the profit made out of the in-
dustry, but the whole value of the output—to pay interest
on the State’s public debt alone. And Westralia’s public
debt isn’t a circumstance to its private debt. Also, in
Westralia borrowing is about three times as big a resource
as agriculture. Last year, astounding as it may seem,
the Australian continent borrowed almost as much as the
whole value of its agricultural produce I Also, very
nearly, if not quite, its whole agricultural output was re-
quired to pay interest on its foreign public and private
liabilities—the profits of agriculture, after allowing for
the maintenance of the people employed, weren’t suffi-
cient to pay one-third of the interest on its foreign
liabilities! Australia is a barren country, and in
order to increase its crop by £lOO,OOO, it generally spends
at least £2,000,000 of loan moneys on roads, railways, and
other processes of “ opening up the country.” That is
why The Bulletin believes in the encouragement of
manufactures which don’t require such a costly process
of “ opening up."

The Sydney and Suburban Hydraulic Power Co. claims to
have £13,550 in reserves (Plant Renewal account, £9250;
Waterloo Lease Reserve, £2750 ; and undivided profit,
£1550). But against these it still keeps a goodwill item,
“ Parliamentary Rights and Concessions, £15,000,” stand-
ing among its assets, so it has an actual deficit of £1450.
And if the two reserves are really required for renewal of
plant and to cover the deterioration of the co.’s lease,
then the real deficit is £13,450. The absence of any
attempt to write off the £15,000 goodwill item is a very
bad feature about the management of the Sydney and
Suburban Hydraulic Co.

* • • *
In the Municipalities Bill, now before N.S.W.
Assembly, a special provision has been introduced for the
rating of mining properties. The system proposed is that
adopted in Queensland—that the rate is to be on the sur-
face of the land at the same value as is fixed for land of a
similar quality in the municipal area, and without regard
to production. In Victoria and W.A. mining properties
are exempt from municipal taxation. The Bulletin has
cited several instances where the assumption of munici-
palities that they were entitled to impose rates on the
value of the products of mines has acted most pre-
judicially to the mining interest. It is understood in
political circles that Premier See will have no difficulty in
inducing the Assembly to agree to the proposed system.

* * * *
Last half-year, with lead at =6ll 7s. Id. and
silver at 2s. 2Jd., the B.H. South made a profit of £23,622.
In the previous half, with lead at £lllos. 7d. and silver
at 2s. 4id., it made a profit of only £8695. In
the half-year to June, 1902, it treated 15,624 tons
more crude ore than in the half to December,
1901 (67,359 tons against 51,735 tons), in addition to
12,276 tons “old tailings.” The average value of the ore
and the percentage of extracted metal remained about
the same. Costs were a trifle lower. The increased
tonnage would account for about one-fourth more profit
than in the last half of ’01; the economies would no more
than counterbalance the further fall in metals—would, in
fact, hardly do that. This would leave over £13,000 of the
total profit to be accounted for by the “old tailings” ;
and, as these tailings will be exhausted in about six
months, it is pretty evident that, unless the metal mar-
kets improve a lot, South shareholders must not expect
after this current half-year any more profits of between
£23,000 and £24,000. In plain English, the previous half-
year’s profit of £8695 forms a far truer basis for estimating
the co.’s profit-earning capacity than the past half-year’s
£23,622. Of course, the mill’s capacity is larger than
when the £8695 was earned—between 40 and 50 per cent,
larger—and that must be allowed for.
The most hopeful feature about the B.H.
South’s report is the steady reduction in costs, not-
withstanding a steady increase in the percentage of the
metals extracted. Six years ago mining costs were
16s. 7Jd. per ton ; they are now 9s. lOd. (This is apart
from development.) Milling costs, formerly a fraction
over 55., have now been got down to 3s. 4fd. Meantime,
the recoveries have gone from 61'89 per cent, of the lead
to 70'22 percent., and of the silver from 47’60 per cent,
to 54’90 per cent. ; and instead of the concentrates being
“firsts” and “seconds,” the realisation charges on the
latter being very heavy, they are now all “ firsts.” These
double-barrelled improvements are the result of brains
and steady application. Compare the figures with
those of the Junction prior to its shut-down in
the middle of 1900. The recovery was there 30'7
per cent, of the silver and 52 per cent, of the
lead; the mining costs were 13s. 4£d. ; the milling
costs 4s. 6fd. That a mine should fail in those circs, isn’t
wonderful. Since then a new plant has been provided
at the Junction, but it, like its neighbour at the North,
is waiting for the lead-market to move. Probably the
Junction will never be able to give such good results as
the South ; but, in view of the work which the South is
now doing, long-suffering Junction shareholders certainly
need not despair, Indeed, there have been several
developments in the mine lately good enough to make
them very hopeful.
* * * •
The B.H. South Block half-yearly report (to
June 30) deals in generalities which are not good enough
in mining. The “work of development has progressed
satisfactorily." No. 3 shaft has been sunk “through
high-grade ore.” Other workings have been in “good ore,”
and the exploratory work has been “of a satisfactory
nature.” This is the sort of information which directors
and managers give. Not a single assay-return, not a
single fact by which to judge whether this “ high-grade
ore ”is payable or not. It seems most likely that, though
“ high-grade,” it would not, at present prices, anything
like pay to treat. The co., this half-year, brought 843
tons of “ good-grade ore ” to surface, at a cost (apart
from development) of, say, £B5O ; and its estimated value
is £295 ! That doesn’t look very promising. It is even
doubtful whether the co. could get £295 for it. The co.
still has £5517 to its credit; but that will not go very far
towards providing the concentrating-plant which will
have to be got before the co. can hope to make a profit,
even with a very substantial improvement in metals.
* * * •
Mt. Boppy, near Cobar, an off-shoot of the
Anglo-Australian Exploration Co. which floated the
Gibraltar, Gallymont and Bimbimbi mines in N.S.W., has
paid a div. absorbing £5157. Value of gold produced last
year, £18,822 ; expenses, £13,397. The magnificent plant
which was erected at Gallymont on the advice of Eissler,
was removed to Mt. Boppy. Reported that there are
several years’ work in sight, but the water difficulty is
seriously retarding operations. A large condensing plant
is now being erected to provide fresh water for the boilers
and other purposes. The Anglo-Australian Co. was very
unfortunate in its speculations in N.S.W. It developed
three mines on an extensive scale, and equipped two of
them correspondingly for the benefit of tributors. All
that now remains at Gallymont as evidence of Eissler’s
over-sanguineness is a big dam, the existence of which
has provoked many a big D 1 Eissler now poses as a
copper expert. But he made a sad bloomer when he
refused to recommend to his principals the purchase of
that splendid mine, Burraga, for a paltry £37,500.
* • * *
Reported that Mitchell’s Creek Freehold mine
(N.S.W.) is under offer in London for £200,000. Develop-
ments of late, no doubt, have been very good, and the
mine has been equipped with a fine up-to-date plant, but
there is a mighty jump between the value fixed by an
arbitrator about two years ago (when the then owners
were dissolving partnership) and £200,000. It has been
one of the standard gold mines of N.S.W. for years, not
sensationally rich, but remarkably consistent and with
marvellously long shutes of ore. Mitchell’s Creek presents
a conspicuous example of the lack of local enterprise. In
the Freehold mine there is one level about 4000 ft. long,
nearly all in pay ore, yet neither north nor south of this
mine has any legitimate attempt been made to locate the
lode. Rich surface patches were worked at both ends
many years ago.
* • * *
Though in the attack made upon them the
Moonta and Wallaroo directors won by 83,611 to 45,862,
the adverse vote was so great as to compel a change.
For the directors know there is more behind. A. G.
Downer, the largest shareholder in the co. and a man
whose word goes very far in S.A., declared for “new
blood ” ; yet it was well known that he would either not
vote at all on the motion to remove the directors, or vote
against it.
* * * *
The Walhalla (Vic.) amalgamation proposal has
developed into an unseemly wrangle between the two
co.’s as to the values of the mines. Long Tunnel people
assert that they have practically three mines to develop
within their ground, but that it will cost £30,000 and
take three years for them to again reach div. stage. The
Extended authorities reply that the L.T. figures are
largely imaginative. Noble, manager of the Extended,
hits the nail squarely when he asks, “ Would it not be
better for the L.T. people to grasp the substance which
is within reach than wait for three years and expend
£30,000, with the chance of finding only a shadow ?’’
Why not appoint three impartial men to appraise the
values of the mines and accept their decision as the basis
of amalgamation ?
* • • *
Fraser’s (Southern Cross), the forerunner of Cool-
gardie and Kalgoorlie, has long been under a cloud, bub
looks like emerging again and soon becoming a regular
producer on a larger soale than ever before. Previous co.
only worked the oxidised zone in a very primitive fashion.
New owners are opening up the sulphides systematically
with the aid of every known modern labor-saving device.
Results are proving highly satisfactory, the long-lost con-
tinuation of the lode having been located, of a payable
value, in No. 5 level.
* * * •
Bewick, Moreing and Co. continue spreading
their net to capture W.A. mines, their latest haul being
Bellevue Prop., Mt. Sir Samuel. This co. was but recently
reconstructed and provided with £70,000 in extra cash
capital. Mine so far has been a vast disappointment
though the property has great possibilities. B. C. Banon,
late of West Fingall, and previously of Hannan’s Kapai
and Kalgoorlie Mint, has been placed in charge. The mine
makes the man, and with such cats as these Banon
has not had a chance to earn a big reputation. Bellevue
may give him a show.
• • • •
May’s patent process, now in operation at the
Flagstaff, Ooolgardie, and also installed at the Eclipse,
Kalgoorlie, not fulfilling expectations. Plant was
warranted to treat 10 tons of concentrates daily, because
it treated experimentally that quantity of crude ore—a
vastly different proposition. Capacity of plant on con-
centrates said to be 3 tons only, so that the estimated cost
of 15s. per ton will be enormously exceeded.
* * * *
Merton’s Reward (W.A.) nob developing at all
well; the more work done the less promising does the
show look. No really defined lode can be found, which
accounts for the fruitless search going on all round
during the last three years. Half the mill is now running
on oxidised ore from the open cut, and very high averages
will doubtless rule for some time to whet John Bull’s
appetite for the Barmecide feast being prepared for him.
* • * •
W.A. July gold yield of 184,2270 z. brings up
the total for the seven months of the year to 1,219,9630 z.,
an increase of 201,5560 z. over the similar period last year.
Island Eureka (W.A.) paid its 15th div. of 3d.
on July 3i, bringing up total to £ll,OOO. It would be
better for co. if divs. were temporarily suspended and
developments pushed further ahead. Tnere is no hope of
this, however, as local holders look to the monthly div.
for beer-money, and with the approach of summer the
thirst question will outweigh all others.
* * * *
®y^ ne y Mint, Kunanalling (W.A.), just
provided the sensational result of 36Goz. from 3cwt. of
stone. Mine is owned by Berliner and Bester, who have
been lucky enough to drop into a very rich patch. Mines
of this sort generally have a short life and a merry one.
however * a Soodtace of highly payable ore is
Hannan’s Oroya (W.A.), after cross-cutting
considerably further east than the point at which it was
anticipated that the continuation of the rich chute woHld
be found at No. 8 level, have started driving north, or
parallel to the lode, if it exists. Same policy was pursued
ere the lode was finally located at No. 6 level. As the
crosscut has merely confirmed the bore test, it looks as if
the chute had either cut off, or is above No. 8 level.

A new reef, carrying rich gold on the surface,
has been located at Edjudina, W.A., and Boulder Hamil-

the breed of their wild cat litter. Some very fine speci-
mens have been exhibited in Kalgoorlie, broken from cap
of reef.
* • * •
“Ida H.” gold-mine, Laverton (W.A.). “A
Miner ” writes : Everything is now in readiness to give
shares in this mine a boom, but . Facts are :At 300 ft.
level ore worth 6to lOdwts. Ore too poor to crush, as
reef averages under 12in. At 200 ft. ore being picked, the
best only going to the mill. At 100 ft. level everything
being rooted out as fast as possible. All development
knocked off to keep expenditure at lowest ebb. The
stamps all weighted to increase crushing capacity. At
present rate of progress, all payable reserves will be
exhausted inside 12 months, out meanwhile mine will
make a big show, and the unloading boom may come off.
Then it will be a case of shut up shop or reconstruct.
An attempt to introduce “ boy ” labor and the whittling
of contracts was met by a strike, and had to be aban-
doned, as a strike would not be conducive to a boom in
shares. Management very partial to foreigners, and
every pay-day some of us have to stand aside to make
room for another square-head."

CLARCON
The story of the Drayton Grange, as dis-
closed by sworn evidence before a Royal Commis-
sion, is about the most horrible The Bulletin can
recall. The transport was a floating Black Hole
of Calcutta, with the agony drawn out over weeks
instead of hours. Filth, squalor, stupidity,
cruelty—which stands out more strongly in the
unspeakably squalid tragedy it is hard to say.
But one thing is certain, that the voyage closes,
with an absolutely fitting incident, the story of a
shameful participation in a shameful enterprise.
The Drayton Grange sums up and epigrammatises
the whole war, so far as Australia is concerned ;
and in the return of these men, pest-ridden,
vermin-infested, reeking with the mire of the
styes in which they were immured, Australia
may see symbolised the just reward of her part in
the war.
So far, the Drayton Grange has claimed 12
victims; this will probably not complete the list,
as there are several still ill of pneumonia. The
Britannic, which brought to the island of the
Chief of all Frozen-meat Patriots, a crowded con-
signment of done-with warriors, has had about an
equal death roll. Why not extend the Royal
Commission on the Drayton Grange to the
whole question of the treatment of Austra-
lian soldiers in S’Afrioa ? There were many things
which occurred in S’Africa about as scan-
dalous as this Drayton Grange business, but
which were more or less hushed up. The
THE GREEN-EYED MONSTER,
“I thought you and Miss Fluffy were great
friends.”
“ We used to be, but we've both been in the same
church choir for nearly three months.”
cables from England, whilst always careful to
record the marmalade with which Lord Roberts
and Mr. Chamberlain spread the Australian
soldier, kept a stolid silence, or went in for hard
lying, about the many occasions on which Austra-
lians were victimised—shot as scapegoats, bullied
by bounder-officers until they were forced to
mutiny, pressed into the most unpleasant and
dangerous work to save pet English regiments
from trouble, and so on. A bit of the truth about
War Office treatment of Australians having floated
to Australia, we might as well have all the facts.
• • • • •
The postponement of the final consideration of
the Federal Defence Bill from the Jingo days of
war to the tainted times of peace, with a Drayton
Grange smelling loudly in the background, will
probably save the Australian people tens of thou-
sands of pounds annually in military expenses.
Will General Hutton now kindly revive his pro-
posal that there should be two armies raised in
Australia—one for local defence, the other to be
available at John Bull’s call for service beyond
the seas !
• ■ • •
“ Swaddy ” writes :
While the high military authorities exhibit a desire to
hush up the Drayton Grange scandal, and aver that the
published reports are “ exaggerated,” those who were on
board know that not half the full facts of the case have
yet come to light. The returning men were simply a
chaotic mob, less amenable to discipline than scrub
cattle, not through their own fault, but the circs, of the
voyage. They were thrust into the space that should
have accommodated half the number, and were able to
get fresh air only at the cost of soaking clothing that
there was no provision for drying. The rule on troopships
that one-third shall always' be on watch duty—giving
those sleeping sufficient room for comfort —had to be dis-
regarded, and, for 24 hours in the day, 1943 men lived and
took their meals in a Black Hole charged with their exhala-
tions and exudations. Added to these were the fumes—no
other word can describe them —from the multitude of
hospitals that had to be erected about the ship, at the
cost of further curtailment of space; and, by the time
the ship reached Albany, the whole vessel absolutely
reeked. Measles has an odor of its own, discernible
when a number of cases are clustered together, and
a blind-folded medical man, taken to the gangway
of the Drayton Grange, would have pronounced
the ship a death-trap without knowing any more.
The frightful atmosphere was aggravated still further by
the fact that the latrines were so situated that their
stench pervaded the surrounding space for several yards,
and as it was impossible to clean up the floors they
became so insanitary as to be a menace to life. The dis-
cipline is alleged to have been good, notwithstanding that
the men openly refused orders and threatened to toss in
a blanket any officer who ventured among them ; and, on
one occasion, an officer of high rank, endeavoring to allay
the discontent of the soldiers, was howled out of
the ’tween decks with blasphemy and contempt.
The true state of the discipline is shown by
the admission that the orders to discontinue spitting on
the upper decks were disregarded, and the plea that if
the men had only taken to their hammocks like profes-
sional soldiers or sailors there would have been no trouble.
If this was the case, why did the officers not see that the
hammocks were used? To anybody who visited the
Drayton Grange ’tween decks the wonder is, not that 10
men died, but that 10 were alive at the end of that three
weeks’ horror. It is significant that in this matter the
conflict of evidence is between Imperial officers on the
one hand and Australian officers on the other.
♦ * * *
Federal Commandant Hutton escaped abad
quarter-of-an-hour by omitting to visit the Dray-
ton Grange when she steamed into Hobson’s Bay.
As the anchor was dropped a launch approached
with several officers in staff uniforms. The
sweltering, fever-stricken swaddies, clustering like
bees on the side, mistakenly identified one figure
as that of the General, and cries of “Stand still,
damn you ! ” were cast at the nearing launch.
The news quickly spread all over the ship, and
the germ-laden soldiers rushed about, wildly
threatening what they would do with the Com-
mandant if he came aboard. Under ordinary
conditions, such talk is talk only, but the Drayton
Grangers were desperate men and utterly un-
disciplined. The uniformed officer turned out to
be Brigadier-Gen. Gordon. Had it been Major-
Gen. Hutton, it is possible that onlookers would
have witnessed the spectacle of a wet and profane
officer being dragged on board a launch with a
boat-hook.

Cards of private entree threaten to damage
the social peace of Governors’ levees. In N.S.W.
the heads of a couple of churches receive the
pasteboards now, but one by one the heads of
others put in claims.
* * * *
A long church service makes the best of
Governors yawn. He “shouldn’t show it,” said
a cleric lately. But that is a mild way of ‘ ‘ showing
it ” compared to Botany Bay’s early way. The
most pathetic letters written in Australia were,
possibly, those of Parson Johnson, the first N.S.W.
chaplain, on the insulting way in which
Governors treated his Sunday services. Johnson
was too long-winded one Sunday, and Lieut. -
Governor Grose struck up the tattoo and
marched his soldiers and convicts out right in the
middle of the sermon.
• * * *
Legislative enactment against the use of
naked lights in any coal-mine is evidently neces-
sary. Mount Kembla disaster has shown that
a “tunnel” mine may be just as unsafe as a deep-
shaft mine. The Mt. Nicholas (Tas.) mine is,
like Kembla, a tunnel in the hill-side, and strongly
suspected of being unsafe. Yet naked lights are
used. Left to itself, Tasmania will never institute
legislation. The inhumanity and incapacity of
State Legislatures is driving the Commonwealth
irresistibly in the direction of Unification.
• • • «
After all, the Kembla disaster will not
mean anything like the drain on the Miners’
Accident Fund that people who haven’t gone into
figures seem to think. Though only 18 months
old, the fund is .£45,000 strong. Widows and
certain other dependents receive Bs. a week; chil-
dren under 14, 2s. 6d. each. Say there are 35 of
the former and 120 of the latter (and the
number is steadily decreasing), the “pay” would
amount to .£l5OB a year ; while £45,000, at 3J per
cent., would bring in £1575. And the fund
is growing all the time. Probably there
hasn’t been a single mine-accident relief fund in
Australia which hasn’t surprised its administrators
by its vitality. It is that way with the Bulli, the
Australian (Creswick) and the Broken Hill South

When a Victorian railway strike appeared
imminent, there straightway crawled from their
lairs and caves those strange animals, “ Pro Bono
Publico,” and “House Father,” and “Constant
Reader,” and “ Head of a Family ” and others, to
suggest, in the press, that the railways ought to be
leased to a syndicate, so that Private Enterprise
might get a’chance to prevent such horrors. A
shapeless idea that the great Australian maritime
strike, and the various shearers’ strikes, and the
huge U.S. railway strikes took place on Govern-
ment property seems to haunt “Pro Bono Pub-
lico’’and “House Father,” and such-like casual
human happenings.
The Long Arm again : One of the two
boys drowned at Parramatta recently, a few
days before visited a cemetery to see a
tombstone erected to his grandfather’s
memory. “ When I die,” he said, “there
will be no need to put my name up
there, as it is the same as grand-
father’s.” A week after his body was
buried beside his namesake.
* • •
A man just back from Maori-
land tells the writer that he met an
old friend, now a local resident, in
a prohibition town. As they sat
together in the parlor of a local
boarding-house my friend be-
thought him that it was a rather
dry town, and remarked the
same. T’other man rang a bell.
“Waiter,” said he, “you’ll find
a couple of bottles of English in
my room. Please bring them here.”
The waiter did as he was told. After
the ale had disappeared the man
with the well-stocked room rang the
bell again. “Waiter,” he said, “you’ll
find a bottle of whisky in my room.”
The waiter did find one, and the two
friends went to bed mellow. Next night the
visitor sat alone and dry, and he thought of his
friend’s room. “I wonder if my room has any-
thing in it like that ?” he thought. He rang the
bell. “ Waiter,” he ventured, “ you will find a
bottle of ale in my room. Please bring it here.”
Then he waited in agony of mind. But the
ale came. He says that when you visit that pro-
hibition town you can make your room respond
to orders for all sorts of drinks.

Apropos of Torrey and Geil’s craze about

* * * *
N.S.W. Arbitration Act does not apply to
domestic servants—which is reasonable enough,
for the female who drops the dishes in the hall is
so nearly extinct that she may be considered a
non-existent quantity. But the restaurant-keepers
of Sydney claim that their employees are domestic
servants, and therefore outside the Act, 'which is
quite an unlooked-for slab of impudence. When
did the shop where the sixpenny chop swims un-
naturally in cheap grease become a home, and
why should the person who sells cooked food be
more domestic than the one who sells it raw ?
Also why should food consumed on another per-
son’s premises be more domestic than ginger-beer
consumed on another person’s premises—say over
the counter of Julius Dago, fruit-dealer? As
a rule the tired girl who tells you that chops are
“off,” but that there is still corned beef to
be had, doesn’t even live on the premises.
Then the status of the barmaid who supplies
counter-lunch, and the theatre manager who
allows pea-nuts to be supplied and consumed on
the premises while he is on the stage playing
“Hamlet,” comes into question. Also, there is
the domestic servant who runs the midnight pie-
stall, which is a restaurant on wheels ; likewise
the person who cuts down trees for starving cattle
to eat, and who is a precarious restaurant for
cows, and is mostly named Bill. He may be a
domestic servant, but it is better to call him one
by telegram.

Recent affiliation cases in Sydney courts
seem to demand that the young woman should,
before going astray, fortify herself with a properly-
attested statement as to the young man’s income.
Affiliation thrift should be as good as any other
thrift.
■ » * *
At Sydney Central Police Court Stipen-
diary Donaldson laid down the law, as understood
by him, in an affiliation case as follows :
I shall make an order, but she seems to have contri-
buted so much to her own undoing that I shall make
only a small order. „.
Defendant was ordered to pay ss. a week and £llos. 6d.
costs.
A wrongly-interpreted law. The weaker the
woman, within certain limits, the greater the
responsibility of the man.

LOVE-WAYS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Which Love-way’s best, you bid me prove :
The man’s love, or the woman’s love ?
The woman gives her heart—her all;
Man —crumbs that from his table fall.
Because our love leaps hot and fast
Proves not your slow love will outlast!
A woman loves for all life long—
The man’s love dieth with the song . . .
The man'B love pauseth to take breath ;
While woman loves till Death—till death!

The Government of Icicle Irvine has
“mitigated ” the sentence of death passed lately
on a Gippsland youth into 15 years’ gaol and two
floggings. This “mercy” appears to have been
extended to the culprit—a lad of 17 —on the
ground that he was of weak intellect ; that, at any
rate, was the jury’s recommendation. The lash,
as a cure for weak intellect ? If it could only be
tried on an occasional Cabinet Minister !

Concerning the latest Melbourne sensa-
tion :
Just as Melbourne was settling down to be interested
In Thos. Bent’s efforts to get up a railway strike, there
came news of the Dandenong tragedy, and the report
that the murdered man’s wife (arrested on sus-
Sicion of complicity) was a sister of Thekla Regina
'ubberke, the masterful, handsome “heroine” of the
Boot-box tragedy. Melbourne instantly dropped
politics and centred all its interest on Dandenong.
The widow, Selina Sangal, and her fellow-accused and
alleged paramour—an ex-employee named August Tisler
—both began making statements at an early stage. So
far the yarn seems to be that someone entered the San-
gals’ bedroom late at night; that the wife got up and
went into the children’s bedroom, and Sangal was then
violently assaulted. He struggled for a space, then called
for a drink of water. This his wife took him, then
retired, and he was battered some more. Meanwhile
two “ hands ” were sleeping in a hut 20 yards away, but
no one made any attempt to call them. Also, it is stated
that Sangal’s son, a lad of 11, awoke, and wished to see
why his father called out, but his mother prevented him,
••lest he should get hurt "—which, considering all things,
was quite likely. Mrs. S. made no mention of these

desired the police to come and investigate her well, as
she thought her old man had committed suicide down it.
He was down it, right enough, but as his throat was cut
and his head much battered, it didn’t look like a suicide,
Tisler says that at the time of the murder he was at a
certain place in Melbourne, but the man whom thq police
found there put the matter otherwise ; he said that Tisler
asked him to say that he (Tisler) was there, which was
quite a different thing. Tisler seems to have even written
down a full statement of the story he wanted his Melb.
friend to tell, and the police now hold the document,

whirling narrative of battle, slaughter, and sudden
obliteration on the veldt. Last of all, an alleged Spaniard
has come forward to swear that he was offered various
sums to murder the late Sangal, but that his request for
some money in advance was refused. The other party
reckoned that the transaction should be caBh on delivery
of corpse. It is a long, sordid, gruesome series of allega-
tions—the most gruesome being that the loving wife took
her partially-murdered husband a drink and then retired
till the homicide should be finished. That item deserves
to be preserved in spirits and put in the Chamber of
Horrors.
• * * «
About the only notable “ Coronation ”
function in Melbourne was Mayor Gillot’s dis-
tribution of 10,000 medals to the city children.
Three unfortunate kids —duly coached by some
sycophantic worm—reciprocated with an awful
effusion setting forth: “ May it please your
Worship, In the name of the boys and girls of
Melbourne we wish to thank you with our hearts
for the entertainment you gave us on 27th June.
We were very sorry that His Majesty, King
Edward VII., was unable to be crowned, etc. We
shall try in return for your generosity to be good
citizens of Melbourne and the British Empire, and
pray that God may bless Lady Gillott and your-
self with long life and happiness.” Several of the
medals were subsequently found in an adjacent
penny-in-the-slot machine, which badly fails to
corroborate the greasy effusion above quoted.

• • * •
Holy Adelaide has just sent “ up ” for two
years’ hard labor another saint. In this case, aa
in others, the young man went to church every
Sunday, spoke with great earnestness to his Sun-
day-school class of the everlasting hell in store for
those who smiled or went fishing on the Lord’s day,
and flushed rosy-red at the very sight of a Flossie.
And because he did these things his unsophisti-
cated employers let him oollect the cash and lock
the safe by himself till the yearly audit, when over
£2OO was missing. The pious young man allowed
that he took the cash, but whether it went in
Flossies or fishing-taokle or prayer-books didn’t
transpire.
* * * •
The See Government proposes to place
female nurses in the male wards at Parramatta
Hospital for the Insane. This does not meet
with the approval of the male or female attend-
ants. Some nurses will resign rather than under-
take the new duties. In many cases patients have
to be forcibly bathed by the attendants, and it is
hardly women’s work to hold a naked madman
under a bath. A tussle with a nude maniac is
fairly common in the wards at night; and it
doesn’t seem reasonable to call upon women to
undertake that responsibility.

• * * *
Casual point of interest in that Ade-
laide divorce-case is the statement of one of
the man-servants that he and another man-servant
spoke very seriously to their mistress about her
alleged misconduct with the preacher man, and
warned her to desist for the sake of herself and her
family and morality in general. Judging by the
evidence, the lady was first subjected to severe
reproof by the gardener in the kitchen, and then
admonished by the coachman ; and, though it
doesn’t appear for certain that she was also spoken
to as an erring sister by the cook, and subjected to
contumely at the mouth of the housemaid, these
things may also have happened. It was a luxurious
household, where the lady’s allowance ran to some
thousands a year; yet when she was semi-publicly
reprimanded by her own menials in the kitchen,
beside the slush-bucket and adjacent to the rolling-
pin, it was a striking evidence that wealth can’t
purchase happiness.

* * * *
Apparently more or less apropos of the
newest Victorian sensation and of certain things
connected therewith:—
Seems to be about time that the State Governments
published some statistics showing what proportion of the
murder, manslaughter and mysterious tragedy business
in this country is associated with people of distinctly
non-British parentage. More than a fair share of the
name 3 that become public through gruesome newspaper
stories, coroners’ inquests, and trials for murder are, in
an English way of speaking, foreign names. Australia
needs all the light it can get upon the subject of white
aliens and their tendency to increase and multiply and
furnish the local press with congenial cross-headings. An
inquiry into the importation of murderous proclivities
from Europe would give a new turn to A.N.A. sentiment.
• • •
The two fresh outbreaks of plague, one at
Brisbane, t’other at Newcastle (N.S.W.), call
forcibly to notice the necessity of continuing pre-
cautions against the disease even after it seems
to have entirely disappeared. With regard to the
Newcastle outbreak, the lasting wonder of those
who know the coaly city is that the present is but
the second case it has produced so far. The town
itself is one long, water-side street, and a
more or less successful attempt to build on the
almost perpendicular sides of a hill. Most of the
town is right on the water-frontage, and the
wharves are about the cosiest place a plague-rat
could ask for.
