Even amid the howl for retrenchment at
the railway worker’s expense to block fresh
direct taxation, the Vic. dailies cannot altogether
prevent such items as this from creeping into
their country news:
BROADFORD, Monday.
Much indignation is felt at the sweating carried, on at
the local railway station, where the hours are about
thirteen per day per man. Customers of the department
also complain that they do not receive the attention they
deserve, and attribute this to the fact that the staff has
been reduced.
Present writer, who considers himself a very hard-
worked man, wouldn’t change places for much
money with some of the up-country railway
servants he has met. He has particular recollec-
tions of a G-ippsland station-master-booking-clerk-
telegraphist-porter- sh unter - and - everything - com-
bined, who was deprived, about a year ago, of the
assistance of a night officer, and only got him back
by reason of falling ill from overwork at the end
of two months. During that period he had to get
up before seven to take the “staff” from the
driver of the early goods train, remained at his
post all day in his capacity of township post-
master, and finished up just about midnight by
seeing another goods-train off the premises. In
addition to his duties as stationmaster and
postmaster, he was supposed to run telegrams
across the township to any recipients who lived
handy, and likewise performed duties con-
nected with old age pensions, dog-registration and
similar matters. And now, this versatile
individual is to have a chip knocked off his £3 a
week to preserve the pockets of under-rated shire-
property owners from assault.
• * • *
Apropos that N.S.W. Abolition-of-Capital-
Punishment Bill. A doctor who has attended
many hangings tells The Bulletin that the idea
of killing by that method (to shed no blood) is not
guite technically correct. Blood is shed, but
internally; the victim is first rendered uncon-
scious by an effusion of blood on the brain (just
as though he had been sand-bagged), and is then
killed by strangulation. A hanged man is often
alive lOmin. after the drop, but generally (so far
as can be ascertained) suffers no pain.
The importance of hanging, as a Bulwark to the
Bible and the Constitution, in John Bull’s mind,
is shown by the fact that, in the Crown Colony of
British Gruiana, the barrister who defends a penni-
less person charged with a capital crime is only
paid 24d01. by the Crown ; but the hangman, for
hanging the accused after his conviction, gets
25d01., with the loan of a black suit, a hat, and a
pair of spectacles!
Writes “ C.H.L.”
Re “ crumbling houses in Sydney’s suburbs” (B. 2/8/’O2).
There are many cottages in the suburbs built with only
4£in. brick walls, and with foundations that simply beggar
description. About a dozen years ago, present writer,
then himself in the building-line, saw,' in Balmain, some
workmen, just after knock-off time on a Saturday, leaning
against the outer face of a chimney-stack in the wall of a
small building, which had evidently been finished only
that very day. On entering into conversation with the
men, they told me that the contractor had gone to the
proprietor to draw the balance of the money, and that
they were meanwhile holding the blanky thing up until
he returned with funds to pay their wages.

He woke from dreams, and kicked the Basket o’er
That held a dozen cosmic Eggs, alas I
A dozen universes smashed like glass—
The Landlord thrust him from the Tavern Door.
V. J. DALET.
To Lydia
For The Bulletin
A T this far lonely village on the sea,
Waiting until some marriageable purse
Prostrates himself before your dainty skirt
For better or for worse,
You deign, fair Lydia, to flirt with me ;
And I am grateful ’tis with me you flirt.
You praise my verses with a kindling eye,
You have a glad, quick smile for all my fancies ;
Pansies I praised to you, and so, since then,
N.S.W.
You have worn pansies ;
And yet, dear, if you treat me tenderly
’Tit but that there are here no other men.
I ask not, Lydia, that you should be mine,
I could not for one week afford to dress you !
Vm grateful for your gift of passing smiles,
And so God bless you :
A month of water you have made all wine
With the vjhite magic of your maiden wiles.

A WOMAN'S LETTER.
Sydney, August 19, 1902.
My Dear Arini.—
From two “visitations of God” —drought and
the Mt. Kembla disaster—our attention is dis-
tracted by a third horror that calls for a visitation
of Justice on someone, or several. Out of the
depths of the Drayton Grange infamy comes the
exceeding bitter cry, “ Sad Death of a Returning
Trooper”; the dailies are keeping the line in
type. The mother of Trooper Burke-Thompson,
who died of pneumonia a few hours after the arri-
val of the steamer* in Melbourne, lies seriously ill
in Sydney, where she had come in the expectation
of meeting her son. Another of her sons was
buried less than a month ago, and one 18 months
since. The dead trooper was the eldest
son of the late James Thompson, of Mt.
Emu, Tarbrax, and Fairlight stations,
Queensland, and he was said to be the
first white male child born in Burke, N.Q., where
his father was an early pioneer. A funeral “with
full military honors ” was given his body—when
it was dead. Alive, it couldn’t secure as much
honor as a hor3e—which isn’t overcrowded on a
transport, and is “groomed every morning,”
according to the army regulations.
Lady Rawson opened the Babies’ Home, at
Newtown, last week, in the presence of 28 babies
(who sang “Three Blind Mice’’and “Pop Goes
the Weasel ”), a group of clergymen, and doctors,
and. philanthropists. Babies’ Home is the out-
come of a creche , founded by a few ladies, nearly
14 years ago, in Macquarie-st. In its present
state, there is only accommodation for 30 children,
but, when the funds allow, it will take in about 20
more. After Dr. Walter Spencer’s speechlet,
Lady Rawson must be fully impressed with
the faot that a neglected child is a menace
to the State. He also told her, in
language that wouldn’t bring a blush to
the cheek of the Blessed Damozel herself, that
the society of which the Home is an offshoot
chases the male parent who tries to escape the
affiliation order, and compels him (when it can
catch him) to contribute to the support of the
babe ; also, that mothers are expected to contri-
bute out of “theirscanty earnings” towards the
same small object.
Their State Exes., Mis 3 Rawson, an<T Capt.
Watt, came to the Town Hall on Saturday to hear
Mr. Alfred Hill’s Maori maiden, Hinemoa, tell
her love to the young chief, who played the flute
like Lemmone, and wore a slab of greenstone
in his nose, the huia feather in his hair, and a red
flax mat on his tattooed front. Hinemoa does
the running, or swimming rather, as thoroughly
and gracefully as her cultured prototype, Shake-
speare’s Rosalind. Libretto of “Hinemoa” was
written by that disconsolate half genius, Arthur
Adams, who came amongst us a few years
ago—a blue devil seated in either fine
dark eye—and later, went hence to San Toy
land. Now he is in London. Music of Hine-
moa is captivating in numerous places. The
orchestral number, “Lake Music,” deserves the
most delicate of praise adjectives. Alfred Hill
has enticed the voices of the raupo, and the wind
whispering through the taua trees, into his score.
But the conducting of this melodious bantling of
his must have been anguish. He looked more
spook-like than ever after the closing Maori
maidens’ and braves’ chorus.
Soc. of Artists’ show of students’ work, mostly
in black and white, comprises work of some 50
students, after some six months’ study. Mr.
Bradbury, one of the proprietors of London
Punch, who is in Sydney at present, was looking
round and pounced upon two sets of studies
by girls named Derbyshire and Lovett, and said
that the work was some of the best student effort
he had ever seen. The former girl is a Russian by
birth, latter a Tasmanian. Miss Derbyshire
resides with a very young aunt, and a very pretty
one, whom she has used as a model in many
phases of attire. The Amalgamated societies will
hold their exhibition on Sept. 8, in the old
society’s rooms. It is said they are keeping to the
premises, though they are badly lighted, for
sentimental reasons. The sentiment is probably a
keen desire to triumph over “the other animal.”
There was so much of the crusty old solitary in
each of the art. societies which have just united
that the manage is rather suggestive of the happy
home of two strictly-teetotal old bachelors.
Queen Alexandra has, if London and M.L.
papers speak with authority, succeeded to her late
mother-in-law’s Sydney-conferred title of “Pre-
eminent in Grief.” An English and M.L. daily
of almost the same dates have had leaders on the
Queen Consort’s crown of sorrows—counting the
thorns thereof with loyal care and watering
each with a tear. Though the Queen, it seems,
has had an “idyllic married life ” and has never
had a tear or even a sigh wrung from her by Ned
staying out late or making eyes at another
cat, she had an attack of acute rheumatism when
she was 22. She got better of that (Sydney brand
of rheumatism is like the Biblical poor). After
that first woe came the deaths of her elder son,
of the Duchess of Teck, of Mr. Gladstone (“to
whom she was greatly attached ”), her mother and
mother-in-law; and so she is a “Queen of
Sorrows.” Yet all these, save the poor young
Duke of Clarence, had a pretty long life-innings.
Gladstone, the late Queen Victoria, and the Queen
of Denmark were over 80. The D. of Teck over
60. What one of us can’t name friends, children,
parents, lovers lost ?
The dead are very many, the living few.
There’s a crawful craze to place monarchs on an
imaginary lonely height, made an island by
tears of their own shedding. The crawl papers
seem to like to persuade the people who have to
do the paying into the belief that the monarchical
life is infinitely more sorrowful than any other,
despite its gilding.
A Brisbane correspondent points out that her
city has the advantage of Sydney, or any other
city in Australia, in at least one particular—that
of garden breakfasts and lunches. These meals
used to be cooked by a first-class chef, who had
Eaid to the public garden trustees £24 per year for
is stand. Trustees must have had their eyes
opened to the value of the concession, for this
year’s accepted tender is £4OO. The retiring chef
has leased the top of the Courier buildings, where
he will plant a roof-garden and continue his
admirable cooking and service. Now, why doesn’t
someone here profit by Brisbane’s example : Why
can’t we have garden meals in Sydney ? Hyde
Park at present is a loafing ground for dead-beats
and a place wherein the sandbagger lurks. If the
railings were removed, the human, canine and
other rubbish swept out and flowers planted, tea
and luncheon rooms started, the place would be
beautiful and useful. Now is the time for some-
one to deputationise the park trustees, that is
if anyone can find where they are. They are
probably lying low, leaving the park to become as
useless as possible, so that presently it may be
grabbed for a rail way-station or other public ugli-
ness.
Twenty-five guineas for a man’s hat! Ex-editor
Brient, of Sydney D.T., writing from London,
mentions hat and price in astonishment. “ You
may see as many straw hats in Oxford-st. and the
Strand as in George and King-sts.” But this
simpler, more rational clothing is, for the benefit
of the London Johnny, made in very expensive
qualities. The favorite hat for both men and
women is the Homburg-shaped Panama, sold for
the common herd at from half-a-guinea to two
guineas, but for the swelled head of the real Lon-
don Johnny (who will have none of these bargain-
counter prices pressing on his exquisite brain) it is
geared up to 25 guineas.
A mining man who recently went to London
has been confirmed in his belief that “know-
ledge by suffering entereth.” He drew the
favorite for the Derby in a sweep, and a man
offered him £7O for his chance. He declined and
the horse didn’t even get a place. He went out
to the Derby in new clothes with his son, a pocket-
ful of money, and several tips, which he intended
to back. On the way he was robbed of all his
coin, and so he could not back his fancies, all of
which won. Then the rain came and he and his
son had their clothes spoilt because they hadn’t
the price of a cab. Later he went to Paris
to look at the Grand Prix with two other
men, and one of the party was robbed of
a valuable pearl pin. They caught the thief
(but after he had passed the pin to a
confederate, who escaped) and the robber pulled
out a swag of gold and' offered £5O for
his liberty and the pin, and the Englishmen
agreed, and were just taking over the money when
a French somebody in police uniform came up and
told his men to take them in charge for com-
pounding a felony. And they had a lot of trouble
in proving that they were only innocents abroad.
Two London correspondents on Regina Nagel:
No. 1—“ Miss Nagel’s London concert was almost
ignored by the press. The Sunday Sun said that
her merits as a vocalist did not justify the
enthusiasm with which she was received, for her
voice was uneven and lacked charm. It recom-
mended study.” No. 2—“ Mr. W. Yert, the suc-
cessful London entrepeneur, has stated that at
Miss Nagel’s recent concert in St. James’s Hall
there was more money taken at the doors than at
any of the debutantes’ concerts this season. Miss
Nagel is at present only accepting a few of the
many engagements offered her. The song which
is received with the greatest favor among those
which Miss Nagel is now singing is ‘The Land

After the supper at a recent Friendly Society’s
annual ball the M.C. requested the company to
suspend the toast-list as the district secretary had
just died—would the company go back to the ball-
room and resume dancing ?
Miss Vida Goldstein, a little plumper, a little
more attractive, and with a hint at an American
accent, was met on her return (per Ventura) by
an elated group of woman-suffragists and the
joyous news—“We have got IT!” They then
caught her up in a chariot and whirled off to Miss
Rose Scott’s residence. The pretty emancipator
is speaking this week in Sydney on her American
Lesson, learned at the international Women’s
Suffrage Conference, held in Washington. She
attended and addressed scores of meetings, inter-
viewed the President, who is a suffragist, and was
charmed by hint, noted the good work being done,
especially in an aesthetic direction, by women’s
leagues in the poorer parts of New York and
Chicago. In reply to an interviewer’s question
about the vote just granted her sex, Miss G. said :
‘ ‘ What the ballot-box cannot do we still hope to
accomplish by subtler methods.” Now, that
“ subtler methods ”is very significant. Same old
chiffons, same old sighs, same old curl in the
middle of your forehead, open-work stockings and
Louis shoes, same old dimple and glance and
engaging moistening of the lips ?
Mary Chatteris, the white, plump, petite ingenue
with the funny little marmot profile, brought out
by the Broughs a year or more ago, was married
on Thursday to Mr. W. Allen, son of Mr. W. B.
Allen, of Waverley, and the pair left on Saturday
for London. The Allen family have a soap-factory
and are well-off.
Captain Bosanquet, R.N., who died last week,
near Sydney, was one of the most popular men
that ever sighted a cannon or handled a sub-
marine missile. He was commander of the N.S. W.
Naval Forces and staff officer in charge of torpe-
does. His widow and four sons survive him.
Sydney’s Mayoress, Mrs. T. Hughes, will be At
Home to her friends in the Town Hall this (Thurs-
day) afternoon. In her rooms at the Hall, mind
you ; so, if even a fourth of the people in Sydney
roll up as they did at her last “ At Home,” there
won’t be enough room, or tea, or pie.
Writes my Brisbane correspondent:—
“ The Lady Lamington (women's) Hospital com-
mittee has had the most successful ball of the
season. The ladies recognise that children of
a larger growth have a passion for dressing up.
Their first poster ball made them financially
sound, and the present ‘ sheet and pillowslip ’
function gives them a substantial building nest-
egg. Even Vice-royalty donned the classic cover-
ing.' In their robes of purity, some looked like
Druids, some like Dabee Singh and other fish-
vendors, while others were as if arrayed in the
plainest of bedroom garments ready for ‘ bye-bye.’
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ There was quite a breeziness about the Coro-
nation festivity at ‘Stonington’ on Monday night,
due to the sweet smiles that Gov. Clarke and Lady
Silverlocks and their ewe lamb threw in with every
hand-shake. Meeting them you really think that
the dearest wish and highest gratification of their
hearts has been achieved by your particular
presence ; and that is the secret of ‘ charm ’—the
secret that makes a ladies’ doctor or a dentist
successful. Things swung right from the start,
the State fossils taking on a caperish air and
waxing as facetious as juveniles. ‘ What a bright
party ! ’ murmured a much-medalled Consul to
the V.lt. host; ‘We are quite brilliant without
the Federal element.’ ‘ Ah,’ was the perky reply,
‘ we must see that the State holds up its little
head,’ and Gov. Clarke gave an upward jerk to
his own, like a dear little cock-sparrow thinking
over a triumph ; and one or two near neighbors
thought how very determined was His Nibs to
show his State how to do it. Such a perky,
pleased, amiable little Governor, with a frank air
for all his little conceits !
“ Lady Clarke was in a red silk outfit, draped
with black lace, cherry-colored chiffon ridges on
the bodice, and diamonds everywhere. Miss
Clarke was pretty and unaffected in black, dis-
engloomed by a little turquoise. She converses ;
therefore old politicians and grave-looking officials
with rows of decorations on their braided coats
haunt her side. As one of them put it the other
night, ‘ Miss Clarke does not try to say things
admirably, she tries only to say them plainly.’
He had been reading Landor. Herr Carl Pin-
schoff, sporting the brightest red coa, and the
chastest white continuations in the gathering,
shook off his grand, gruff consequential air, and
talked to the leading spinster like an uncle.
Madame, his wife, was in the front row of the
frockers, and showed herself a favorite at ‘ Ston-
ington.’ The Clarkes have a passion for music.
“ Standing out in that State party, as a good
deed in a naughty world, was the superb figure of
Mrs. Harry Payne. She stands up to her inches,
and rears her head in a way that suggests horse-
manship. Appropriately enough, she boasts one of
the best mounts of the hunting-field. Her dress
was a smoke-grey satin voluminously trained,
with sunset-red ridges rising above the cloudy
background. Diamonds flashed about the neck,
shoulders and hair. Her husband walked beside
this vision, full of diffident pride, humbly con-
scious of her outshining all the sex that night.
For a politician, he is unusually bashful. Two
new arrivals in this State were Mrs. W. Wallace
(a broad, dignified, well-set-up dame wearing a
black gown with spangled net-veiling and occa-
sional white lace ornaments with diamond out-
posts), and Mrs. Gordon (a fair, placid, composed
lady, with fine shoulders, in cream overlaid with
ivory-tinted lace). The latter’s military spouse
again strutted his peacock hour. A swashbuck-
ling Athos is this Brigadier-General Maria
Gordon, who has come to lick the State militia
into shape.
“Where do all the decorations, orders and
medals come from that the official world wears ?
On the ‘Stonington’ occasion, almost every
common or garden individual breasted a couple.
Even a politician who has a corner in chiffon,
sported a big, bright, beautiful star. The most
microscopic of the lot was a tiny cross—only the
strong-sighted could detect it—pinned on the coat
of the Italian Consul. The most courtly figure of
the knee-breeched order was old Sir Henry Cuth-
bert, tall, pinlt-cheeked, whose velvet trappings,
rich white lace frills, gold braidings and silver
locks were entirely suitable. The average man
looks like an ordinary goat in court-trappings;
the extra-average man, like a dignified regimental
goat.
“A pathetic sight at this Gov’ment House
party was the tall figure of Minister M‘Kenzie
being led from room to room, and from, group to
group, by his wife. The stranger cannot tell that
the keen, intelligent face has sightless eyes, and
to hear his bright, brisk, cheerful and intellectual
conversation no one would credit that the speaker
is totally blind. There was not a cheerier man
in the gathering, yet his hand had to be directed
to that held out to him, and his steps guided ever
so carefully.
“One saw with half an eye, at the Tennyson
Coronation spree, what assiduous court Better-
Not Wally paid the Federal Powers. He went
and gathered in Lady Fysh to the supper-table,
collected certain other Senate dames, dallied at
the buffet with William Lyne, laughed to his
ears and round the back of them at one of the
sprightly Deakin’s jokes, and conveyed the im-
pression of great fondness for all the Federal
party. A rather fat Federal billet is said to be
waiting for Wally, who will fill it to perfection.
“ An Australian tells me, this mail, of a dinner
at the Vagabond Club, London, which Dick
Seddon spoilt with his swelled head and swelled
speeches. Conan Doyle was in the chair, and
among the pen-people there was the author of
‘My Japanese Wife.’ The dinner was excellent,
only three toasts listed, and the music promised
to be first-class. Everything prepared one for a
brilliantly good time. But they’d reckoned with-
out their guest. In reply to the toast of his health,
Mr. Seddon got on the hustings and bellowed and
roared heroics and gibberish for three-quarters of
an hour. Then, in reply to the toast of ‘ Com-
merce, etc.,’ proposed by Lord Charles Beresford,
Seddon got on the stump again for another eter-
nity. Londoners marvel at the sublime egotism
of the man who could thus let loose his election
eloquence on a ‘ booky ’ gathering. One of those
present put it this way :
This man from the Antipodes
Has sure a swollen head on ;
He talked of lands beyond the seas
And said, and said, and Seddon 1
He talked till 11 o’clock to a gradually-emptying
room, and the concert was lost. He looked
fatigued at the end, but evidently enjoyed himself
immensely.
• °f Woman’s inhumanity to
smirched sisters and make the most of scratches,
inflicted by Hospital Catmittees, on the feelings
of unmarried inmates. But ‘ when lovely woman
stoops to folly, she’s apt to find brother man, too,
none too willing ‘ her guilt to cover,’ provided, of
course, he is not personally interested. This
really happened in a local hospital, managed
wholly by men. A young student questioned a
Sl r an d noted down her answers. Next,
a full-blown doctor went the rounds of the ward,
with a following of boy-Sawbones, one of them the
little diagnoser, with a note-book. The young-
ster read out his jottings. ‘Did you believe her
when she said that ? ’ chuckled the Senior. ‘ Yes.’
Why, you Infant, she’s been enciente (only he
put it more brutally) four months ! ’ guffawed the
kind physician. Nearly everyone in the ward
overheard and those who didn’t were told by the
others, so that the woman without a wedding ring
soon got to Coventry. Even the nurse snapped
away a chair with a vicious jerk, when the sick
hussy took the liberty of propping her bad leg
thereon.
“‘Sweet Nell,’gives generous glimpes of her
gorgeous hose in the second act, when she sits
touching up the eyebrow of Dryden’s Muse.
Sitting at the spindle-legged table in front of the
audience. Mistress Gwynne first allows a glimpse
of her Cinderella shoes of turquoise shade with
jewelled buckles. Then she nurses one leg, while
the other, dangling, kicks the audience and fixes
its vision. It is such a poetical limb. Dryden
might have written a finer sonnet on it than he
did on the eyebrow. The stocking? Oh, yes,
wonderfully woven and spangled, or embroidered
or hand-painted, but after all a mere covering.
“Petticoats and hose now vie with chiffons in
the social cake walk. Fancy under-skirt frills,
and open-work novelties and embroideries about
the hose d la, ‘ Nell Gwynne,’ were sported by all
the flash promenaders at Flemington. Dress
upholsterings were lost to sight, and all the grey
clouds were rolled up for the sake of their silver
linings. No wonder the pedicurist flourishes.
Women now pay more attention to their Trilbies
than to their teeth. And there is a fortune await-
ing the toilet-saloon that can treat knees effec-
tively—-make ’em pretty and graceful, take out all
the knobs, and accentuate the dimples. The time
is rapidly coming when Mrs. Flash’s knee-dimples
will be noticed in the papers.
“ The Maddens are enthusiastically celebrating
the recent arrival of the Vallange heir. They
are old-fashioned enough to rejoice at family addi-
tions, while so many Sassiety folk tilt up noses at
such plebeian events.
“Men have taken alarm at their wives’ visits to
the sibyls of the city. There is, in fact, much
cause for it. The mischief done by these people
is very great, not alone in encouraging supersti-
tious folly but in promoting criminal conduct.
And one of the chief things that the average
woman wants to know when she consults a fortune-
teller is, how long her husband is going to live.
Every woman is at heart a widow.
“How is it that the friends and admirers of
Carrie Moore do not bother about handing her up
floral trophies ? It seems years since she had to
reach forth her hand for even one of these offer-
ings. But she manages to look (such is her art)
perfectly happy as the other girls of the co. scoop
in the crumbs from the rich man’s table.
“ Some Melbourne people have been scared by
receiving solicitors’ letters on behalf of a musical
lady who, after her marriage with a rather well-to-
do gentleman, found that old pupils were quietly
ignoring her claims for a settlement of outstanding
accounts. A first gentle reminder from the bride
failed to bring in the guineas., A second re-
minder, kindly but insistent,"' met with no re-
sponse, and at last the lady handed her little list
of debtors to a solicitor. The effect was magical.
Next morning’s post brought a sheaf of apologies
and cheques from the laggards, with humbie re-
quests from the writers to say no more about it.
“Maie Saqui, the sparkling little skirt-dancer of
many years ago, has developed of late. A
recently-returned globe-trotter describes her as
quite massive, but one of the most stunning of
Gaiety Girls. The same trotter reports a falling-
off in the Maesmore Morris charms. She is no
longer the aesthetic creature of grace, looking as
if she subsisted on fresh air and flowers, but is
plumper than of yore. Londoners like her, and
her portraits are in prominent display all over the
Big Smoke. She puts up at the Grosvenor, so
things look prosperous enough.
“ A learned professor of Melb. University told
a dinner-party, the other night, while the discus
sion of Women’s Rights was under we’igh, that
the female in bloomers and knickers was fore-
shadowed when Omphale donned the skin covering
of Hercules and carried his club. After that the
subject was not pursued seriously.

Obit.: T. M. Sloinan, aged 91 years. He
arrived in N.S.W. in 1833, and in 1835 went to
Bathurst, where he resided up to the time of his
death. Think of a man existing in Bathurst for
67 years!
Among the celebrities that the Australian
XI. met when they were guests of Lord Warwick
was Marie Corelli, who takes a deep interest in
the game, and knows all about the abilities of Dar-
ling, Trumper and Trumble.
The only objection taken to the Westralian
Govt.’s appointment of a new boss-detective from
Scotland Yard is that Mr. Mellish, the chosen
one, is past his prime—he will not see 53 anymore.
Mellish’s beat has been the East End.
Opinions are converging to one astronomer
and one meteorologist for the Commonwealth.
One of each should certainly suffice, but vexation,
heart-burning and scientific riots crowd round the
selection and the residences of the officers.
Whether or not Selina Sangal is guiltily
implicated in the Dandenong tragedy, the repro-
duction in print of misdeeds of her sister,
Thekla Dubberke, at the present stage is a crime.
It is trying the woman in a blood-red atmosphere.

Premier Irvine (Vic.), glorying over the
recovery of King Edward, said that H.M.’s
“marvellous” restoration to health was due to
“his indomitable courage.” That’s the best of
being a king—people even think better of you for
getting well. Only monarchs get credit for their
improvements in this way.

According to the report of Melb. Herald
editor Reay, Bigjohn Forrest surpassed himself in
his violent Imperialism, at the London Conference.
Reay states that the Emperor of the West was
declaring that the colonies should support the
defence of the Empire, and added, “I care not a
snap of the fingers for the opinions of Canada or
Australia.” If this is true, Bigjohn should be
given an opportunity of showing his contempt for
his own country when he returns.
Mr. Balfour, England’s new Prime Mini-
ster, owns a big estate in Wairarapa (Maoriland),
and the local paper suggests that something should
be done to compel its high-placed owner to cut it
up for closer settlement. The property is sur-
rounded by enterprising and industrious settlers,
whose efforts to improve their own farms result
in greatly enhanced value to the idle Balfour
estate. If the M.L. land-tax were not a mere
fleabite, such a state of affairs would be impossible.

Herbert Henty. I know not how far the state-

One of Melbourne’s best-known city swag-
men is a German mason, who occasionally gets
arrested “on the vag.” While he was doing one
stretcli in Melbourne gaol, he was ordered to re-
move the initial “ W.” over the grave of murderer
Deeming (who was hanged under the name of
Williams) and substitute a “D.” Next day,
when he came along the wall where the executed
criminals are buried, the Governor found that
everyone of them, right back to the fifties, had a
“D” after the initials previously sculptured.
Demanding an explanation from the mason, the
latter looked up cheerily and remarked—“Vy,
dem all dead, ain’t it ?”

Healey, of Wangaratta (Vic.), in 1876. Mr. Sutherland,
surveyor, reported to the police that certain articles had
been stolen from his camp at Tagumin. Sergt. Foley and
Const. Townsend, suspecting a man named Turner,
tracked him towards Narandera. At the Belmore Arms,
Ganmain, they were informed that a strange man had put
up there. As Foley had an ordinary overcoat over his
uniform, and Townsend was in plain clothes, it was only
natural that the “ strange man ” (Father Healey) mistook
them for criminals and “ drew a bead ” when they bailed
him up. Result was that Foley mortally wounded him.
Father Healey died a few days after at Wagga hospital.
No one thought it worth while to question him, and a
verdict of justifiable homicide was duly returned at the
inquest on the body of the supposed John Turner. It
was only later on that the mistake leaked out. Foley
and Townsend were tried for manslaughter and acquitted.
Foley, now dead, afterwards kept a pub. for some years
at Narandera.
Adelaide, a handful of people with 900,000
square miles of country to rest upon, talks about
a meat-famine and Govt, help to bring stock from
Maoriland. There are not many cattle, and what
there are, it is now seen, are in the hands of the
Kidmans, who also pretty well manage the Wes-
tralian meat-market. A remarkable family are
these Kidmans, a big brotherhood of them, none
of them old yet, but all experienced, hard-
grafting, keen-bargaining cattle-men. They
started at Kapunda (S.A.), wandered over into
N.S. W. still droving and grazing and butchering ;
made a pot out of feeding Broken Hill; took up
bit by bit hundreds of thousands of acres of
country in Queensland, N.S.W., and S. Aus. ;
fingered western mail contracts so long as there
was anything in them ; and early scented the
possibility of repeating on a bigger scale in the
West what they had done on the Barrier. In
many respects they are following Tyson methods
—for one thing, like him, they practically make
the bank keep their books. But, unlike the
Queenslander, they are inclined to “play up” a
bit of their makings on the turf.
Rev. Horatio Gilbert Parker, one time of
S.M. Herald, and who, although a man that
doesn’t know the tail of a horse from the
animal’s ear, lately favored Australia with advice
on the breeding of remounts, publishes a “ poem ”
in Harper’s Weekly. Horatio wrote it on the
Oceanic and calls it “ Verses at Sea.” There are
twenty verses of it, but the first three are above
the average:
Our ship is a beautiful lady,
Friendly and ready and fine ;
She runs her race with storm in her face,
Like a sea-bird over the brine.
In her household work n© hand does shirk,—
No need of belaying-pins,
And the Captain dear and the Engineer,
They both look after the Twins ;
The Twins that drive her to do her best
Where the Roaring Forties rage—
From the Fastnet Height to the Liberty Light,
And the Customs landing stage !
Yet Horatio has some sort of a following and his
alleged novels find type somehow. But his
“poem” has done its work. The Rev. Horatio
is Sir Gilbert now.
Australia is a very big island. Wherefor
a little ABC about Tommy Bent, for the inform-
ation of Longreach, Cambridge Gulf, the caboose
on the top of the Blue Mountains, and the empire-
sized constituency of Page, M.H.R. Bent began
as rate-collector in Moor-abbin, near Brighton,
Vie. He ousted Higinbotham as M.P. for
Brighton, because Higgy “ was no local good.”
Bent rose to be first Minister of Railways, then
Speaker. Was knocked out as a land-boomer,
and got back into Parliament, after sundry years,
as a very forlorn-hope-looking man. The dog
days were not over.

The Vic. railway enginemen sadly miss
their former gen. sec., that born fighter, G.
T. Ross, in their present struggle. The masterful
organising ability of “Tommy” Ross was the
despair of ex-commissioner Mathieson. Hence
his (Ross’) promotion, it is said. Ross left
the Drivers’and Firemen’s Association some years
ago to take a position as Loco, foreman.
HERO M'CABE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Major M'Cabe I they laid him down,
In Dead Man’s Acre hy Wollongong town.
On the soldier’s hier, to the soldier’s grave,
They hore him with panoply grim as brave.
Death drums rolling their awful notes,
Death shots roaring from cannons’ throats.
Over the roofs of the little town ;
Over the beaches, golden-brown ;
Over the Islands Five, whereon
The long wave thundered in Antiphon.
Upward to Kembla’s riven side ;
Over the ridge of the Big Divide.
They will name him better another day,
When, the smother of agony rolled away,
Each record of horror shall stand and shine
On a fair white stone in a golden line.
For Hero M‘Cabe was the man to dare
Battle with princes and powers of air,
With the unchained terror whose track was knows
By corpses of strong men overthrown ;
By the jagged rift on the shatter’d hill;
By the reek of the White-damp, deadly chill—
He counted-the odds by the Fates arrayed,
He reckoned and ’countered them undismayed.
Life was the stake, there was life to win.
But the Silent Player his life dragged in.
Major M‘Oabe might have won renown
In a spatter of blood when the Boer went down |
But Hero M'Oabe gave his life’s last breath
To win back life from that pit of death.
There’s a prayer for the soul of the Hero M‘Oabe
From each widow who croons o’er a fatherless babe)
“ The Hero M'Oabe ! ” is the silent toast
Where’er man pledges man on the Wollongong coast.
And the Islands Five, and old Kembla’s head
In boulder and beach sand shall be spread,
Ere that prayer and that toast are there forgot,
Or lapse with Life’s mean things which matter not.
F.M.

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors.— ln the ease of ethnological and
natural-history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
Cable tells us the present Nile flood is the
lowest on record, so the drought is getting fairly
universal. Apropos of drought: Starving horses,
on a Liverpool Plains -(N.S.W.) station, recently
killed weak sheep and ate the wool.
• * * *
“ Walgett” : Be prickly-pear in Queens-
land. Cattle do all right for a time on boiled
pear, but they soon sicken of it, and unless it is
cut up, it kills them. No doubt pear cut up and
mixed with bran or chaff will keep cattle alive,
but horses quickly die from it, as the fibre forms
into a ball in the stomach. I have seen many post-
mortems on horses and cattle which have been fed
on it, and the cause of death lias been through the
insufficient mastication of the fibre. Cattle,
through chewing the cud, live much longer, but
should they drink on top of a feed, they die. I
have seen cattle live for months on pear in its
native state, hut you should see the inside of them
after death ! Every practical cattle-man whom I
have spoken to on the subject has not a good word
to say for pear as a fodder for cattle. As regards
placing it on Queensland’s coat-of-arms, why not
also place ticks, rabbits, dingoes, marsupials and
droughts! Prickly-pear has such a tremendous
hold of S.-W. Queensland that it will simply cost
millions, to. get rid of it. In the Maranoa and
other districts it has completely overrun the
country ; is impenetrable in places; and is 10 and
12ft. high. Instead of Irmng a blessing, it will in
years to come be one of the greatest curses of a
very much-cursed land.

A Westralian teamster recently travelled
300 miles in a dry country on the Murchison with-
out washing himself. At his journey’s end he
fought a man who referred to his uncleanliness,
and travelled hack 300 miles with the dried blood
of combat on his face.

an’, bedad, ’fore I could get me boot on ’im ’e bit the pick. An’,

shearers, as they keep the same table as a first-class hotel>
as they feed all swagmen and sundowners, exercising a
semi-royal hospitality, how can they expect to make and
keep big cheques?” There is no doubt that a good many
more pastoralists would like to get back to the old days
and the old ways, when bread and butter were luxuries,
and jam prescribed like opium ; and any troublesome
hand could be sent along to an obliging magistrate-neigh-
bor who would give him five-and-twenty for insolence.
Still,'in a serious time like the present, plain truth might
be told about plain facts. For instance, Mr. Abbott
knows as well as any man in the country that feeding
travellers in shearing-time is regulated by custom as well
understood as statute-law. Shearers feed she.-.r rs,
rouseabouts go to rouseabouts’huts ; unless th ■ station
shuts right down and the shearers have to give the poor
devils a bite or see them go hungry. Cooks, and they are
never too plentiful, get 4s. a week from every shearer,
and out of that pay their own assistants. As to grub, I
don’t know much about first-class hotels, but am pre-
pared to swear that any well-managed sixpenny eating-
house in the city makes a better show every meal than
any shearers’ hut in the country. The food is better now
than it used to be, thank God ! The cursed meanness of
squatters and cockies in the matter of grub (which
shearers "are slowly reforming) has sent thousands of men
to look for billets or join the unemployed in the towns.
* * * *
A farmer near Stanthorpe (S.Q.) recently
spent some hundreds of pounds in erecting an
irrigating plant to water a patch of fertile, low-
lying land. Result : A splendid crop of lucerne,
from which aforesaid farmer realised a
handsome return. Second result : A plague of
wild ducks that settled down like locusts on the
lucerne, and had finally to be exterminated at £1
a hundred. This fact speaks volumes for the
severity of the drought.

A glimpse of the drought
Leaving Queensland at Goondiwindi, you pass through
70 miles of dusty wilderness —no vestige of grass any-
where—to Moree. Dead carcases, and dying sheep
which haven’t the strength to get out of the
way without falling everywhere scrub is being
cut wholesale, and the timber is gradually being
demolished. The stations are all hand-feeding. One
is keeping 1200 sheep alive on whole-maize. Bags,
with a small hole cut in the bottom, are placed on
the back of a horse and ridden amongst them ; the corn
is thus scattered. The cost is Gd. per week per sheep.
Another, near Moree, is keeping 7000 starving sheep alive
on chaff, which is thrown broadcast on the land ; esti-
mated cost of this feeding. £2O per day. On the Macin-
tyre a sheep-man is irrigating 1000 acres with a 10 h.p.
steam pump ; 201 showed a nice spring in the grass which
a flock of wild ducks devoured in one night. Emus,
kangaroos, and wallabies are easily run down on foot; the
carriers’ boys are helping to keep things going out of the
skins ; the fences along the road for miles are hung all
over with grotesque carcases of birds and beasts, placed
in all kinds of fantastic positions. At a selection I saw a
widow and her two daughters cutting scrub all day and
boiling prickly-pear half the night to try and keep a few
head of cattle alive. You witness some pathetic sights
en route, —foals standing over dead mires, calves over
dead cows, lambs over dead ewes. Near Bogabilla I saw
a carrier shoot four equine skeletons, the remainder of
his team, unable to keep them alive, with chaff at 15s. a
bag, and maize at 9s. 6d. per bushel. At a mail-change I
saw the horses eiting their own dung. The bores are the
saviour of this part of the State as regards water—prac-
tically no rain for 12 months. Sheep don’t need much shear-
ing, as the little they have comes off in handfuls. Although
the stations are employing a number of hands scrub-
cutting, skinning dead sheep, feeding, &c., when rain
does come there will be no employment in these parts,
and there will practically be no shearing. Provisions are
going up to famine-prices any distance from a railway ;
meat is becoming a luxury ; beef, Bd. and lOd. a lb., and
hardly fit to eat at that; vegetables unprocurable. From
Moree to Narrabri you pass through G 6 miles of barren
plain country with dead sheep huddled together every-
where. All the stations are hand-feeding and keeping a
few sheep alive. From Narrabri to Walgett (120 miles),
passing through Wee Waa, Pillaga, “Come by chance”
the country is one vast wilderness with nothing living to
be seen, only bones and skeletons everywhere. All
parched, perished, dust and desolation. The mighty
bush a great sorrowful waste—cracked, burnt, baked.
A horror ! a hell!

“Wyoming’': lie the lifting-capacity of
trees. Outside O’Brien’s blacksmith shop in W.
Narrabri, N.S.W., stands a tree with a waggon-
wheel on one of its branches 18ft. from the ground.
Years ago, when the old shop was in work, this
wheal was put against a stout sapling, and the
branch has grown through the wheel and in its
ascension with the growth of the tree has carried
the wheel with it. The wheel would weigh about
160 or 1701 b.

“J.”: On the Macquarie River, near
Euchareena, met with a large carpet snake, which
came by its death in a peculiar manner. Appar-
ently it was endeavoring to swallow a rabbit a
little over half-grown, and bunny, in its struggles,
kicked one hind-leg completely through the
snake’s neck, thus causing its death.

“Boko”: In April, ’B9, caught an eel
with an 18-inch black snake inside it. In Feb.,
1902, at Casino (N.S.W.) killed 4-foot black snake
(red belly) in act of swallowing a 2-foot eel. Had
18 inches of eel down when killed. Don’t know
how snake caught eel, and not at all clear how
eel caught snake.

Treesfts well as humans can be killed by
insomnia. “If powerful lights are glaring upon
trees all night long, the leaves will gradually
wither and drop from the branches. This
deadening of the tree is often plainly noticeable
upon the side affected by the light, and a number
of trees in London are being slowly killed by
electric light.

makes a bloomer in crediting the female lyre-bird

but one note, a long drawn-out “ Quis-s-s-s-e.”
It is the male that is the mimic.

“ Jaymac ” : The “ bower ” of Chlamydod-
era maculata, is not as commonly supposed, its nest.
The bower is constructed by the male and female
birds merely for a playhouse. Two parallel lines
of gravel and clay are laid down about lOin. apart,
and often three feet long. Into this rubble long
shafts of silk-grass and kangaroo-grass in bloom
are firmly planted to a depth often of four inches.
The tops of the grass, often 2ft. Gin, high, are bent
till they overlap. Then the birds set to work
to beautify the bower, and co lect quantities
of pretty pebbles, bones, bits of glass, fencing
wire, copper cartridge-cases, gold watches—when
they can get them (they stole Gould’s watch once)
silver or other coins, etc. When rain falls and
the grass gets set in the ground it is difficult to
believe that it has not grown there. The birds in
spring come to the bower at about 10 o’clock a.m.,
and skylark for over an hour. They chase each
other through and around the bower imitating
every bird in the locality, and often one throws
itself down on its back and holds up a bright some-
thing in both of its claws, which the other snaps
from it and bolts, only to bo pursued and robbed
in turn. The finest examples of bowers are found
in Coonamble district, N.S.W. '
The usual wild and woolly person, down
for the sights, ! struck Melbourne at show-time
and was first heard from when he
blundered into a very superior
Collins-street establishment, and,
throwing his bundle on the luxuri-
ous furniture, asked : “ I say, you,
is Mr. Blank in ? ” Nobody knew
Mr. Blank. “What! don’t yer
know Mr. Blank? Blank, M.P.,
I mean. He’s my member, Blank
is, and I want to put up where
he’s puttin’ up, sd’s he can show
me round.” The stranger from
Dim Distance was thunderstruck
when nobody could tell him
where he could put his finger
on Blank, M.P. He took up
his bundle and stumbled out
on the track of Blank, and for
two whole days a shaggy
man, with burrs in his hair
and rabbits in his whiskers,
haunted Bourke-st. and its
environs, and drifted in and
out of its hotels and public
resorts, and persecuted its
policemen with inquiries
about Blank, M.P., and the
more he wandered the greater
was his wonder. Nobody had
the slightest idea where Blank
was to be found, and at last
the shaggy man in the short
clothes, wearing a mangled
bundle as big as a barrel, got so
drunk seeking Blank in cheap
bars that he was run-in for ob-
structing the Force with his.
idiotic quest. When he was trotted
out the weird spirit of the Aus-
tralian bush fixed its dead eye on
the Bench, and said : “Look "here,
Mister, it’s like this: Blank’s
my member; I can’t find Blank.
Put me on to Blank, an’ I give
yer arfer quid.” They put him
away to cool, and Blank came out of the keg
he had been hiding in.

“ Thurla”: Feather-tailed mice (marsupial
and nocturnal) _ are fairly plentiful in Barraba
(N.S.W.I district. They are there known as
flying-mice, because the hind and forelegs on each
side are joined by a strip of skin, which acts as a
kind of wing. The tail is exactly like an emu’s
tail-feather, only that -the shaft is thicker. The
animals are grey, with a white belly, and are
about the size of ordinary house-mice. When
handled they throw off an offensive musky odour.

“ Hix ” : Travelling once per s.s. Mana-
pouri from Hobart to Sydney, when near Barren-
joey, I saw a flying-fox rise from amongst a stack
of fruit-cases on deck. Got astern some distance,
but caught up again, and followed in the wake
like a gull for over an hour. Would like to know
how far the f.-f. can fly, and whether they often
move in daylight.

During a recent rain-storm at Warwick
(Q.), there came to the ground great numbers of
small fishes—resembling bream-fry. According to
Darling Downs Gazette, the ground was covered
with them for acres. Occasional occurrences of
the kind are so well-authenticated that Rouge-
mont’s account of such a happening is not neces-
sarily a lie.

A bush yarn—Old Polonius :
It was at a country race-meeting up the Murray. The
day was a scorcher, and I had just got outside of a luke-
warm “shandy” at the bough-covered booth, and was
making back to the crowd, when I noticed an old fellow
hanging up to the old fence—a big, dark chestnut horse
with a white hind foot. His legs were a wreck, and he
was dog-poor, but something in the set of his game old
head attracted my attention, and I stood for a moment
speculating idly as to what might have been. A little
bandy-legged chap, walking past with a dirty towel in his
hand, came over.
“ They don’t breed that sort these times, sir,” he re-
marked.
“ What is he ? ” I asked.
“That’s Old Polonius, the hurdle-racer,” he said.
“ The best horse I ever threw my leg over—bar none.”
“ Yes,” I assented, “he looks as if he’s been a good
one.”
“ Good,” he spat eloquently, “ you’ve just said it. He
was an out-an’-outer. I rode him in most of his wins, so
I ought to know. Game as they make ’em, too. Never
need to ask him twice. The feel of that fellow under yer
in a finish was something to remember, I can tell yer.
Poor old Pol. He don’t look too good on it now. Most
of his teeth’s gone, but all the same I wouldn’t like to
show him a fence without a good pair of reins, old as he
is. I could tell yer many a yarn about him—but it’s
d d hot here,” he broke off irreverently.
We drifted over towards the booth, and I shouted, and
waited deferentially.
“ I remember one time,” he said, wiping the froth from
his scraggy moustache with a corner of the towel, “ I
went up to a race-meeting on the Monaro side. It was
after ‘ Old Pol.’ brokedown—and—and after I broke down
too,” he added, hesitatingly. “ I was riding him. I was
only up to have a look-round, but there happened to be a
hack race on the programme, and, after seeing the
cuddies that were going to run, I decided to give the old
chap a cut. The entry was twelve bob, and I only
had eight, but I borrowed a crown from the chap
that had the booth, and shoved Old Pol in. He was
awful crutchy, but when I cantered him he shook it off a
bit, and I took my place among the others. Some of the
chaps laughed, and one young fellow on a slippy-looking
black mare asked me if I thought I’d get round in time
tor tea. I didn’t say anything, I wa3 hoping Old Pol’s
near foreleg would stand to him. If it did there- would
be no need for me to talk. He got off the mark all right,
and as we came round the turn I got a good position,
close up to the leaders—the black mare and a low-
set chestnut horse. The mare was a bit fresh, and
swung out wide. I troubled Old Pol with my heels,
and steered him into the gap. As we closed
up the kid on the chestnut said, “By gawd!
the old ’un’s goin’ strong ! ” The chestnut was too fat,
and I could see he wouldn’t last it out. He dropped back
soon after, and a furlong from home we were neclt-and-
neck with the black mare, and three lengths clear of the
rest. I shook Pol up a little, and at the next post he
had half a length of a lead. The chap on the mare got
out his whip, and she closed up on us. We raced together
for a hit. I was frightened to “ ride ’’ the old chap-
thought his legs wouldn’t stand—but, bless you, he’d for-
gotten all about them himself. About a hundred yards
from home the boy made his run, and I sat down on
Pol, and asked him the question he had never
shirked. By God, sir, it does my heart good to think of
it now ! He never finished stronger in his life. Of course
the mare hadn’t a hope. We romped in.
“ I’m a bit ashamed of what happened after,” he went
on after a pause, “ but it’s no use ‘ whipping the cat ’
now. But I ought to have been shot for it. I led him

and went over to the booth. I had no money, but the
chap knew I’d won a race, and it was all right. He carted
me into town next morning with the other empties in the
dray, and two or three days after he told me he had col-

The late Mr. F. A. Packer was in the first
rank of Australian musicians. During his stay of
many years in Tasmania he got into the way of
expressing in song-music the quiet melody of
Nature in that pleasant island, and composed
many songs, such as “Listening” and the “Yellow
Wattle,” which have gained a world-wide reputa-
tion, and some more ambitious works which,
whilst not gaining so much public recognition,
show the spirit of a scholarly musician.
A Bulletin correspondent lately pointed
out that the Vic. railways’ raw recruit engine-
cleaner would be doing things soon. Here is a
fair start: —
Bairnsdale, Wednesday.
Owing to an unfortunate occurrence in the railway
sheds at Bairnsdale, the department is likely to sustain a
rather serious loss. On Monday morning the cleaner in
charge of the engine which was to take the 6 o’clock train
to Melbourne was getting up steam, when he found that
the water was leaking out of the boiler through the blow-
off cock. Not knowing what to do he went to call the
enginedriver, but when they returned to the shed the
water had all escaped from the boiler, which was red-hot,
and the packing of the boiler was on fire. It was after-
wards found that the tubes had been burnt to such an
extent that the boiler appeared to be completely ruined.
Should this prove to be the case, the loss to the depart-
ment will be about £5OO.
Lukas Meyer’s heart-disease, which
accounted for his death the other day, was the
immediate cause, no doubt, of the Boer overtures
for peace. When the British “flying ” columns—
i.e., the columns that didn’t invariably crawl—
began to bustle Meyer and his attendant officials
from place to place the Acting-President of the
Transvaal practically threw up the sponge. The
cable stated at the time that the invaders had
given him very little rest. Meyer, as it now
appears, was physically incapable of bearing the
excitement of hasty retreats when he finally sent
a messenger to Kitchener under a flag of truce.
With Steyn paralysed, and himself practically
dying, he thought the fleeting shadow of Boer
Government had better retire from business.
“Revilo ” writes from M.L. :
The recent typographical award in Wellington, Tara-
naki and Hawkes Bay districts doesn’t please the unions.
Court, after much dilly-dallying, split award into three
sections. Country comps, get two guineas ; in the larger
towns £2los. and £2lss. day and night work respectively ;
in a couple of towns the wages were fixed at £2 ss. and
£2 10s. ; hours, 48. Preference-to-unionists clause was
completely knocked out and master-printers given almost
unrestricted power to employ boys. This was a severe
blow to the union, which spent a good deal of money in
working up the case. Presiding Judge Cooper is an ex-
type-snatcher. On one newspaper, where the staff was
on piece-work and earned from £3 to £4 a-week, the
Scotch proprietor, when the awardcame in, put all hands
on award-wage of £2 15s. In other plan- s union
“agitators ” were fired out. On several struggling sh ets,
however, the award is a dead-letter, and the old system
of 66 hours and 30s. and 355. a week is still in force.
When Edwards and Martin were the Arbitration Court
judges, their decisions met with general approval. But
Judge Cooper doesn’t give satisfaction in labor circles.

“ Via ” : Up the slopes of Mb. Pring (back
of Bowen, Q.) are to be found dwarf pines that do
not grow more than 18in. or 2ft. high. Spent a
couple of days collecting specimens, but out of 15
only one survived ; they don’t take kindly to
transplanting. The one living is now about 2ft.
high, and is at least seven years old. It is very
delicate. Has any B. reader seen them elsewhere
in Q. ? I have questioned travellers unsuccess-
fully.

“Doris”: He “West Indian Medico’s”
par (B. 31/8/’01) “ Does the earth stop twice in the
24 hours?” Have heard an old well-borer say
that after striking good flow, the best time to
attempt to control it is at noon, as the pressure
eases off at that time. Perhaps some Bulletin
reader can trace a connection between loss of
pressure and the hour before dawn. [Time some
one took up the much fresher question : Does the
ocean dry up twice a day.—Ed. B.]

“Trotter”: Was recently talking to the
editor of a N. Q. coast paper when a man com-
menced to fill the office up with vegetables.
Asking what this meant, I was told, “Oh, that’s
the way lie pavs his subscription.” Once, at
Bellinger River (N.S.W.), I was surprised to see
cartloads of pumpkins going to the race-course.
Discovered that, as the cookies had no money they
did their wagering in pumpkins.
• • * *
Can any botanist recognise the difference
between the male and female paw-paw (Q.) trees
before the flowers appear ? Growers suffer great
loss for want of that knowledge. When the trees
come up they grow nearly nine months before
they flower, and then the males have to be
chopped out (and they are always in the majority).
One farmer told writer that out of 100 plants set
only three were females, and another time of 1000
seeds only 18 were females.
“ H.N.” : My mate and I were work-
ing some distance apart, and he was hidden
by the rising ground. I could see the fast-
drying top of the tree he was at. When I
had finished my log, I gathered up the tools
and was walking over the rise, when I saw
a big, dry limb drop out of the tree my mate
was felling. It had probably been broken
off, and, hanging across another, was dis-
lodged by the vibration caused by the axe-
strokes—the sound of which instantly
ceased. “Good God !’’ I thought—-“he’s
killed. The branch has fallen on him ! ”
After a moment’s horrified pause I rushed
on till I could see my mate sitting with his
back to the trunk of the.tree. Alive, at all
events!—for he moved. Throwing down
the tools I ran to him. “ Hard luck ! ” he
growled. “ Here’s a big lump of a limb fell
out of the head of the tree, knocked the axe
out of my hand, and smashed the blessed
handle. And we haven’t a spare'handle
left in the camp.” Some men don’t know
good luck when they see it 1

Recently at Hillington (Queensland)
Germany and Ireland came to blows, bub
previous to the commencement of hostili-
ties the former removed his false teeth
and handed them to his backer. Each
round ended in the disappearance of the
combatants in the prevailing dust. After-
emerging from the grit of the fifth round
Ireland claimed the tight on a foul, stating
that Germany had been eating his neck.
This Germany’s backer stated could not be
true, as he had his man’s teeth in his pocket,
and he produced them in evidence. Foul dis-
missed.

“G.F.8.”: Re mean men. I know one
who once walked 50 miles (25 miles each way) be-
tween two country towns to save 6d. exchange on
a cheque. Same man, when travelling the country,
if he arrived near a town near nightfall, would
always hobble his horse, and sleep in the open, to
Save the price of a bed. Yet he was brought up
in liberal circumstances in England, and educated
for the Bar.

Thus oracularly the M‘Cooey :
Let all authorities on the Australian blackfellovv listen.
One marvellous attribute of the aborigine is the power or
gift of putting his or her finger on the precise spot one
points at. If one stands 10 yards from ope, and point
with a pencil at any part of an aborigine’s face or body,
upon the precise spot our black brother or sister can put
a finger. This I discovered 30 j ears ago. A female first
cousin of mine, who isn’t a black gin, also has this gift;
but probably only one in a thousand possesses this
peculiar attribute. This is unquestionably the outcome
of inherited practice stopping missiles thrown at their
ancestors in combat.
The M‘Cooey’s remark that his kinswoman
above-mentioned is not a black gin perhaps has
something to do with his name M‘Gooey. The
Mao part is white (or, at the worst, Sandy), but
the Cooey is suspiciously aboriginal and smacks
of waddies and dilly-bags.
* * » *
“W.A. Bushman”: Whilst in Africa
recently I noticed that the niggers there couldn’t
hold a candle to our myalls in following up
“spoor.” At Marandellas (Rhodesia) one of the
N.S.W. bushmen astounded the natives by track-
ing to his hut in a kraal, four miles out, the bare-
footed nigger that had stolen a l’ifle from arm-stand
in tent at main camp. The nigger had followed
the pad used by hundreds of niggers, all bare-
footed. In W.A. I have seen gins track the
bungarra across bare granite rocks. I believe the
yarn about the Forbes police tracker who was
able to trace a mosquito across a galvanized iron
roof to be a lie, yet at Pendinni, in this State, I
have seen a native who could tell which way a
wild turkey flew oft a calm day half-an-hour after
it had passed by the track it left through the air.
Have any of your readers seen this excelled ? The
nigger referred to could always discern the stars
on cloudless days, and I have known him to recog-
nise rum three miles off in pitch darkness.
* * * *
The various Australian Lands Departments
contain stacks of letters which go a very long way
to show how minds become unhinged in the grey
and melancholy bush. One Gippsland settler re-
cently sent his 50th letter to the Victorian Minis-
ter, threatening to do for the whole office, because
of a ti-ivial imagined slight; another persistently
draws attention to the facial resemblance of the
calves born in his district to a certain local official.
Scores of these interesting, but wholly irrelevant,
documents reach the Department in the course of
a year.
* * » *
Writes “ Alpha Crucis ” :
He par (16/8/’O2) about explorer Gregory: Neither
Gregory nor any other explorer crossed this continent
from east to west, or from west to east. Gregory never
tried to, never wanted to, and never talked of doing it.
It does not require a profound knowledge of mathematics
to take a stellar observation, nor is it at all an exceptional
thing for an explorer to do. On the contrary, the latitude
is always ascertained by stellar observation. Longitude
cannot be obtained by solar observations on land, nor is
anyone such a muff as to try, for the simple and obvious
reason that Greenwich time cannot be kept. A chrono-
meter will not stand land carriage. There is not a place
in Australia, outside of telegraphic communication, the
longitude of which is accurately marked. Travelling on
an East and West course, the ascertaining of the latitude
is of little service, and dead-reckoning has to be relied
on. On a North and South course, the latitude is invalu-
able, and on any course which includes much northing
and southing, it serves to check the dead-reckoning.
The early explorers were much more correct in their
positions than the modern ones, but their parties were
larger, and more attention was devoted to the survey.

MISS EVA MYLOTT , the N.S.W. contralto,
whose farewell benefit will take place shortly.
Photo, by The Swiss Studios, Sydney.
“Alia” : Not long since that, under the
influence of the “local member,” Govt, was in-
duced to spend a considerable sum in trying to
eradicate the water-hyacinth from some of the
confluent streams of the Clarence River, various
statements being made that navigation would be
impeded were the plant allowed to spread. Since
the drought, however, it has been found that an
acre of hyacinth-covered water is equal in fodder-
value to five in any of the best paddocks.

Miss Eva Mylott, who is to be tendered a
farewell concert, August 27, at Sydney Town
Hall, lias won an established place on the plat-
form as much by the charm of her manner as by
the beauty of her voice. Miss Mylott is a native
of N.S.W., and was trained by Mr. Riccardi for
voice-production, and subsequently by Mr. Hazon
for Italian and oratorio, and soon won a position
as a star contralto. She now intends to go further
afield and study under the best masters of the old
world ; and, as a farewell, her friends have
organised the concert on the 27th inst, which
should be a marked success.

THE CEMENT INDUSTRY
Two facta which have recently transpired
require to be stated together. A couple of
months ago the Metropolitan Board of Works
aocepted the tender of a firm in Germany
for supplying 6000 casks of cement. Last
Friday the Geelong Cement Works shut
down, and has thrown 130 men out of work,
because of the large stocks of cement for
yhich there Is no local market.

The burning question with Q. Federal
employees just now is whether they can be com-
pelled to pay up under Philp’s income-tax pro-
posals. The Federal public servant has an
insatiable and discreditable anxiety to dodge the
income-tax.
Bananalancl Govt, doesn’t believe in old
age pensions, and yet 887 males and 652 females
last year received an allowance of ss. a week
a-piece. Received it plus the stigma of a pauper
allowance—a brand carefully retained for sound
economical reasons.
The second reading of the Bill for the
Reform of the Constitution was carried in Vic.
Assembly by 79 votes to 5. Despite which the
House of 48 Rich Landlords will doubtless say, in
a shocked tone of voice, that there is no evidence
that the country wants the Bill.
S.M. Herald and Sydney D.T., under
various disguises of feigned moderation, deter-
minedly egg-on the Senate to reject the Repre-
sentatives’ tariff; and at the same time they
maintain that the trading public is woefully
harassed by tariff uncertainties.
THE OLD PEOPLE’S BIT.
“Altogether Victoria has paid £5,000,000 in Civil
Service pensions.”— News item, Melbourne Age.
VICTORIAN TAXPAYER : “ Well, I think that I
can stand that little hit, too.”
There are no recruits to drill in S.A., and
the 10 instructors sent over from N.S.W. and Q.
by Gen. Hutton are being employed on clerical
work, to the exclusion of local officials. Just
now (the time of paying has come), the farinaceous
State is so heartily sick of gore, guns and glory
that it shudders at the sight of kharki.
They have the usual patent way of squaring
the public finances in S.A. There was a deficit of
£239,174 at the end of last June. Of this, £174 is
to be paid out of revenue, while the £239,000 is to
be met by the issue of Treasury Bills. Yet the
Govt, is indignant if it is stated that interest is
Said out of loans. At present, S.A. owes the
•ritish capitalist £27,000,000, and pays £1,037,000
annually in interest.
A certain John Millar, of Glasgow, has
opened up communications with Premier Philp re
a proposed concession of 15,000 acres of land for
growing materials for tanning. Land is to become
the property of Millar if the scheme is successful,
and to revert to the Crown if unsuccessful. As
Millar is about the largest purchaser of tanning
materials in Great Britain, producing about
£250,000’s worth of leather per annum, the scheme
is attracting attention. But why John Millar,
who proposes to raise tanning material, should get
a present of the land required, any more than
Jack Smith, who raisas wheat and potatoes, no-
body can explain.
A good point about Vic. Treasurer Shieis’
Kyabram interim budget is its kick against the
consistent tapping of the Treasury by country
municipalities, the majority of which rate only up
to Is. in the £. Every hole in a road, or plank
out of a bridge, is now made the subject of a
deputation to the metropolis, and the cost of some
of these little pleasure-jaunts by a dozen or more
si lire councillors would go far towards defraying
the cost of the repairs they ask for. There is
little _ self-reliance about the Man on the
Land in Victoria. He has been encouraged by the
newspapers to ask Government for everything,
and the Melb. dailies every morning contain
columns of cheap local growl about twopennv-
halfpenny local jobs that the Government hasn’t
attended to —and shouldn’t attend to.
The proposed reduction of Ministers in the
Q Cabinet has dwindled down to something very
trifling. One Minister only has been dispensed
with, and he has received a compensatory billet in
the position of Vice-President of the Executive
Council—a position usually occupied by the
Premier. The Vice-Presidency carries with it a
solatium of £3OO per annum, and was doubtless
gladly accepted.
The impudence of a common M.P. :
Mr. Quinn asked the N.S.W. Premier whether he would
communicate with the Admiral in order to ascertain if
he would send some of the idle war-ships of the Austra-
lasian Squadron in search of the missing vessel (Quirang).
“ Idle war-ships ” indeed ! Mr. Quinn evidently
doesn’t dine with Excellency Beaumont more than
three times a week.
If the White Australia law can do what it
was started to do, Federal Ministers should be
only too glad to publish monthly a return showing
the number of colored persons who sought admis-
sion during the month, the number allowed to
land, and the number rejected under the regula-
tions. The alleged reluctance of Ministers to
provide such returns, except in answer to specific
motions in Parliament, admits of a bad con-
struction.
S.M. Herald :
Our special reporter in Melbourne states that Federal
Ministers seem to be of opinion that the Northern
Territory would not he a very desirable acquisition for the
Commonwealth.
For that matter Australia isn’t a very desirable
acquisition for the Commonwealth, but that can’t
be helped. Desirable or not, Australia has ac-
quired Australia, and it has to take it as it comes,
the Territory included. It won’t “acquire” the
Territory any more by putting it under the
Federal Government than by leaving it under the
S.A. Government, though the Federal authority
may be able to put the Lost Province to better
use. _ The foolishness of needlessly acquiring the
outside wildernesses and niggers of Papua and the
New Hebrides is obvious enough, but we can’t
help acquiring our own country.
Premier James, of Westralia, proposes to
cut down the two Houses from a membership of
50 and 30 to 46 in the
Assembly and 21 in the
Council. Even that means
67 legislators for about
200,000 people—one to
every 3000 inhabitants.
Yet in a huge country like
Westralia 46 M’s.L.A. are
not too many if it is in-
tended that the Assembly
shall have some know-
ledge of every part of the
country it is legislating
for—it is the 21 M’s.L.C.
whoare superfluous. James
proposes adopting the
Joint Sitting as a remedy
for deadlocks, but the best
remedy for deadlocks is to
make it a Joint Sitting all
the time—the Sitting can’t
be too Joint. Premier
James says that “it
struck him as a private
individual that there
ought not to be any neces-
sity in a small State like
Westralia for two Houses.”
The sooner this obvious
fact strikes Mr. James
as a public individual also,
the better. A Premier is a public character, and
things like that ought to strike him publicly.
The Federal Government has been fighting
hard to establish, by means of bonuses which will
be paid by the whole Commonwealth, a great iron
industry in New South Wales. Yet this fact
makes not the least difference in the conviction
of the cranky parochial party in Sydney that
the Federal Ministry, in which Victoria is,
in proportion to population, the worst represented
of all the States, is “a Victorian Ministry ” bent
on selling N.S.W. bodily* to some imaginary
person of bad character in Melbourne. The
Carruthers crowd has dwelt upon that imaginary
person of bad character in Melbourne till he has
become a monstrous mental disease.
The soul of Daniel Levy is harassed over
the constitutional position of M’sL.A. in N.S.W.
Leg. Assembly who are at the same time news-
paper proprietors. There are at present four of
these—Messrs. John Norton, “Monaro” Miller,
Arch. Campbell and R. Davidson. As these
members receive Govt, money for Govt, advts.,
Mr. Levy asked Premier See whether such was
not an irregular thing. Mr. See replied that the
matter had been settled in ’95, when it was
decided that the transactions referred to did not
amount to a “ contract,” as defined by the Consti-
tution Act. Certainly, the fact that a member of
the House has the chance of securing, by conces-
sions, a liberal supply of Govt, advts., is open to
suspicion.
Just now every Freetrade rag on the face
of this continent is rising to say that it will be
nothing short of a crime on the part of the Federal
Government and the Protectionist party in the
House of Reps, to provoke a conflict with the
Senate by not consenting to all the requests of
that body for reductions in the tariff. A long list
of the woes that will afflict the land if this evil
course is persisted in is then reeled off, and the
hope expressed that better counsels will prevail.
So far none of these papers has been struck with
the idea that it would be a crime on the part of
the Senate to provoke such a conflict with the
JAeps. by demanding wholesale reductions of the
tariff. All the sophistry in the world will not get
away from the fact that the men who designed
the Constitution intended the House of Reps, to
be the predominant partner in matters of finance.
The Constitution is plain enough on the subject,
and if any crime is committed it will be by the
fanatical, unpatriotic Senators who wish to
destroy Australian industries for the benefit of
the importing gang, of which M‘Millan, the Dry
Dog, and the blatant Conroy act as agents.

bushels of wheat and 22J million cwts. of flour.
One shilling a bushel on wheat and 2s.
per cwt. on flour would return £8,250,000
a year, which would give old age pen-
sions to the Britisli people on the
A feature about M.L. re-
ligious census of 1901, which
makes the church organs whoop
excessively, is that while the lead-
ing denominations have increased
nearly 12 per cent, since 1896, Free-
thinkers and Agnostics have declined
about 28 per cent. Another aspect is that
the emotional religions are barely holding
their own—Roman Catholicism doesn’t in-
crease very much and Salvation Army-ism
shows a huge decline. But Presbyterianism
—the Cash religion—is stronger than ever.
Premier Irvine of Victoria, when it
seemed possible that the railway strike might be
averted, began to put on the airs of a Kitchener
and talk about “demanding unconditional sur-
render” and about the terms on which the
leaders of the men who disliked having
their wages cut down would be “forgiven.”
Irvine has only to maintain this insulting
attitude long enough to get his strike after all,
and then he will probably be sorry. His apparent
position is that he and Bent are willing to risk
dislocating the whole trade of the community
merely to gratify a coarse desire for the humilia-
tion of their opponents—the desire of two poor
mean men to be apologised to, whatever it may
cost. And the country isn’t so anxious as all that
for Bent to be apologised to. Bent never apolo-
gised to Victoria when it lost its good deposits in
the financial institutions he used to be connected
with.
Be that clause (carried by the Representa-
tives) prohibiting the nomination of a member of
State Parliament for a seat in the Federal Parlia-
ment. Nearly all the Q. Federal Democratic
legislators owed their return to the fact that
they were already members of the State Par-
liament and as such had become well-known
and well - advertised politicians. Conse-
quently the action of certain of them in sup-
porting above-mentioned clause looks very
like an attempt to break the ladder that hoisted
them up for fear someone else might use it. This
thing will infallibly militate against Democratic
progress. The Fat Man candidate (even when
not a State legislator) can always get abundance
of advt., and his social position is a big thing,
while the Democratic Federal candidate (unless
he has won reputation in a State Assembly) comes
forward handicapped by the fact that he is un-
known, and has no public political record.
Writes “ Gumleaf ” :
If the Victorian railway men did not strike, it was not
the fault of the Irvine Ministry. The arrogance and
ignorance of Bent and his Tory colleagues in dealing with
the question of classification and retrenchment in the
Railway Department have been colossal. Bent is a
blusterer who has hustled his fellow Ministers into a ridi-
culous position. All trouble could have been avoided by
a promise to put the scheme of classification, agreed to
between the Commissioner of Railways and the men, into
force as soon as possible. If that had been done the men
would never have raised any objection to the percentage
reductions. Bent refused the classification in the most
piggish manner. All along, Bent, Irvine and co.
have irritated the men whenever they got a
show. The merits of their case were never con-
sidered by the Cabinet. Ministers found it was popular
to cut and hack at the public servants instead of putting
on a decent-sized land-tax, which Premier Irvine himself
has admitted over and over again is the proper way to
CURIOUS HEATHEN RITES LATELY WITNESSED IN THE
NORTH. WANTED, MORE MISSIONARIES.
pull the country out of its financial troubles. They also
found that the two Melb. newspapers, in whose shadows
they eternally stand and shiver, were going to lend their
aid in jumping on the Public Service, and particularly
the railway men. This quarrel between the public and
its servants seems to indicate that the people of Victoria
have lost the last remnant of honesty. They have turned
to the doctrine that might is right. In effect they say
“ We shall howl for remissions of taxation, we shall insist
upon the expenditure of large sums of money on roads
and railways that do not pay ; on S’African and China
contingents ; on ducal razzle-dazzles ; on old age pensions
(the one commendable item on the list), and we will make
the public servants pay the piper.”
