Rev. Silly-Dilly Macky sees a great danger
to the cause of the Yellow Pup in N.S.W. in
Womanhood Suffrage, as the R.C. women “will
vote as their priests tell them to.” Thus, though,
as he admits, “the Protestants could muster three
adherents to every one Catholic,” Popish domi-
nance is threatened ! Mr. Macky finds an effec-
tive reply to his own foolish statement. If the
R.C.’s only number one-fourth of the community,
it is nonsense to pretend that they can, by their
votes, oppress the other throe-fourths. So soon
as they attempt anything which the rest of the
community doesn’t agree with, they’ll fail, and
that’s an end of it. What the people who are now
so industriously fanning the sectarian flame aim
at, is not Protestant equality with R.C.-ism, but
the absolute subjection of the R.C. section, as in
the Kiama Ghost days ; and in this aim they are
being very materially assisted by the foolishness
of some R.C. dignitaries of the Cardinal Moran
stamp.
A Victorian pressman writes :
The present political situation in the Cabbage-Garden
is, in some respects, wildly humorous, the most comical
view being the one that has the large, squat figure of Bent
in the middle distance. Bent has made the somewhat
common mistake of reckoning men as cabbages, and the
possible results of this are large and various. Somehow,
railway men don’t seem game to strike, though they must
know that by so doing they could turn the State upside-
down in a week and gain all they want. A strong Liberal
Ministry would stick to the retrenchment scheme (having
once promulgated it)and give the men what they want more
than non-retrenchment—a fixed, just classification, with
reasonable certainty of tenure and freedom from super-
session by the “ casual ” hands. But such a Ministry
doesn’t seem obtainable at present in Victoria. The one
thing Victoria gets with absolute certainty is a crowd of
Sleepy Immovables, dwelling stolidly in one place
and refusing to budge. The House of Fat, at latest, had
consented to hasten its consideration of the Factories
Act. It’s even betting that its “ consideration ”of that
measure will end in its throwing it heavily into the gutter
and dropping off to sleep again ; and if that eventuates
anything may happen. It is only the necessity for
renewing the Factories Act (which expires with the
session) that keeps Irvine in power, and if that goes
Irvine, Shieis, Bent, Peacock and the rest of the In-
capables will go to the country forthwith.

J. G. Griffin, alderman, Sunday Shell-
back wild-cat tipster, member of the Water
and Sewerage Board of Sydney, and other
things, too numerous for print, has now
handed over to the St George Cottage Hospital
the business of the notorious Chain Letter.
According to the statement rendered by Mr.
Griffin the people who sent the used stamps for-
warded none of exceptional value, as the number
received (between two and three million, according,
to Sydney D.T.) only realised £248 17s. 6d.
Mr. Griffin’s well-meant enterprise has thus met
with very bad luck; this writer has seen £l5O
worth of used postage stamps stuck on one page
of a collector’s album. In future the hospital
secretary will receive the stamps and dispose of
them for the purpose of erecting a ward at the
institution.
“Clinical Experiences.” —There has
never been published in Australia a more excellent
or worthy addition to the literature of the colony
than the 1902 edition of “Clinical Experiences,”
compiled by the Consulting Staff of Specialists
of the Freeman and Wallace Electro-Medical
and Surgical Institute, Bathurst and Elizabeth-
streets, Sydney, whose advertisement appears in
another column. The treatise is a handsome one,
and is of an interest to the public, which can be
readily understood when the volume is shewn to
comprise the large amount of si>o pages, which
again are interspersed with 150 full and half-page
plates of the human system, and of the Institute.
These medical illustrations show the perfection
of illustrative excellence, and will enlighten the
reader of much that refers to his anatomy, or the
various functions of his body. The book is invalu-
able to persons living in the country, and may be
secured by posting to the Specialists of the
Institute the sum of One Shilling in Stamps,
which will mail it free to all Australia.*
A weird story, true or untrue, of the
sea was lately told in Sydney Water Police
Court by Chun Yun, head fireman of the
S.S. Chingtu, per medium of an interpreter.
Chun declared that when the steamer was
about a day out from Hong Kong, Li Hing
went on deck to get some water, and the chief
engineer, finding him there, fell upon him with a
club and struck Hing across the shins, whereupon
Hing fell down and started to sing out. Just
here the second engineer happened along, and
according to Chun, a common impulse seized the
two officers, who promptly grabbed up Hing and
swung him over the side of the steamer ; and then
proceeded to smoke with the aspect of gentlemen
who had performed an onerous duty to society.
Chun said he didn’t complain to the captain as he
was afraid he might go the same way, and that the
same sensible conclusion occurred to the other
Chows who saw the little unpleasantness. Ten
Australian ports were touched at, but Chun said
he was not allowed to land or communicate with
anyone at any of them, and it was not till he
reached Sydney that he managed to get a letter
ashore. The alleged murder is said to have taken
place about 9 a.m , on the lower deck, in a most
public place and manner. A curious feature of
the case is that the prosecution was conducted by
some private personages, although it was for
murder on the high seas ; and the accused, though
committed for trial, were allowed bail on the under-
standing that they are to work their steamer back
to China and will be tried by a special court when
the steamer returns. Meantime the Chinese
witnesses are to be detained in Sydney.

A Bulletin correspondent:—
Vic. Govt, proposals re percentage deductions, tnough
appearing moderate to some, have only added to the un-
rest of the public service, particularly the Railway
department. Doubtless, could the original intentions of
the Irvine-Shiels crowd be known, they were much more
severe. The agitation caused by the hint of percentage
deductions was fruitful, inasmuch as it almost certainly
moderated the cutting policy. The railway employees
have a particular horror of percentage deductions. They
have not forgotten the 1893-98 reign of the system. But
when the trouble comes to a ballot for or against a
strike itTinds the men much divided. A strike is more
dreaded by many than the retrenchment policy. Accus-
tomed to a monthly (or fortnightly) pay-day for years,
as accustomed to spending the pay, many prefer even
a “ retrenched ” pay-day, to the nsk of no pay-day at all.
And the old “ compo.” men pause ere risking the
loss of pension-rights. Some say, “ All right; the public
demands retrenchment. We will retrench too. Nothing
but bare necessaries of life in future. Goods subject to
Customs duties especially to be eschewed. The Age and
Argus want retrenchment. We’ll retrench them."
Paris Nesbifc, K.C., is privately circulating
in Adelaide copies of a petition he has sent to
Lieut. Governor Way, against the 20-years sen-
tence on Flynn, lately convicted of highway-
robbery. Lawson, admittedly guilty, declared
solemnly when sentenced that Flynn had no share
in it. The “humble petition ” sets out that the
verdict was against the weight of evidence, also
that the presiding judge showed great animus
against the prisoner and misdirected the jury ;
and a fresh trial is asked for. And the presiding
judge was Way himself ! However, the petition
reads seriously enough up to Clause 7, which
merely sets out that * ‘ One good turn deserves
another,” the next clause claiming that, though
Flynn is “only an Irishman,” he has as much
claim to consideration as if he were “of Russian
or any other nationality.” This is of course a
delicate allusion to the Andersen case, where a
Russian sailor, convicted of manslaughter, was
quietly released after a time by the Holder Govt.,
after reference to Way, and without reference to
Judge Bundey, who had tried the case ! It was
always understood that Russian Govt, influence,
working through Chamberlain, brought about that
curious leniency, and the point is still a sore one
to touch. Altogether, reading between the lines,
Nesbit’s Humble Petition must be pronounced
“ red hot.”

George Rignold’s sale (at Sydney last week) was respon-
sible for a miscellaneous collection of autographed photo-
graphs. Known and unknown celebrities from the early
fifties to the present day were there in plenty, and G.R.
himself, in the dress of every period, from 2000 B.C. to
1902, A.D. Forty-seven plain, colored, charcoal, chalk,
pastel, oil, watercolor, mezzotint likenesses represented
the Limelight Monarch in equestrian, aquatic, frowning,
smiling, warlike, peaceful, and kingly attitudes. The
theatrical effects were slaughtered—s 2 wigs went for 265.
No bids for scenery or Australian rights of “ Fedora ” or
“Romany Rye," but Bland Holt secured “Lights o’
London” for £26o—not a bad prioe, considering that
George Rignold has had nearly 20 years’ use of it; but
there’s money in, “ Bess—Bess, my poor Bess,” yet.

former. ‘ Quite a small man! ’ That is the second
thought. It is not strictly true, of course. He is sft. 9yin.,
as he explains on being asked, but the size of him every
other way takes away the apparent height. Sandow
shakes hands gently—no such grip as you get from Ernest
Jones or A. H. Jarvis—and hopes you will call on him at
his hotel. As he stands a little later, naked except for a
leopard-skin belt and a curious pair of open-work boots,
before a hard-staring, semi-circle of doctors, lawyers, and
men about town, with no unnecessary fuss he shows
up his various muscles, and then invites and answers
questions from all-comers. They come fast enough, but
his wit is ready, and the merely frivolous questions are
skillfully turned. It is not well to inhale when smoking ;
one can eat pretty well anything if his System is in need ;
it is absurd to think that one gets sufficient exercise in
the daily’ moving about; games of all kinds have his
cordial approbation, but you must use his System for 20
minutes daily'. ‘ I have a good appetite, but not a very
great one,’ he says in a pleasant voice with a strong
German accent; ‘ but I always leave off feeling that I
could hold a little more. That is important.’ ‘ And the
same with drink ? ’ puts in an irreverent lawyer ; and for
once Sandow has no reply ready. Then the leader of the
medical profession tests his heart. ‘ I wish I had it,’ says
the professor, simply.
The Lady’s Realm (London), which came to grief so
amusingly and decisively over the Coronation number
that it printed far in advance, described ceremonies
that didn’t take place, and maliciously criticised artists
who didn’t perform, is run by that unco guid firm of Con-
gregational publishers, the Hutchinsons (writes The B.’s
London correspondent). Punch, under heading “ Clara
Vraie de Vraie ” (which is a drive at “A Peer’s Daughter,”
the Realm’s lady-contributor), verses stingingly on the
matter, and libel actions are in the immediate vicinity of
Hutchinson and Co,
The sudden closing of Melb. Bijou after the Bth night
of “ Cyrano de Bergerac ” is said to be likely to result in
interesting legal proceedings. But the Bijou has known
several sudden closures since Brough and Boucicault
resigned the lease some 10 years ago, and on more than
one occasion the expected law-case did not ensue.
A complimentary ben. is being arranged at Melbourne
for Mrs. G. B. W. Lewis, the Rose Edouin of yore.
Though she only looks 40, she has the hardihood to
notify that her first appearance on the boards was just
50 years ago, at the Strand Theatre, London. The
Edouins were Charles, John, Eliza, Rose, Willie, Julia.
The new Queen’s Theatre, Melbourne (n&e the Victoria
Hall), is as pretty a little place as there is in the city ; it
has been redecorated by architect Billy Pitt, but it will
not even hold as much money as Toole’s little old crib of
a theatre in London. And the two entrances are long,
narrow passages, on either side of a pub. A fatal
objection.
The only authenticated portrait of the real De Bergerac
—if it is authenticated—represents him as a rather good-
looking man with a not abnormal nose of the Hebrew
type. But in the drama it is spoken of as quite abnormal.
Ragueneau, the pastrycook, says that when you see the
nose you think it is a joke and expect to see De Bergerac
“ take it off.”
Irving, in the winter of his career, finds himself a
financial wreck. The house he had lived in since 1870
was sold just five years ago, his cherished library follow-
ing. A few months afterwards Terry’s daughter, Elsie
Craig, opened a milliner’s shop off the Strand,
principally patronised by “ the profession.” By the way,
a famous dictum of Ellen’s is, “ Never insult a ballet-girl,
she may be manageress of a theatre next week.” Every
poor, but attractive, young ballet-girl carries a sealskin
jacket in her shabby carpet bag, so to speak.
There seems to be an Anglo-Australian association for
sounding the praises of one Madame Minna Fischer, as a
singing mistress. Madame Minna secured Amy Castles
for an occasional “pupil” when the damsel came to
London. Regina Nagel followed the Castles lead, and then
the whole army of London paragraphists for Australian
papers (including, of course, the British Australasian)
started to boom Madame Minna aforesaid. The audacious
puff for the teacher is accountable for pars of this sort:
“ Miss Nagel has made some improvement under Madame
Minna Fischer, but her middle register is still very weak,
and I fear &c., &c.” The fact is that Regina Nagel had a
magnificent voice and was beginning to sing beautifully
when she cut herself adrift from Marchesi, who had had
her in hand for two years, or more. Her throat became
more troublesome than ever after she left Paris, and the
result of sundry operations on her enlarged tonsils and
post-nasal growths has been rather disastrous. Her voice,
it would appear, has lost power and “ quality.”
In the prettiness of his pianoforte-playing, Barron
Morley reminds one of Albert Friedenthal, who travelled
through Australia some three years ago. Perhaps Morley
is equal to Friedenthal in Chopin music, but, at present,
he can’t be compared with that gifted person as an enter-
tainer. Friedenthal used to keep his audience for two
hours, and everything he touched was made bright and
interesting. But, then, he didn’t give his delicate, un-
assisted performance in town halls. The pianist who
would draw a couple of thousand people to a big building
must be a powerful and tempestuous thumper in the first
place. By the way, Mark Hambourg is said to be due in
Australia again early next year.
Brough has engaged George Bromley, a clever young
Melbourne pianist and musician, to manage the little
orchestra for his forthcoming Indian tour. *
The End of The War.
“Thank goodness, the war is about ended,”
remarked Brown. “ What’s that ? ” asked Green.
“I say that the war is nearly over,” repeated
Brown. “What war?” asked Green. “Great
Scot! ” shouted Brown, ‘ ‘ don’t you know that the
British Empire has been fighting the Boers for
three years?” “Now you refer to it more par-
ticularly, I think I have heard it mentioned; but
then, you know, I never had any memory for
small matters.” Brown groaned, and had thoughts
which he rejected as unfit for utterance.
That’s just the trouble with some of us—we
are unable to perceive the importance of certain
profoundly important things. Nothing warms
the upper part of a tall building in winter so
effectually as for the basement to be well alight—
but the subsequent developments are too rapid
and destructive for the comfort to be long
enjoyed, and it is as well to adopt practical
measures at once. Now here is the true story of
a woman threatened by a danger who, instead of
ignoring it or vainly hoping to dodge it, wisely
fought and conquered by aid of the best and most
approved weapon for that purpose.
Mr. Charles M. Morrison, of Arcade Buildings,
High-street, Armadale, Victoria, writes _on
January 9, 1902: “In 1887 a fire broke out in a
house I then occupied in Melbourne. My wife
discovered it and rushed forth to give the alarm.
A neighbour’s dog, hearing her outcries and
seeing her run, sprang at her and knocked her
down. Fortunately, the fire was soon extin-
guished ; but the double shock of the fire and the
dog’s attack had a calamitous effect upon my
wife. For a time she was prostrate, her nervous
system being entirely deranged. It was bub little
food she was able to retain ; more often than not
it caused violent fits of vomiting. Her sleep, too,
was much disturbed, and she wasted away
alarmingly. Notwithstanding that she was
treated by five doctors, she continued in this
pitiable condition for years. No doctor seemed
to understand her case or able to afford her
relief. We had lost faith, both in doctors and
medicine, when, about three years ago, a friend
recommended us to try Mother Seigel’s Curative
Syrup. He was so persistent that we procured a
bottle of it. My wife took it regularly, and soon
began to benefit by it. In a little while she was
able to take ordinary food, and to retain it. For
three months she took Mother Seigel’s Curative
Syrup, by which time she was quite cured. She
has remained well ever since, and at present is
quite able to perform her household duties.”

Walking-stick Dibbs ! It is somewhat

Extract from the chairman’s speech at the
annual meeting of theMulgrave Central Mill (Q.):
The directors had arranged for a plentiful supply, of
Island labor, and were prepared to find the growers with
the whole of the labor they required to carry on their
farms. ... If the farmers wished this efficient labor
supply to continue it was necessary that they should
avoid contracting for any work outside the mill “ boys.”
They must find work for these “ hoys ” ; if they were idle
they would still have to he paid. If the farmers did not
employ these “ boys,” but employed outside labor, they
would have 2000 Kanakas sitting down, and the share-
holders be paying them for doing nothing. They must
encourage the use of these “ boys ” during the slack
season in every shape and form.
That lets a big cat out of the bag. The Kanakas,
imported only for field work, are to be used for
“ every shape and form ” of farm toil.
The petition now being signed at New
Caledonia, asking the French Government to re-
commence the sending of convicts, repeats in
almost identical phrases the petition sent by a
section of N.S.W. society to tne British Govern-
ment requesting a renewal of convict transporta-
tion. The development of the country is the
basis of the petition in both cases. That is
followed by the need for “ reliable labor,” and so
on. When transportation to Botany Bay was
stopped the wages of labor jumped up. . That was
really the whole trouble. But so speciously was
the case stated, and such all-wool patriots did the
petitioners represent themselves, that the British
Government was for a time seriously minded to
revive the old diabolism, and would probably have
revived it but for the vigorous stand made by a
little group in Sydney. N.S.W. and Victoria
have had much to do with the stoppage of trans-
portation to New Caledonia, as the New Hebrides
and the Australian coasts tempt escapees, and it
would be as well not to let the petition go too far
without a corrective.

Tortured by Rheumatism.
Like a thief in the silence of the night
influenza and common cold have carried many to
the silent grave. It is caused by a sudden
check of perspiration by which the stomach is
rendered inactive, and the lungs become loaded
with corrupt matter which enters the blood, and
disease is the result. .Rheumatic fever and rheu-
matic pains are frequently traced to a cold con-
tracted when the system was run down through
indigestion and a disordered stomach. Mr. John
A. Phypers, of 173 Pitt-street, Redfern, Sydney,
graphically describes his own experience along
this line, and we cannot do better than use his
own words, which are:—“Some years ago,
while fishing on the Hawkesbury, I got very wet
and had to sit in my clothes for hours, the conse-
quence was a frightful attack of influenza, which
ended with rheumatic fever. I was under the
doctors for months. I seemed to get better,
but shortly afterwards I got back the old rheu-
matic pains, and from that time (about five years
ago), I suffered more or less, the pain almost
driving me mad. I tried everything; tried
rubbing the affected parts with oil, and outward
applications, but seemed to get worse and worse,
until I tried Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills, and
they speedily proved effectual. After taking one
or two bottles the pains in my legs and back
became less acute, and in a short time I was
able to get about again. Besides curing the
rheumatism, the pills improved my health
generally, and I may say that I am practically
cured altogether.” Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills are a perfect blood purifier, and a positive
cure for biliousness, indigestion, constipation,
headaches, sallow complexion, liver and kidney
troubles, piles, pimples and blotches, and for
female ailments. Put up in amber bottles and
the full name blown thereon.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
For the present, there is no more complaining
that test-matches in England are' limited to three days
this season’s experience has been quite satisfactory to
Darling and 00. In the first test, the Englishmen won
the toss and scored 876 for 9 wickets, the innings being
declared c.osed. Then the Australians lost 10 wickets for
86, and 2 for. 42. .At a rough calculation, the three days’
rule saved them from a defeat by an innings l and 200
runs. The weather conditions had given them no earthly
chance of winning, therefore it came fair enough that the
delays caused by rain enabled them to “ draw.” In the
second match the wicket was wet at the outset, and only
the best of weather could have made it otherwise than un-
favorable to the side that lost the toss. The Englishmen
won the toss. When they had scored 102 for two wickets the
tv? own . a gain and lasted on and off for three days.
Did the Australians complain that the three days* rule
relieved them of the necessity for waiting until a fourth
or fifth day in order to finish the match on a quagmire
when their opponents had led off with 102 for 2 wickets ?
Not much. Darling won the toss in the remaining three
tests, and, although there were interruptions every time
caused by rain, or failure of the light, it was possible to
finish the three games. Australia won two.
A three days’ test-match in Engiand provides
what would be four days’ play under the drag-it-out sys-
tem prevailing in this country. To get a hard, “ true ”
painfully true —wicket for three successive days you must
have at least four clear days of sunshine —a rare occur-
rence in England, anyhow. When you have got your
three true-wicket days, which are equal in length to four
Australian days, it may be fairly argued that the
side which cannot win the game within that time
has not shown cause why the other side should
get beaten. If a game were stretched into a fourth
or fifth day of continuous play the side which
got last strike would be almost certain to suffer the disad-
vantage of a somewhat worn wicket, and truly it wouldn’t
deserye to get beaten by time. In such cases a time-
limit is a fair thing. The English three-days’ rule prac-
tically tells the side that wins the toss to go in and beat
the other side, or rest contented with a draw if it doesn’t
get beaten itself. All things considered, the rule makes
for fairness in the rainy country. Lengthen the day’s
play, and it would be a good rule for Australian cricket.
What can be more ridiculous than the conclusion of some
Australian matches? The teams battle for four days,
and at the end of the fourth day the team that lost the
toss has a fair chance to make the (say) 150 runs required
for winning the match. A deluge of rain comes. No
play on the fifth day. On the seventh day, half-a-dozen
wickets fall for 50 runs, or less. The weather wins the
match for the team that had the luck of the toss.
In the natural course of things Time mostly aids
the side which batted first on a sound wicket, if the game
is carried beyond three days. But with a three days’ limit-
rule Time mostly aids the team that lost the toss, and it
thus tends to equalise the luck. The Australian system
of allowing a whole week, if required, to inter-State
matches whilst limiting each day’s play to 5 hours is a
rotten gate-money system. So there now ! The cricket
reporters for the newspapers sing the praises of this rotten
system because it suits them very nicely. Likewise the
old fogies whose sole remaining dissipation in life is
watching the game and talking about it between the acts.
The game cannot be too slow a tragedy for them, and as
for the unfairness of the present system—well, those iged
enthusiasts prefer it. It enables them to indulge the
oftener in their gay and sparkling truism that “Cricket
is a funny game.”
• * * »
Some fifth test-match trifles : The game ended
sensationally, and is certainly one of the most remarkable
of English-Australian tussles. Judging'by the queer
happenings, the wicket was decidedly doubtful. Trumper
and Duff broke the ice, and Duff, Hill and Darling were
all out to Hirst by the time 69 had been scored, and of
these Trumper had contributed 31. When Noble joined
Trumper the score shot up to 82, and then a slow one
from Hirst ended Trumper’s precarious existence of one
hour-and-a-quarter, during which he managed to make
42. It took four men to make the other 40 of the meagre
score bo far accumulated. It took Gregory and Noble
half-an-hour to make even three against the bowling of
Hirst and Braund, and eventually Hirst added Gregory’s
scalp to his little lot at a cost of 23. The first innings
lasted shr. 35min. and resulted in a total of 324. Best
score was Trumble’s, who made 64 (n. 0.)
Second day saw Palairet and Maclaren begin
for England on a wicket sodden with a night’s heavy
rain, and something like a debacle ensued. Hirst hit out,
and knocked-up 43 in 45min., and Lockwood flashed
through 25 in 25min., but in three hours the Englishmen
were all out for 183. Australia went in, and eight wickets
fell for 114 in 2h. 45min. Next day, Armstrong was
biffed out early, without scoring; and when Kelly, the
last man, had reached 7, Trumble got 1.b.w., and the
innings was over for 121, leaving the Englishmen 263
behind. When five English wickets were down for 48,
Britannia’s prospects looked particularly blue, and the
appearance of the unreliable Jessop with Jackson was
not calculated to get the Lion’s tail up a little bit. But
in 75min. the Unreliable had hit up 104, when Nohle
caught him. He had hit one five and 17 fours. When
eight wickets had fallen, England only wanted 19 to win,
and people were holding their hair. Hirst and Rhodes
steadily piled them up, and England won by one wicket.
Hirst had batted for 80min., and made 68 (n. 0.), of which
24 were made off six hits.
* • • ■»
Australians only wanted to win latest to be
level-pegging with England in test-match successes.
* * • »
Vic. Trumper is a Crown-st. (Sydney) schoolboy
and first showed his cricket promise as the pride of the
school eleven at the early age of 13.
* • * *
The manner in which Darling worked his
bowling in the second innings of the last test looks, half
the globe away, incomprehensible. Saunders and
Trumble bowled unchanged until the Englishmen had
only 100 to win. The result, of course, might have been
all the same, anyway.
'* * * *
“ The third test-match,” writes The Bulletin’s
London correspondent, was the best and most satis-
factory defeat of England-at-home for very many years.
The papers were unanimous in speaking of the ‘ brilliant
victory,’ and their praise was almost unalloyed. They
were also terribly agreed that O. B. Fry is at present not
test-class—well, five runs. in four test-matches ! Read-
ing his column of stuff each day in the newspapers
explains much, and proves that, for batting averages, the
pen is not mightier than the willow. Listen how he got
out, first innings, as related by himself : ‘ It was almost
impossible to see the ball from the pavilion end. When,
after making a single off Noble, I faced Saunders, I found
I could not get any sight of the ball. Playing purely at
the bowler’s arm, I missed the ball, which was rather
wide, lost my balance, and was stumped. ... It seems
contrary to cricket to make excuses, but the light was
very sorry.’ Of course, a batsman who goes wobbling
about hitting at a bowler’s arm which is not there is more
than likely to be bowled by a ball that is. It was a great
match and a glorious win.”
* * * »
Consider Hirst’s share in the glory of England’s
success over Australia. In both innings he came to the
rescue at a critical juncture—first, by making 43 and
saving a follow-on, and next his extra strong nerve and
cool judgment undoubtedly won the match. Put together
Hirst’s runs for the two innings totalled 101 for once out.
His bowling, too, on the first day resulted excellently—
five wickets fell to him on a batsman’s pitch for 77, which
compares favorably with Trumble’s 8 for 65 under con-
ditions that favored the bowler. Wherefore Tate’s in-
clusion and Hirst’s shutting out from the fourth test is
inexplicable at this distance.
* • • *
Australia-M.C.C. match started at Lords,
Thursday, last week. M.C.C. batted first, and scored
212 ; highest individual notchings being 60 and 27. Howell
got 6 for 105 and Armstrong 3 for 53. Australians made
427, towards which Hill brought 136, Gregory 86, and
Noble 70. Armstrong bagged 6 for 44 and Trumble 3 for
61. Visitors succeeded by an innings and 34 runs. Pre-
vious match this tour between same sides was a draw,
with scores: M. 0.0., 240 and 280 (innings closed); Aus-
tralia, 271 and 3 wickets for 217. Latest M.C.O. team was
remarkably weak, compared with first .one, and its easy
walloping no surprise. Big county matches probably
absorbed most of the best players. Hill made his fourth
century of the tour, and Noble reached his 1000 runs ; he
is now second in the batting averages, Gregory’s 87
against M. 0.0. is the best done by him so far in England
this time.

Cabled that projected tour of ’Murka by Aus-
tralian XI. has been abandoned ; they’ll take in Westralia,
homeward bound, instead. Strange no cricket combi-
nation from this section has visited S’Africa, though
invasions by Englishmen are common.
* « * *
How blindly turf - scribes generally accept
official pronouncements! Timekeeper’s clocking of crack
lepper Col. Shillinski’s last mile of recent Caulfield G.N.
Steeple shows he got over it and at least four fences—
possibly five—in 1.56 J, carrying the backbreaker of 13.2.
A Carbine twice over couldn’t do this, under the condi-
tions ; it’s questionable, too, whether, with only half the
weight, Shillinski would clear a mile and the same
obstacles in the time. A similar mistake occurred when
Arcadia won last year ; he was made to do the finishing
mile of the four in 1.55| or thereabouts, and the state-
ment was laughed at everywhere. Small wonder that
seasoned touts, racecourse guns and the like won’t have
the official watch anywhere at any price, and usually
place “form "above the evidence of the best “ticker”
ever held by the most capable man.
* * * *
Adelaide Grand National meet ended Saturday.
Tote investments reached £10,497, and, compared with
last year’s total for two days, evidenced a shortage of
t'2948. Express and Convoy were made hot for the
Steeple, but Sir Oliver—Myrtle nag Supple Jack got
home easily and paid better than 20 to 1. Carlyon—Lady
Doris colt Troubridge showed high-class form in annexing
the Mayor’s Mile, and Carlyon—Sylvan Queen colt Caer-
leon, humping top weight and' lining up at odds on,
secured the 2yr.-old event.
* * • •
In view of coming Spring’s turf events, running
of some prads at Rosehill on Saturday was impressive.
Manner in which handsomely formed Air Motor appro-
priated Clyde Handicap made her look particularly
“good goods "for the Epsom, next month. Stillwater
showed marvellous improvement on recent form by fairly
spread-eagling Parramatta Mile field. With only 6.8 to
hump in the Metrop. he ha 3 certainly much better than
an outside chance. Top-rung quality nags disputed w.f.a.
Rawson Stakes—such a swell lot hasn’t lined up for many
a day. Blue Metal, Felicitous, Sir Leonard, Glenore,
Patronage, Abington, Horace, Brakpan and Abundance
were there. Brakpan was first choice. Big odds could
have been had about Patronage (allowed 71bs. through
never previously having won a w.f.a. race) till some
minutes before flag-fall, when coin came freely. Patronage
triumphed with a run that branded him particularly hot
for the Metrop. or one or other of the Cups—as those
pulling the strings desire. He humped 8.11 (91bs. over
his Metrop. and Melb. Cup loads, and 81bs. in excess of
the Caulfield allotment). Cyanide was another worth
noting for future reference ; she annexed August Handi-
cap in hollow style, and landed a big stake for those'
behind her. Cyanide has 8.3 in the Epsom and 7.13 in
Metrop., and should give either or both a big shaking.
• * * *
S.A. tote extension Bill will be amended con-
siderably if Dixon, M.P., has his way, and ’tis said a
recent reckoning up revealed a majority for him.
Summed up, what he wants means legalising bookmakers,
as incorporation of Lottery and Gaming (’75), Totalisator
Repeal, and Gaming Further Suppression (97) Acts is
aimed at; also, he purposes introducing two fresh clauses
which will clearly define a “ place ” and prevent betting,
unless with the consent of clubs licensed to run totes on
particular racecourses.
• * * •
Leading M.L. turfite, Mr. G. C. Stead, tells
that he hasn’t backed one of his nags for 12 years, and
yet no owner in Australasia has been a more consistent
winner.
» • * •
Looks just now as if A. J.C. and V.R.C. Derbies
are going to induce bigger fields and be much more open
than hitherto. Brakpan’s shaping at Rosehill, Saturday,
takes away from the sure-thing appearance he wore the
day before. Recent displays by Abundance, Free State,
Homeward Bound, Loyal and Troubridge create the
impression that every one of them will need some reckon-
ing with, leaving Long Tom, Ceres, Point Piper, Great
Scot, Clytus, The Caretaker, Good Speed, Lord Middle-
ton, Strata Florida and War-i-an out of the question,
though the greater number of those are high class and
not by any means unlikely winners.

Yol. 14 of the Australian Racing Chronicle,
just issued, deals with 882 race-meetings that happened
in sundry parts of Australia and Tasmania between
Jan. 31 and Aug. 1 of the present year. Punters who are
looking for evidence re conflicting “fancies” for the
Spring handicaps had better buy the Chronicle. It may
help them to find a winner, arid at the worst it will add
very little to their loss. The vol. is posted free for
10s. 6d. by J. J. Miller, Post-office Place, Melbourne.
* • • *
Colonel Shilinski is one of the best-bred of the
jumpers. His sire, Zalinski, was by Nordenfeldt (the son
of Musket, who begat Carbine) out of Frailty ; and his
dam, Rosalie, was a daughter of Vengeance, a Caulfield
and Australian Cup winner. Rosalie was of no account
on the turf, running only once, but Zalinski, as all old
sports remember, ran second for the Champion Stakes to
his stable companion, Strathmore, and subsequently won
the Loch Plate.
* * « *
Harry Punch-Billy M‘Kell glove fight’ at
Sydney Gaiety A.C., last week, disclosed a second Joe
Goddard in lightweight Punch. M‘Kell perambulated
generally, but Punch, though bumping up against heavy
ones coming from all ways and often, took them mostly as if
they were nothing, and drove a straight left in that made
t’other : man squirm visibly. Only once (in the third
round) did the fight look like going to M'Kell, and then
he had Punch sagging and rocking ; but latter gathered
himself together and was boss thumper ever after till the
wipe was skied from M'Kell’s corner at the close of the
sixth round. The beaten man went home badly used up ;
but the winner’s iron countenance barely showed a sign of
the bombardment it had stood.
• * • •
Recent scrap ’tween ’Murkan heavyweight
Scanlon and the mining footballer Mick Cullen, at New-
castle, was a tough scrambling riot that was exciting
while it lasted. Cullen shaped crudely, but with wicked-
ness and vim begotten of bruising instinct and unusual
force. But equally strong and much cleverer Scanlon
breasted the cyclonic charges and coolly waited for
openings, through which he mostly scored heavily.
Cullen acted the mad footballer from end to end, while
the house screeched in excitement. In the second round
Cullen sprawled, the result of a smasher on the jaw, and
in the next three minutes a right found the “ point ” and
a left the bingy, with such power that the miner sank
limp and sick, a badly walloped man. Scanlon showed
much better than with M'Coll, better than he has yet
done in Australia, and more in keeping with his Yankee
reputation, but still much below championship form.
• * * *
Extent of boxing boom in Sydney is evidenced
by the fact that every night present week there’s
scrapping at one |or other hall, also that a big athletic
club, with a capital of £15,000, for pug purposes princi-
pally, is in course of construction.
* * * *
Announced that Larry Foley will return to
teaching the gentle art of sto'ush. In the future, heavy-
jawed individuals with the corners knocked off their
beauty will be common sights in Sydney streets. Seems
the first thing that has to be drubbed into the novice is
to keep out of reach of the other fellow’s fists, and the
best way to instil that idea is to make a chopping-block
of the novice aforementioned till he learns what his feet
were given to him by a benign Providence for. Ana
Larry is the man who can bring home that truth quicker
than anyone else on the continent. Anyhow, men who like
real bruising will be glad to hear that the veteran is
returning to his teaching, as the present age Australian
pug is a very crude and inferior product.
* * » •
■A big cycling meeting, is mooted by N.S.W.
League for next season. Among other big things,
a handicap for more money than has ever been
given in any part of the world for. a similar com-
petition will be run off. Matters are in train towards
bringing negro world’s champion bikist Major Taylor
here, and to this end the cable has been freely utilised.
A bait of £lOOO for an Australian tour failed to tempt
him, though that consideration would have ensured his
figuring in eight races. The Federal Cycling Council pur-
poses adding another £5OO, and this, they are satisfied,
will fetch the attraction. Races are to be run on Sydney
Cricket Ground afternoon and night, while three separate
carnivals will be held—in Nov., Jan. and March. Prizes
of £5OO each are to be given in the first two instances,
and £lOOO on the third occasion. It is expected that a
big proportion of the 700 Australian bikists affiliated, as
well as visitors from all parts of the world, will compete.
At least 300 Australians are expected to line up for the
big money.
* • • *
English swimmer J. A. Jarvis’s announcement
that he would, besides the mile, compete only in long-
distance (7 miles) supremacy this season is a nasty knock
for Australians Cavill, Read, and Lane. They went ’Ome
purposely to have a cut at Jarvis. Read and Cavill were
certainly beaten by him in the mile, but Cavill had only
arrived five days before, and Read five weeks
previously. The long swim on the Thames was much
beyond anything either Australian had ever thought of
doing. Both did excellently to finish without having
undergone that special preparation which, long-distance
work at any athletic exercise exacts. Jarvis’s back-down
looks like a bad attack of scare, as previous years he com-
peted everywhere in the British Isles and in everything.
Cavill has now swum himself into form enough to
seriously menace the English amateur champion’s posi-
tion. Oavill’s hundred with Lane, and his half-mile
world’s record a week or two ago, clearly demonstrate
that Jarvis made no mistake in sizing the situation up as
he apparently did, still it doesn’t justify his shabby back-
down.
• * • •
Details of English mile swimming supremacy,
and happenings prior thereto, indicate that Australian
George Read “ chucked it.” Against his 26.51 in the race
Read’s friend and mentor, Farmer, tells that he did 24.40
in a trial, which, strangely enough, was exactly the time
put up by the ultimate winner, Jarvis, in a test of his
capabilities. English files state that Farmer left for
Sydney disgusted, and also left his proteg6 behind.
* * • *
Australian pigeon murderer, Don Macintosh,
made a big splash at London international meet, July 5.
Five-pound sweep, with a £5O cup thrown in, saw him
and Frenchman De Bons, at 33yds. rise, account for 38
other contestants and whack first and second money.
Shooting on for the trophy, Macintosh won with 9 kills.
Later same man shared a £3 sweep with Vernon Barker,
each grassing 61.
**, * *
Hard luck that Bladon’s Braddon’s Blot should
go under by one point in final for N.S.W. National
Coursing Ass. Champion Cup last week to his half-sister,
We Two, whom old “Sam” Bladon gave away as a puppy
to present owner. Same bitch also put “the Blot” out
in Sydney C.C. Cup a week or two since. We Two hasn’t
the toot of Braddon’s Blot, especially if the going be soft,
but she is nearly as fast, and when on the game heaps
closer. Sensational Great Heart, though sized up a world-
beater in some quarters, succumbed to We Two in second
ties without notching a point. He wasn’t, by long chalks,
the dog that gathered in the rich Challenge Stakes a
month ago. Latest was the first time Great Heart had
been led up, or saw more than three points notched in
opposition to him.

Present position of Sydney first-grade Rugby
toe-ball competition is that Glebe and ’Varsity, who
divided last year’s premiership, top the list. This Satur-
day these two will ’rastle, and the result should virtually
decide absolute supremacy.
* * * •
“W.S.T.”: No such thing as luck in life, say
the Gradgrinds who laboriously pile one shilling on
another. But a well-known Western N.S. Wales M.L. A.
can tell another story. In early life he made a small pile
“ backing ’em,” and this led to bold speculation that
landed him in affluence. Circumstances then again, how-
ever, brought him to the bottom of the ladder, but a
week or two ago Fortune gave the wheel another turn.
A visitor upon leaving the member’s house, presented the
youngsters with a sov., and told their mother to put it in
“Tatt.’s.” This she did, with ss. of her own, on the
principle of “ luck in odd numbers.” Ten days later the
news came that a horse was drawn and had run second,
giving a div. of £703 net. With rare discretion the money
was used to purchase a suburban home, with several
acres of land, orchard and garden, and before the price
was all paid, a premium of £2OO was offered by another
buyer, who was a little late in the market. Now, if these
things were not “ luck,” what were they ?
* • « •
“H.P.”: Many cricket fads are being given a
chance of proving their worth during the present English
season. The much-talked-of leg-before-wicket alteration
is being tried in all M.O.C. and Ground matches. The
rule, as it now stands, requires a ball “ to be pitched in a
direct line from wicket to wicket ’’ to enable a batsman
to be given out l.b.w. ; the suggested alteration is that
should a batsman prevent the ball from hitting the
wicket with his legs or any part of his body he shall be
out. The idea is not meeting with favor, and will
certainly be dropped. Another experiment which is
being tried at Lord’s is the painting of the cricket stumps
white. On the whole, it has been favorably received.
* * * *
The humor of Scotch cricket! The Australian
XI. played their match at Edinburgh on a ground which
had a hump in the middle, and tapered away like the
back of a tortoise. The only local player who was not in
a blue funk was a Victorian, studying medicine in “ Auld
Reekie.” When the Australians took the bat they passed
the Scottish total of 109 without losing a wicket. Steven-
son, a local bowler, delivered underhand lobs, and fielded
by turn to his own bowling at mid-on, mid-off, extra
mid-off, and cover-point. Once he got to point, only to
see Noble step back and pull the ball to square-leg.
Queerly reminiscent of the matches played by the first
All England XI. that visited Australia.
* • * *
A Voice from Vic. : Eor rough football, go
on the land, young man, and watch country teams per-
forming. Principal causes—ignorance of the game and
amateur umpiring. Saw two cookie eighteens battling
t’other Saturday. Contestants didn’t bother much about
the “leather ’’—they simply played each other. Slinging,
throwing, tripping, rabbitting, pushing—in fact, any-
thing was allowed ; the supposed umpire only giving one
free kick throughout the afternoon. Excited barrackers
followed the game all over the ground with “ Kick their
livers in!” The modern football patron is twin-brother
to the raging animal who stands outside the ropes and
punctuates a prize-fight with shouts of “ Kill the
blanker! ”
As the strike of Vic. Railway men now seems
unlikely to occur it is presumed that somebody
has been soothed to calmer reflection by a more
peaceful spirit. D.C.L. Whisky again no doubt.
After careful treatment with that unerring
remedy, the best side of everyone’s nature and of
every circumstance comes on top. For nipping
in the bud any looming unpleasantness D.C.L.
should always be on hand. In every emergency
it is The Whisky of Wisdom and Conciliation.
Pastor Dill-Macky’s calves must have lost
their springiness on Tuesday when the news came
that King Ned had given a Crucifix with a figure
of the Virgin Mary to Westminster Abbey. Ned’s
gift seems to be a return for Cardinal Vaughan’s
cute and courtly compliment in calling his
new Cathedral tower “St. Edward’s Tower.”
But the'flattered King is not nearly so slim as the
Cardinal, who leaves himself free to explain to the
faithful (who might be shocked at all avowed
compliment to a Protestant king) that it’s Saint
Edward, not King Edward, at all, at all, whose
glorification is aimed at, whilst Ned’s round-
aboutly-reciprocal gift is distinctly not one that
can be explained away to bigots on his side.
The second reading of the Bill to reform
the Vic. Constitution was passed by the Leg.
Assembly last week. The voting was 79 for and
5 against, but this is no indication of what will
happen the bill in committee. Like the Irvine
Ministry, which fathers it, the measure is a sad
mixture of rank toryism and liberalism. Liberals
rightlyobject to a number of dangerous concessions
which the“ Bill makes to the 48 Rich Men, and
Tories cannot bring themselves to swallow the
double dissolution and the joint sitting. Then
there is not a man in the House who really believes
that the number of representatives should be re-
duced to 56. No explanation has been given why
the Govt, selected that number. It seems to be
agreed that Irvine and Shiels made it 56 so as
to save their own skins at the Ministerial elections.
Even if the measure gets through the Assembly
in anything like its present shape it will be thrown
out by the 48 Rich Bosses and the Irvine Govt,
hasn’t got the stomach to fight them.
A Nasty Jar from Johannesburg!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
[Some of the influential mining houses upon the Rand
are said to be preparing the way for the introduction of
Chinese labor.— Vide Cable.]
When you rushed to the ranks, old chap,
When you went to the war,
What did you think the row was about ?
What were you fighting for ?
Read that cable there, old chap,
Secret’s all out now 1
You went to the war
To slaughter the Boer
For the sake of a blessed Chow.
When you went to the war, old man,
You thought, and so did I,
You went to fight for the English right
With the Union Jack on high.
Of course, we knew of the Cockney Jew,
But gulped that down, somehow;
And nobody thought
The fight was fought
For the sake of a pigtail Chow !
When you went to the war, my friend,
You went in a time of need,
“ With impulse grand to lend a hand
To the boys of the bull-dog breed ? ”
You fought for the Anglo-Saxon race,
You fought for the Grand Old L ind
To help in the fight
For the Empire’s right
To the rule of the Golden Rand !
And now it’s the man with the mustard skin
And the man with the almond eye
Who comes along, with a cautious grin,
To work where the white men die ;
To gather the grain that the white man reaped
With the sweat of the white man’s brow.
The war’s at an end
And the loot, my friend,
Reserved for the docile Chow !
Oh, you’re all right for a long, fierce fight,
And the chance of a dose of lead !
To sleep in the soaking rain all night
With a pack-saddle under your head !
Oh, you can live on the potted dog,
And the beef that hums with stink ;
But when there’s a show
Of reward, you know,
Clear out! —make room for the Chink !
W. T. GOODGE.

Colliery Disasters and their Cause.
A working miner (writing on Monday) tells The
Bulletin this (name of colliery suppressed, but
the Government inspector can have it):
I and mate, with three other pairs of men, are driving
four bords towards a large area of worked-out ground,
usin°* open lights. Both the under-manager and deputy
warn us every day, on their rounds of inspection, to be
very careful about the lights when we break through,
as there is sure to he gas in the old workings. We asked
about a month ago for safety-lamps, as the gas
might reach our lights before we had time to put
them out, but we were told that the gas in this pit is very
“ slow,” and, besides, there might be no gas at all. Since
the Kembla disaster we renewed our request for safety-
lamps, and were told that there was not a spare one on
the premises. Someone then suggested that the under-
manager should give us a long drill to bore ahead and
test the air through the bore hole, but as that would cost
a small amount of extra pay per yard, it was not approved
of. However, we were told that we could use
our own drills, which bore only just four feet and
are, therefore, useless for the present purpose. So the
matter stands thus : If there is no gas—against expecta-
tations—in the old workings, or if it is slow enough to let
us blow out the lights, there will be no accident; other-
W Now, is it reasonable or is it criminal to risk a catas-
trophe—perhaps more disastrous than the recent one if
six or eight safety-lamps or the expenditure of
a few shillings in boring would insure safety l ldo
not think the under-manager or deputy is to blame,
because they seem to feel very anxious themselves.
I have no burning desire to see this statement published,
but, if you consider that it would be instrumental in
causing an investigation, or act as an effective caution
in similar cases, I would be willing to lose my job over it,
and even chance being called an informer.
Assuming the correctness of this,. there is.no need
for any Royal Commission to inquire into the
cause of N.S. W. colliery accidents in general; the
cause is as plain as a pike-staff.
Another cherished theory exploded.
Stated that the 800 men employed in Paris sewers
suffer less from sickness than the average popu-
lation ; further that they are seldom or never
attacked by zymotic diseases.
The Westralian State Labor bureau seems
to be really doing something to bring employers
and workmen together. This year, down to the
end of July, engagements had been fixed for 1106
men, of almost all trades. The bureau’s report
confirms the private news that there are not
enough jobs now to go round ; practical trades-
men especially are having a bad time “owing to
there being many arrivals from the other states.
Premier Irvine is the weakest of leaders.
During the whole Reform Bill debate he .was in
total ignorance of the state of mind of his own
following, and at one time Peacock took matters
in hand and told the nerveless Irvine that some-
thing serious might happen unless he controlled
the House a little better. Of course the Smiler
wouldn’t have given him this gratuitous informa-
tion had there been any intention to let the thing
happen until the present Govt, has properly
stewed in its “ reform and retrenchment ” fat. At
the present rate it will not.be very long before
Irvine and co. have been nicely browned. Then
A lick the Gleeful will step up and eat them, and
it is tolerably safe to say that he will be helped in
this congenial task by Mr. D. Syme, of the Age.
Harry Morant and his sorry fate, told of
in “Bushman and Buccaneer” (now in its second
edition). The history of the Breaker and his
verses and letters, including photo, reproduction
of the Petersburg gaol MS. Price one shilling.

The Boer generals visiting England seem
to have been treated with the extreme of what
“society” would call “condescension.’ Daily
paper account :
The King received the Boer generals on board the
Viet iria and Albert. His Majesty was on deck when the
Boers arrived, and, seeing them approach, went towards
the gangway to receive them. After they were presented
his Majesty shook hands with each of the generals, and,
later, introduced them to the Queen and Princess Vic-
toria.
King Ned went to meet them —a mark, (among
people who take any count of these things) of
equality and comradeship. The King seems to
have acted like a gentleman, and the “filthy,
barbarous, treacherous, murdering, ignorant, de-
graded, superstitious ” Boers acted with a dignity
which bears out all the good opinions that the few
reasonable people in Australia last year had
formed of them. They neither grovelled nor
gushed.
When, two years ago, R. D. Meagher,
M.L.A., the solicitor who was struck off N.S.W.
Supreme Court rolls for having conspired to
defeat the course of justice in the memorable
Dean poisoning case, applied to the Court for
re-instatement, after he had been for several
years outside in the lay world, his application
was refused. Chief Justice Darley, however,
remarked that, though it was premature tor
Meagher to apply for re-admission, such
an application might be favorably con-
sidered “in a few, a very few yearn.
The ex-solicitor, who doubtless reckoned he had
impressed the Court with his formidable petitions
(signed, among others, by 113 M’s.P. and 113
attorneys) decided to re-apply after two more
years had passed, and this application was heard
the other day. But the result showed that the
Chief-Justice (now absent from the country) was
the only Judge inclined to grant him mercy ; the
other Judges were as adamant, and unanimously
refused hi 3 appeal. The moral of which is that a
man may be good enough to be a M.P. or an
alderman, and yet not be fit to be a trusted officer
of the Supreme Court —the one institution of the
land which should be emphatically without re-
proach or blemish.

Read the Words
If You Please, Miss, Give Me Heaven.
“ Papa, I’m so sad and lonely,”
Sobbed a tearful little child ;
“ Since dear mamma’s gone to Heaven,
Papa, darling, you’ve not smiled.
I will speak to her and tell her
That we want her to come home;
Just you listen, and I’ll call her
Through the telephone :
Chorus—
-1 If you please, Miss, give me Heaven—for my Mamma’s
there;
You can find her with the angels on the golden stair.
She’ll be glad it’s me who’s speaking—call her, won’t you,
please,
For I want to surely tell her we’re so lonely here.”
When the girl received this message,
Coming o’er the telephone,
How her heart thrilled in that moment,
And the wires seemed to moan ;
“ I will answer, just to please her :
‘ Yes, dear heart, I’ll soon come home.’ ”
“ Kiss me, Mamma, kiss your darling,
Through the telephone.”
Chorus—“ If you please, Miss,” etc.

The latest New York notion is a cold-air-
servlce for every house. An invention has been
patented whieh will enable a cold-air-place to be
fitted to every room just as conveniently as a fire-
place. A tube from outside will connect with the
“ grate,” which will take-in up to 20,000 cubic ft.
of air a day at outside temperature and deliver it
inside as required, down to 18 Eahr. Cost about
equal to that of a fire with coal at 3 dol. a-ton.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par-
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance,
is solicited.
“ Burglar Bill ” writes :
Re Bulletin comments on the number of insane persons
in gaol. The Separate-cell system may have done much
in keeping prisoners from the influence of older “ hands,”
but the complete isolation of the confinee from 4 p.m. till
6.30 a.m. has (especially with boys) fostered practices
which are held to be largely conducive to the increase
of insanity in prisons.
With the now abolished separate treatment of nine
months “solitary” for all sentences of three years and
upwards, the unfortunate wretch had one hour out of his
cell in the 24 (during his non-working hours, between 3
p.m. and 8 a.m.). Next morning, he was allowed a book
(all light reading barred). Certainly, gaol is not supposed
to be a pleasure-resort, hut separate confinement without
interesting reading-matter furnishes a fine forcing-ground
for lunacy.
And the ordinary gaol doctor ! Well, I have seen him
get through 16 patients in 2Jmins.! He generally prides
himself on his smartness in not letting a prisoner “ bluff”
him, and doesn’t take much pains to discriminate between
the “ malingerer ” and the really sick man.
“ R. 8.” : Stated recently that the Jews
are the healthiest of people. In the face of the
facts of their in-breeding and rarely doing any
other work than office-scheming, I doubted this
much, and now I find that, of all people, the
Hebrew is the most subject to nervous and mental
maladies, which, Dr. Kroeplin states, are directly
traceable to consanguineous marriages. “Out of
100 cases of paralysis, there are 20 among Jews.”
Another specialist found that Hebrews furnished
25 per cent, of the paralytic cases in his experi-
ence. Of 1219 cases of mental aberration examined
at the psychiatric clinic at Vienna, 134 were
among Jews—lo’9 per cent. Yet, although alco-
holic madness forms the largest proportion of the
general total, not one Jewish instance.is known.
The psychosis of the Jew is the type known as
hereao-degenerative. Pilez, the great psycholo-
gist, of Austria, says that the Jews’s tendency to
mental trouble is aggravated by his keenness in
the struggle for money,
T. W. Chambers writes Irom Milparinka:
I take exception to the allusion made to the proposed
Cobar-Wilcannia railway in your issue of 26th ult., on the
ground that the comment is not fair. Firstly, you deal
with 120 miles of the proposed line instead of 160, cutting
off its connection with Cobar altogether. It would be
just as reasonable to speak of the paying nature of the
line from Bathurst to Sydney by stopping short at Pen-
rith. Secondly, you omit to say that when the line is
brought to Wilcannia it will be within 60 miles of
the prosperous settlement of White Cliffs, a mining
township with from 2000 to 3000 inhabitants, and
that the intention is to carry the line on to
this and other mining centres and the unexplored mineral
country lying west of Darling River up as far as Mt.
Browne. Furthermore, the Bill proposes to take power
to levy a special rate on the pastoral country through
which the railway will pass corresponding with the
advantage accruing to the pastoralists by its construc-
tion. My experience of the Darling country dates back
to the year ’65, and I admit that the expenditure of £4OOO
per mile on the proposed line is not justified—light lines
of railway are all that will be needed for the next 50
years in this Darling country, and with such an
outlay as would be required for a light line
interest and working expenses will be assured from
the start. In years to come, when the water flowing
down the Darling (spasmodic though it may be) is utilised
as it should be, the production of this western country
will be largely increased ; meanwhile the line is urgently
required to assist struggling settlers in both the pastoral
and mining industries, and it is to be hoped that the
present Government will reign long enough to carry out
their Public Works policy in regard to the Western Divi-
sion of this State, despite all the adverse criticism that
is being showered upon them.
As regards the above: (1) The fact that Cobar is
at the eastern end of the proposed line doesn’t
show that it will pay, unless Cobar is going to
send traffic into the howling wilderness. A line
from Cobar to the wilderness, or from Melbourne
to Sahara, or from Sydney to Gehenna, won’t
necessarily pay . owing to the fact that a town
is at one end of it. The Goulburn-Cooma line
doesn’t pay, though it has a city at one end.
(2) The prospects of a line must be judged to some
extent by the country it will pass through. (3)
The Bill says nothing about any extension to
White Cliffs, or Mount Browne, or even Sheol.
But if the line is going out into the region of
diminishing population and one inhabitant to
five square miles, then so much the worse.
(4) A special rate may be levied on the pastoral-
ists ; there is no difficulty about levying anything
—on paper. But there is a very poor show of col-
lecting any of the money levied. (5) The work
should only be built out of revenue. It has hardly
a ghost of a chance of paying, but it is an act
of charity, just like old age pensions. Charity
shouldn’t come out of loans.

Mr. Isaacs, M.H.R., brought up in the
Reps., the other day, the question whether Lord
Tennyson was properly sworn in as Actmg-
Governor-General. Attorney-General Deakin re-
plied that Mr. Chamberlain had sent the neces-
sary instructions by cable. The history of the
affair is that Mr. Crouch asked about the matter
in the House in June last. In consequence Mr.
Deakin cabled Mr. Barton, who saw Mr. Cham-
berlain, who got the King to sign a warrant to
appoint a Victorian Judge to act instead, of the
Federal Justice (not yet appointed) required by
the original Governor-General’s instructions.
What would have happened if Crouch had not
called attention to the matter ?

There is a project afoot for bringing meat
from the Northern Territory down to Adelaide,
where the butchers have “ ringed ” mutton chops
up to 7d. a pound and beef-steaks to nearly a
shilling. Why is Australian meat so much cheaper
in England than it is in the Commonwealth ?

It is said that the King wished for a
Regency to be appointed at once if his own illness
was found to cause any delay in State business;
but the Prince of Wales showed deep reluctance
to assume the office. The Mighty Atom does not
value himself at the high figure of the grovellers
who surround him.
The Bendigo spinster, a domestic servant,
whose baby was found wailing in a paddock 24
hours after she had dumped it down to take its
chance of death or rescue, seems to have struck
Judge A’Beckett in one of his softest moods. In
excuse for her heartless abandonment of the un-
welcome little stranger the girl pleaded that she
herself had been heartlessly treated by the child s
father. His Honor, after sternly remarking that
the sins of the father didn’t affect a baby s
claim to natural affection from its mother, sen-
tenced prisoner to 12 months “ hard. He might
as well have raised the question whether the bad
young man didn’t temper his iniquity with pre-
science when he bolted from his victim.
Surely there is no marriage made in Heaven for the
sort of girl who plants her month-old babe in a
lonely paddock on a winter day, and then journeys
away, cursing “ the villain who deserted her.

There is already in operation a Bush veldt
Carbineers’ curse. The man who really originated
the corps has, it is reported from S’Africa, lost his
reason and been put under restraint.
Curious medical item from Maoriland in
the dailies: —
While a young lady was recently taking a hot-air bath
at Hanmer Springs, the gas ignited, and every bit
of skin was burned off her body. The sum of £350 now
appears on the estimates as compensation.
Seeing that the loss of less than half the skin sur-
face (to say nothing of any shock) will kill the
average human being, this lady, who seems to be
alive and collecting skin-compensation, is indeed a
marvel.
Italy has just struck the suspicious fact
that it is still paying pensions to 18,421 alleged
survivors of the campaign of 1848. That was 54
years ago, and the Italian Govt, begins to feel that
it is being taken in.
Mr. Barling, of N.S.W. Public Service
Board, will have well repaid the cost of his trip
to England if he succeeds, as he is at present
sanguine of doing, in placing N.S.W. hardwoods
on London market for street-paving. The im-
mense quantity of paving-woods used in England
opens an illimitable field. Barling, Works
O’Sullivan, and Agent-General Copeland are the
trio on the job.
