A Nepean Idyll.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In his mouth was a short pipe. He was sitting
before an easel beyond the bridge where pine and
willow cast dark green shadows over hummock
and river. A ruffled coote clung to the shade of
a large wet stone almost opposite him. Lower
down a brindled bull moaned in the hot stillness,
the wet reeds brushing his red flanks, his surly
head bruised against the bank.
The artist made sudden strokes with the brush ;
his brow creased as his eye drank in the Dantesque
shadow of the bull.
A fence creaked, wires twanged, a small na,ked
boy flashed suddenly into the near perspective;
the coote vanished, and the artist looked up
sharply. “ Hullo, sonny ! ” softening at the sight
of pure flesh —“ having a wash?”
The naked boy appeared too suspicious of the
artist to answer immediately. He splashed towards
a snag in mid-stream, thrashing the water hand
and foot. The artist painted on, and secretly
thanked the gods for the sending of the naked
cherub. “Wonder if he will stay there for a
while?” he muttered, “just as he is. All the
posing in the world wouldn’t give him such an air
of unconscious impudence. . . . Walks right into
my picture unasked, unbidden, and yet there are
men who say the gods are dead.”
After a while the artist discovered that the
boy’s persistent splashing disturbed the river sur-
face until the long shadows quaked and dripped.
For a moment his eye gloated on the live, warm
flesh silhouetted against the willow-fringe. But
. . . the imp’s feet were tearing the shadows to
smithereens.
“I wish,” he began, blandly, “you wouldn’t
smack the water for a while, sonny, I’m—l’m
doing something with it.”
“It’s our crik.” The cherub leaned back
sulkily. “An’ if mother kitches yer drorin’ it
she’ll shot rocks at yer.” And, to the artist’s
grief the cherub slipped from the snag and stood
waist deep in the stream.
“Surely,” smiled the artist, “you will allow
me to take the shadows back to town ? ”
“Yer better not.” The cherub frowned.
“ Mother ’ll rock yer if she kitches yer takin’ our
shadders. We ain’t goin’ ter buy criks an’
shadders fer yer to dror. See ! ”
There was a long silence in which the artist
painted with great rapidity. In the sun-spaces
of the dark bush beyond he saw three magpies
fluttering towards a lean, drab fence.
A cloud drew across the sun like a ragged
shawl; the river turned leaden-gray ; a thin trail
of wild duck streaked from where the cranes were
standing in the river. A black and white heifer
passed near, and the artist heard the grass being
torn in rich mouthfuls from the earth.
The bull nosed the bank and moaned, “That’s
our bull.” The cherub grinned at the artist,
“He’s a terror. Git aout, Bill!” He hit the
beast’s flank with a stone. “ Git aout, Bill! ”
Bill, the bull, swished from the cool reeds and
trotted sullenly into the dark scrub.
The cherub thrashed his way into midstream
and eyed the canvas with a degree of contempt
that soon passed into the freezing stage. “Are
yer paintin’ me?” he demanded. “You wait
till I git me clothes on. I’ll git mother ter rock
yer.” Another incautious step took him into a
hole, and it was evident he could not swim.
Turning from his canvas, the artist saw the
cherub battling for life in the deep water. Then
he remembered that he himself was unable to
swim. “Boy,” he said, hoarsely,' “is this an
infernal joke ? ” But the boy began to drown
with such dreadful earnestness that the artist was
seized with a shaking of the limbs. He already
saw himself carrying the small body to the door of
the slab hut at the end of the paddock : he saw
the cherub’s mother gaping in agony at her dead-
son.
The boy continued to drown.
The artist turned in frantic haste towards the
paddpck. ‘ ‘ Help ! help !” he shouted.
A gaunt man with a dusty slouched hat was
crossing the footbridge lower down ; his hands
in his pockets jingled the two-and-six he’d
probably received for the last load of hay. The
artist seized him almost fiercely. “For God’s
sake, man, can you swim ? ”
“Swim!” shouted the man; “I’ll swim any
bloke on the river fer a hundred quid,
down now ! ” His voice had a far-away sound, as
though his dense beard muffled it.
“There’s a boy drowning,” gasped the artist,
“up there under the willows. I’ll give you five
shillings if you’ll bring him out.”
The man with the dusty beard ran to the bank
flinging his coat to the earth. “ Make it ten bob,
mister,” he said earnestly, “and I’ll fetch him
out.”
The boy continued to drown.
“ Hurry, for heaven’s sake ! ’’shouted the artist.
“Five shillings is all the cash I have at present.”
“Ten bob,” said the man, stubbornly, “will
only buy me a new pair of pants. Make it ten
bob.”
The boy curled over in the water ; his head sank
into the reeds. The artist tore his watch from
bis pocket and thrust it into the man’s hand.
“Fetch the boy put, for God’s sake ! T a ke every-
thing I have.”
The man with the whiskers in his voice placed
the watch in a secure spot, then dashed into the
water and brought the cherub to the bank. Then
he took the watch from its nook, bit the case to
see if it was real silver, and began pumping water
from the boy. “This kid,” he said, turning to
the artist, “gits drowned about every fortnight.
He’s a terror for gittin’ drowned.”
- The boy opened his eyes suddenly and looked
into the man’s face. “Hullo, father!” he said.
ALBERT DORRINGTON.
A Cock-fight at Scanlan’s.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Scanlan was a man of importance—something
between a cockie and a squatter. He owned
Wonbadgery, where cock-fighting was a favorite
Sunday indulgence. Scanlan’s champion was an
old bird he called Draggletail. The name fitted ;
it seldom had more than one shabby feather
waving at the back of it.
The heir to the dungheap was a beautiful
young bird. His name was Bill. Scanlan took
much pains to teach him the way a rooster should
go ; but Draggletail had kicked so much terror
into him that he rarely had company except when
he found a hen away out by herself somewhere.
He always took the precaution to look carefully
around before stooping to pick up a grain of corn ;
and, when he occasionally mustered courage to
crow, he would stop in the middle of it, to ascer-
tain if Draggletail creeping, up behind
him. He was the last to roost at night, and the
first off in the morning ; even then the most
horrible squawks would come from him in his
sleep, and sometimes he would start running and
nearly brain himself against a tree before he woke-
up. He was dreaming of Draggletail.
Scanlan was much troubled on Bill’s account.
“I’d give ah ’onest fiver.ter see Bill turn round
an’ knock slops out o’ that old dog,” he said, as
Bill started across the 40-acre paddock on one of
his wild stampedes. I suggested whitewashing
as an effective means of bringing on a battle. I
explained the idea, and Scanlan chuckled m
anticipation.
On Sunday he whitened the old fellow from
head to foot, and when the coat was dry set him
at liberty, and threw out some grain. Bill came
sidling up, looking suspiciously round for Draggle-
tail. The latter spied him, and immediately took
the offensive. Bill thought it was a newcomer,
and stood rigid until the intruder was within
reach. Then he let fly, and Drag, was knocked
kicking on his back.
The old chap got up slowly, and stood glaring
at Bill, while his head shook, and peculiar
noises came out of his throat. Nothing in the
world was ever more surprised. Then in-
dignation filled his feathered breast, and he made
a furious charge at the presumptuous cockerel.
Bill .met him again with a staggering drive.
The veteran couldn’t understand it. For another
moment his head shook as if he was trying to
swallow something. Some of his favorite hens
began to cackle at his downfall, and that seemed
to rile the ancient more than anything. He put
down his head, flung out his neck-feathers, and
fairly flew at his rival, but only to be knocked
sprawling for the third time. Draggle thought
there must be something radically wrong some-
where ; he surveyed the youngster with a critical
eye. It was Bill sure enough, the fellow he had
thumped a score of times. Where had Bill got
his courage and prowess from ?
Drag, shook the dust off himself, and seemed to
pluck up courage from the memory of former vic-
tories. Then the pair went at it hammer-and-
tongs, and for 10 minutes it was the best cock-
fight ever witnessed on Wonbadgery. Scanlan
danced with delight. The veteran had met his
Waterloo, and Bill was licking him out of recog-
nition, kicking skin and feathers off him, and
jumping on him all over the yard.
“Good on yer, Bill!” yelled Scanlan, “kick
the old (beauty’s) lights out! Well done, Bill!
Well done//”
Bill started to crow in triumph, but stopped
short, and a scared look shot into his blinking
eyes. The whitewash had all been belted off
Draggletail, and Bill recognised him. He stood
straight up, his neck stretched, his head jerking
from side to side, and chucked in a scared manner.
His comb was a shade paler than usual, and he
began to back away.
Draggle had regained breath, and came sidling
up to make one more desperate attempt to main-
tain his reputation. Then, with a frantic screech,
Bill turned and sped across the paddock with the
stride of an emu; and after him full tilt went
Draggletail.
Scanlan was speechless, and stared after them
with his mouth open. A mile from the house was
a round swamp, and we could just make out the
birds, as they crossed the clear spaces, pelting
round it. All at once they disappeared.
“Well, I’m d—d!” said Scanlan, straighten-
ing-up. “ Put my saddle on Bowler, Paddy.”
We saw him ride some distance round the
swamp, then turn suddenly and ride into it.
When he pulled-up he stared at the water and
back at the house alternately. Finally he picked
up two limp-looking birds by the necks, and when
he bashed Bowler across the ribs with them we
knew there was peace at last between the two
roosters.
EDWARD S. SORENSON.
Tnough the S’ African war is now a back
number, this note, which comes from Maoriland,
and is inspired by a recent Bulletin item, will
stand perusal:—■
I have just returned from S’Africa, where as sergeant in
the Prince of Wales’ Light Horse (the Maorilanders)
and as special intelligence scout with various columns, I
came in contact with officers and men of all classes,
belonging to irregular and regular bodies. I had, on
occasion, men under my command who had served in the
Canadian Scouts and in Strathcona’s Horse —the roughest
lot of “ boys ” that drew a bead upon the enemy on the
British side. Our orders, when engaged against the
rebels in Cape Colony, were not to take any prisoners in
kharki. That was the only case I can call to mind of any-
thing like severity of treatment of the enemy. It was
only by the greatest difficulty that, in some cases, the
men were prevented by the officers from retaliating on the
enemy (a rebel enemy) for certain dastardly acts. The
murdering of sentries by single snipers or farmers at
whose houses the troops had bought bread the day before
is not war, and has never been considered as such. I
have every reason to believe, since several told me who
said they were present upon the occasion, that the men
of Strathcona’s Horse did hang a party of Boers who had
murdered some of their comrades under the white flag
“ slim ” trick ; and I believe scalps were taken on several
occasions, but these incidents were very rare.
The statement made by your unseen and sniperlike
correspondent as to the chivalrous character of the Boer
is hardly worthy of remark. The Boer, with many manly
virtues, cannot be called humane or chivalrous. The
atrocities committed by “ up-country ” Australians upon
the defenceless natives have been equalled, if not sur-
passed, by the Boers, which is saying a great deal.
Your correspondent found Tommy Atkins to “ approxi-
mate to the city larrikin”; well, he does incases, no
doubt. Personally, I found him far better than I had
expected. The mounted regiments are much superior in
every respect to the foot. The militia are a disgrace to
any country. The 3rd Middlesex were the only cowards
under fire that I saw during my time in the country, save
a few of Nesbit’s Horse —who were “loyal Boers,” and
whose capture by the enemy meant sudden death at the
hands of those chivalrous gentlemen.
Were I asked the great difference between the British
and the Boer armies I would, say that,. broadly, the
former was a drunken army and the latter a sober one.
No one would believe how many lives and battles were
lost through the alcohol-clouded brains of the British
officers —especially the younger men. I have on several
occasions seen men taken out to disaster and death at
the hands of officers who had deliberately stupefied them-
selves with whisky because their grog-poisoned nerves
would not stand the strain without more hair of the dog
that had already bitten them. One example : When we
were on a march with “light saddles” and men and
horses on the lightest of ghostly rations, one Regular
officer had a Cape cart carrying about a dozen cases of
whisky “to his own cheek.” This cart was so heavily
laden with this and'other “creature comforts” that it
caused delays that nearly brought about the capture of
the whole command (170 men and about 12 Cape carts)
by 650 of the enemy, who, at one point, surrounded us and
caused us a 20 miles running fight in retreating.
In conclusion, the chief source of damage done to these
States through their participation in the war is in the
drunken habits engendered in the younger soldiers at the
base and detail camps—especially in Capetown. lam not
by any means a teetotaller, but I am sure of one thing—
that the efficiency of the British Army and of the Aus-
tralian troops is being undermined by the drunkenness of
their officers; and "there is hardly a doubt that the
authorities in Africa, by their liberal reading of the
canteen regulations at the base camps (drink could be
cheaply obtained all day and night at most), have ruined
the unformed characters and bodies of thousands of young
fellows, both British and Australasian. Colenso and
Magersfontein are not the only examples of the curse and
disgrace alcohol has been to the army, and that, too,
strangely enough, under a Commander-in-Chief who is
supposed to be a temperance man and whose advocacy of
coffee as against beer in his “ pocket-book ” caused much
remark at the time of its first issue.
A new patent, Keytes’ automatic lire-
escape, invented by a Maorilander, has a decidedly
promising appearance: —
The “escape” is a wire rope with an iron ring at the
end of it, the rope being wound around two drums, and
controlled by a ratchet wheel and brake. You fix the
apparatus beside the window, and when your house is
afire each member of the family in turn catches hold of
the ring and descends to the street just as rapidly as the
brake will permit. On letting go of the ring, the rope
rewinds itself at the instigation of a balance-weight and
is ready to the hand of the next customer. Or a light
cage can be attached to the rope, and two or three per-
sons may descend together when the weight-carrying
capacity of the machine has been regulated to suit the
emergency.
Test descents are satisfactorily exhibited by Mr.
Mackesy, who has bought the patent-rights of the
automatic escape, and is taking them to America
and England.
A remark on lawyers by Vic. Justice
Hodges:
The profession of the law had been spoken of in very
high terms, and when that profession was honestly fol-
lowed he did not think those terms too high. To be
engaged in an effort to separate the guilty from the inno-
cent was as noble a calling as any man could be engaged
in. But it could be made a calling in which persons
were engaged in mixing up the innocent and the
guilty, in which a man was engaged in an endeavor
to prevent the due administration of the law.
And Judge Hodges says this just as blandly as if
he didn’t know that every lawyer’s business is to
save.the guilty from punishment and to prevent
the injured from getting justice, provided the
guilty pays his fee and the doer of injustice
approaches him properly through a solicitor and
makes it reasonably certain that the cash will be
forthcoming. .

The Sea Calls.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The waters softly washing
The sea-swept ocean side;
The sweet breeze-sirens singing—
The gold gleam of the tide ;
The spars and funnels swaying;
The house-flags streaming free ;
The ships, the winds, are saying:
“ Come back, come back to sea !”
The big white liners lying
Beside the hot gray piers,
With cool, soft voices bid me •
Re-taste my old sea-years.
The ferry-craft and “trampers ”
Join in one litany—
The whole sea-world is crying :
“ Come back, old man, to me !
“ Come back—leave street and city,
Reave love and land forme;
Come back, come back, my Jason,
O, come back to thy sea !
Reave land and come a-winging
Down trails of cream star-foam ;
Reave land and care and trouble—
Come home, sea son, come home ! ”
All day the sea-call quivers
Rike arrows in my soul;
I hear the cordage singing,
The waiting seas a-troll.
All day and night I hear it,
Above the roaring cars—
A mist of sweet sea-music
Clings round the far-off stars !
At dawn, at dusk I listen;
The sea sings its one song ;■
One rune of peace, of pleasure;
One hymn its whole life long.
I hear it, hear it, hear it,
In streetway and hotel—
My sea-call, softly ringing,
Rike an immortal bell.
I hear it, and I heed it,
The ocean’s endless hymn;
I leave the land-star; vessels
Do wait me, sweet and dim.
The ships are calling, calling ;
The sea sings songs to me ;
Good-bye, good-bye, O cities—
I leave ye for the sea !

The Third Generation.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Well, this is the end of it, mother; I’m not
going on like this any more.”
The old mother burstinto querulous questioning.
“ The life isn’t worth the living,” said the girl,
stormily; “only just making enough to live to
make more, and always falling short of our wants
in everything. What is the good of that ? And
what does it all end in ? ”
“But what else are you to do?” asked the old
woman. “ This is the only way to live—and we
must be content.”
“It’s all very well for you. You have experi-
enced life a little; and you have had us. Having
had children is a sort of happiness. It makes your
life worth living. But what is there for me ? ”
Nervous apprehension of what she knew was
coming, and wanted to stop, kept the mother
silent, and by-and-bye the girl went on.
‘ 1 There’s a man I pass every day in the street- —
Yes,” she burst out fiercely at an interruption,
“I saw a man, and I know what I’m talking
about —and I’ve—l’ve fallen in love with him.”
“Fallen in love with him !”
“ Yes; we used to pass and he seemed to take
no notice of me, and I wanted him to. One day
last week I spoke to him.”
“ You spoke to him ! ”
“I spoke to him! I was dying for him—and
the weakness of starvation doesn’t produce re-
straint. He was kind and tender when I told
him all about myself—which did me good.”
“Girl, what have you been doing ? Don’t you
know that—that if anything happens a man al-
ways takes the opportunity of using that sort of
thing to put it on to the girl’s shoulders ?”
“I know that—and I don’t care. It seems to
me my veins were desperate with longing then.
He says he will do anything for me. Look what
he has given me ! ”
She extended a couple of fine, glittering rings,
at which the old woman looked with a face of
passive misery. But she made no attempt to
excuse or condemn her daughter; her heart,
knowing her sufferings, could not.
“ He says he will give me a great many things.”
“ You don’t think he will marry you ? ” expostu-
lated the mother.
“Of course not; I don’t want him to.”
“ A man expects some return ”
“ I know he does ; and I—it will be happiness
to me as well as him—happiness to be loved and
petted.”
“But you don’t see! —you don’t consider—what
it will bring in the future —what ruin it will mean
to you ! ”
“Do you think I haven’t thought of that! I
know—and I’ll risk it.”
* * • *
The mother sat in the miserable room in which
she lived, ate and slept, with her face in her
hands, long after her daughter had gone. Her
stare was fixed on the golden bounty of the
man to whom her daughter had gone, but no
heroic resolve to cast the money into the mud in
the street rather than touch it filled her —for
heroics are harder in the homes of the wretched
than on the stage. At last she stirred.
“Well, why not?” she muttered —“it will be
her period of enjoyment in this life ; and she will
come back to me trained for her life of everlasting
monotony.”
A man sat looking abstractedly over the top of
his paper at the girl reading a vulgar tale of
impossible dukes and duchesses. He was only a
young man, not much over twenty, and still a crea-
ture of untamed impulse. Just at present he was
sick at the apparent squalor of his life. _ Months
before, when that girl had spoken to him, it had
been an almost chivalrous impulse that made him
take to her. He pitied her sadness, and wanted,
with the sadness that belongs to poets, to cheer
it —and she did not beg for anything but
love. So he had taken her up and given
her everything she desired. At first it had given
him almost sublime happiness. But how every-
thing about her palled upon him—most of all her
intense, glaring want of taste. He absolutely
hated the gaudy way she dressed; her taste, in
pictures, in reading; the untidy splendor of the
furnishing of her room. The mere idea of the
sensuousness to which she had abandoned herself,
that at one time had gratified, now revolted him.
He wished to return to a fresher, cleaner life, and
this was the one who had called him with the clear
voice of an angel.
Suddenly, as she finished her book, which
dropped carelessly on the floor, the girl yawned
unrestrainedly. Then she came over to him
suddenly. “I’m tired of reading,” she said.
“ I’m coming over to sit on your knee.”
He felt a sick sensation sweep through him,
and rose hastily.
“ I’m sorry, Amy, but I was just going to tell
you—l’ve got an engagement that must be kept,
so I’d better be off now.”
And he fled.
• * • •
The mother and daughter again sat in the old,
weather-beaten attic—and a third, the grand-
daughter. It was dressed in a tawdry attempt at
finery. The last of the money Amy’s lover had
left had been spent on it. Amy was bent over it,
a dejected and weary figure covered with the
ashes of burnt-out animation.
“So it’s over,” she said broodingly. “But I
have been really happy, at least for a time. ”
The baby,” interposed mother, softly, “will
now be your happiness. It will make you happy
for the rest of your life.”
“The baby!” exclaimed the daughter, aston-
ished at the soft tone. Then her face darkened.
“ But she must pass through all the misery of the
life I have passed through—and perhaps finish
worse than I shall finish. I sometimes think that
it would be best for her if I killed her before she
knows what trouble is.”
•“ No, no, Amy ! you must cherish the baby, for
its love will save you.”
The girl looked up more surprised than ever.
“Mother, you have said nothing hard tome,
but I thought you would hate my baby. Why are
you so tender about it ? ”
“ Because I have known, too,” said her mother,
with a sudden hoarseness in her voice. “Because
I can feel, too. Because in exactly the way you
have borne this child I bore you.”
The younger mother was standing with the
girl-child in her arms, her face stiffening.
HAMER.
The 29th July, 1902, was the 50th anni-
versary of the day on which the Chusan, the first
P. and O. mailboat seen in Australian waters,,
entered Port Phillip. The Chusan was a screw
steamer of 699 tons. The last P. and O. boat put
on the Australian route was the Mongolia, 10,000
tons.

As a rule, just as soon as a man finds himself in posses
s i on of a glorified dog-kennel, which he calls a store, and

gustingly Respectable, and is found in opposition to the

Of the 3870 foreigner, who have been
naturalised in S. Aus. there are but 13 from the
£.B. to keep Premier Jenkins company; but
there are so many as 2668 other Germans to join
Homburg, late leader of the Opposition. Next to
Germany, Norway and Sweden bulk largest,
though they are only good for 288; after them,
China, with 284. Of Frenchmen there have been
only 42, against Austria’s 117 and little Denmark’s
107. Another curious feature is that of 13
naturalised doctors no less than six have been
Greeks. All this big foreign immigration, how-
ever, is a thing of the far past. S.A. has been
found out.
